Preface
This is a Master’s thesis completed for the MSc Materials and Manufacturing and Industrial Engi-
neering and Management at the Department of Civil and Mechanical Engineering at the Technical
University of Denmark. The project was conducted in collaboration with the furniture company
Stykka ApS.
The author would like to express gratitude to the project supervisors for their expertise and valuable
consultation:
• Daniela C. A. Pigosso, Associate Professor, the Technical University of Denmark
• Giovana Monteiro Gomes, Post Doctoral, The Technical University of Denmark

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This thesis addresses the lack of circular economy integration within the furniture industry, with a
focus on assessing the economic and environmental performance of reverse logistics, in collaboration
with the circular furniture company Stykka ApS. The research employs a design research methodol-
ogy to explore the integration of reverse logistics and understand the barriers and practices prevalent
in the furniture industry. The aim is to develop a support tool that assesses the environmental and
economic performance of reverse logistics systems for furniture products in Europe. This tool is in-
tended to support decision-making for furniture companies engaged in reverse logistics activities,
using economic and environmental performance metrics. A combination of exploratory and sys-
tematic literature review was conducted to identify relevant factors for economic and environmental
performance, alongside an assessment of current circular economy tools for evaluating reverse lo-
gistics. The ﬁndings revealed a gap that informed the development of the support tool, focusing on
helping furniture product designers assess relevant product characteristics for remanufacturing. The
support was co-designed and tested with Stykka ApS, resulting in ﬁve iterations. Despite identiﬁed
limitations and areas for improvement, revealed through tests with two furniture companies, the ﬁ-
nal support tool demonstrated potential to aid designers in identifying hotspots in product design,
which can enable improved remanufacturing performance of cost and energy usage based on product
characteristics.

Page 3
Learning objectives
The following are the learning objectives of this thesis:
1. Describe how reverse logistics can be an enabler for the circular economy within different man-
ufacturing industries
2. Understand and describe barriers to reverse logistics within the European furniture industry
3. Conduct a systematic literature review researching key factors inﬂuencing the environmental
and economic performance of reverse logistics
4. Determine and outline the needs and requirements of a reverse logistics support tool within the
furniture industry through appropriate data collection in collaboration with relevant stakehold-
ers
5. Assess and select relevant factors and measures for economic and environmental performance
to be used in the support development
6. Co-develop a support tool that can enhance the cost and energy usage performance of remanu-
facturing for furniture products with relevant stakeholders
7. Select and use relevant methods to assess chosen economic and environmental performance
measures for relevant factors of reverse logistics
8. Develop and present actionable indications of key areas of improvement in product design that
can reduce cost and energy usage related to remanufacturing based on relevant user inputs
9. Test the usability, usefulness, and reliability of the support tool with relevant stakeholders
within the furniture industry
10. Outline possible limitations of the support tool based on empirical data from intended users
==PAGE:10==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 4
Introduction
The extraction of virgin resources has always been the cornerstone of human societal development.
From 1970 to the present, the human population has increased by 100%, while the extraction of mate-
rials has risen by approximately 233% (European Environment Agency, 2023). This trend is projected
to continue, with material extraction increasing by 85% in 2060 (European Environment Agency,
2023). This growth is driven by the current linear economic models that rely on high-volume vir-
gin extracting materials, transforming them into mass-produced products, and disposing of them as
waste after use. According to UNEP, the production method is expected to drive waste generation
to rise by 81% by 2050 (UNEP, 2024). This issue of large extraction of resources and huge waste
generation creates an unsustainable production trend leading to side effects such as resource scarcity
which can cause price surges, creating large geopolitical risks, driving deforestation, and huge loss of
biodiversity (Forum, 2023). These factors necessitate a signiﬁcant transformation within production
strategies.
Linear consumption driven by the make-dispose model is dominant within the manufacturing sector
(Bjørnbet et al., 2021). As the cost of materials increases the quality of products is slowly falling in an
effort to reduce costs. This shortens the lifetime of products and diminishes the potential for optimal
usage of resources within products (Bjørnbet et al., 2021). To combat this trend, the EU initiated the
Circular Economy Action Plan, which focused on reducing waste by 50% by 2030 and increasing
the adoption of circular practices by doubling the share of recycled materials by 2030 (European
Commission, 2020).
However, the EEA Report estimates that Europe is only 11.5% circular and states that a change within
production strategies is vital to increase circularity (European Environment Agency, 2023). The make-
dispose trend is also seen within the furniture industry, where lower-quality products have become
normalized, resulting in a waste generation of 10.7 million tons within Europe alone (Arditi et al.,
2019). To aid this transition, new lines of thinking enabling a drastic transformation of the furniture
industry must be initiated.
A possible way to change this trend is the circular economy, which proposes a decoupling of resource
consumption from value creation, providing an effective solution to reduce waste by considering
product designs for several life cycles (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2013). By recapturing the value
from resources available within used furniture products, decoupling between the extraction of virgin
materials and value creation is enabled. To enable the increase of a circular economy within the
furniture industry, furniture companies must adopt and incorporate circular activities. One such
activity is reverse logistics, which has been seen as a promising enabler for recapturing value and
reselling products, promoting a circular material loop (Mallick et al., 2023).
“...the effective and efﬁcient management of the series of activities required to retrieve a
product from a customer in order to either dispose it or recover value” Erol et al. (2010)
Against the backdrop of increasing resource consumption and high waste generation, this thesis aims
to investigate how sustainable manufacturing of furniture products can be facilitated through reverse
logistics by assessing its economic and environmental performance, to help companies decide to en-
gage in reverse logistics activities. The thesis initiates this investigation by looking into the following
introductory research questions:
• How can RL be an enabler for the CE within different manufacturing sectors?
• What are the key drivers and barriers for implementing an RL system within the manufacturing
industry?
• How can the economic and environmental performance of RL be measured?
==PAGE:11==
Chapter 1
Methodology
1.1
Design Research Methodology
The structure throughout this thesis follows the Design Research Methodology (DRM) (Blessing and
Chakrabarti, 2009). DRM was developed to assist with the complexity and dynamics of design pro-
cesses, by improving the effectiveness and efﬁciency of the selected design practices. To deﬁne design,
the following description was outlined by Blessing and Chakrabarti (2009):
"Design is a complex, multi-faceted phenomenon, involving: people, a developing prod-
uct, a process involving a multitude of activities and procedures; a wide variety of knowl-
edge, tools and methods; an organisation; as well as a micro-economic and macro-economic
context."Blessing and Chakrabarti (2009)
DRM aims to provide guidelines for the research methodology to steer the selection of relevant ap-
proaches to theories and methods in the design process. DRM enhances reﬂections on chosen meth-
ods, thereby increasing the likelihood of providing relevant design results (Blessing and Chakrabarti,
2009).
DRM consists of the following four stages:
1. Research Clariﬁcation (RC)
2. Descriptive Study I (DSI)
3. Prescriptive Study (PS)
4. Descriptive Study II (DSII)
These four stages are not always followed in a linear sequence; instead, they often involve iterations
between phases. An illustration of the goals and main outcomes of DRM are illustrated in Figure 1.1
Research Clariﬁcation (RC)
In this stage, an exploratory literature review was conducted to provide the basis for formulating a
research plan and research questions for the thesis. To structure this process, the following questions
were deﬁned:
• How can RL be an enabler for the CE within different manufacturing sectors?
• What are the key drivers and barriers for implementing a RL system within the manufacturing
industry?

Descriptive Study I (DSI)
Against the backdrop of the goal and research plan established through RC, the objective of Descrip-
tive Study I is to lay out existing literature to present a description of the ﬁeld based on empirical data
analysis and ﬁndings within the relevant literature. A systematic literature review was conducted to
provide an overview of the research ﬁeld. The aim was to gain a thorough understanding of the
situation and to identify gaps within the current literature. Therefore, the following questions were
deﬁned to support the objectives and goals:
• What are the existing barriers to reverse logistics practices in the European furniture industry?
• What are the critical environmental and economic factors affecting the performance of take-back
and value recovery systems?
• What support tools are currently offered to investigate a product’s economic and environmental
performance in relation to reverse logistics?
• What are the requirements and success criteria for a support tool that can assess the environ-
mental and economic performance of furniture products in relation to remanufacturing?
Prescriptive Study (PS)
Utilizing the understanding drawn from the DSI, PS involves describing the desired situation. A de-
scription of the situation should focus on addressing the gaps identiﬁed in the existing situation that
could lead to improvements. The purpose of this phase was to deﬁne the intended support necessary
to achieve an improved situation and to describe the development and design choices leading to the
ﬁnal support. Then, a presentation of the ﬁnal support is outlined together with an evaluation plan
for the support, which would be applied in DSII.
Descriptive Study II (DSII)
In DSII, the intention was to investigate the support’s ability to achieve an improved situation by
testing and evaluating it on relevant user groups. The following question was used to evaluate the
support:
==PAGE:13==

• How useful and usable is the support in assessing RL in relation to economic and environmental
performance?
1.2
Case Company Description
This thesis was conducted in collaboration with Stykka ApS, a furniture design company that works
to increase the circularity of product designs through remanufacturing (Sparrow, 2022). The collabo-
ration with Stykka ApS was selected based on their valuable insight into the operational and design
dynamics of furniture companies in Europe, coupled with their focus on providing circular furniture
product systems that can compete with the market for economic and environmental performance
compared to linear business models. Stykka has been recognized for its commitment to providing
circular kitchen solutions, focusing on providing reverse logistics business-to-business (B2B) solu-
tions that enable the kitchen components to have several life cycles (Sparrow, 2022). The circular
kitchens are made possible through digitally managed smart systems and modular, durable wooden
components. As Stykka ApS only operates within Europe the scope of the thesis was within the
European furniture industry.
Stykka ApS was also willing to fully engage in a collaborative effort in co-developing a support, by
actively participating throughout the entirety of the DRM process. Therefore, the aim of the collabo-
ration was to utilize the case company in the process by drawing insights from their experience with
reverse logistics within furniture.

Chapter 2
Research Clariﬁcation
This section examines how various manufacturing industries are engaged in circular economy (CE)
through reverse logistics (RL) activities. The aim is to assess how RL acts as an enabler for CE within
manufacturing industries and to identify the barriers to implementing RL. Additionally, this section
explores how the economic and environmental performance of RL can be measured with indicators.
Figure 2.1: Initial Impact Model for Research Clariﬁcation
In Figure 2.1 the initial impact model of the thesis is outlined, showing the focus and assumption,
that knowledge of the economic and environmental performance of RL can support the decision-
making for manufacturing companies to engage in RL activities. The choice to focus on economic
and environmental performance comes from both the relevance to the case company Stykka ApS
and the authors’ interest. Since this thesis was a collaborative effort with Stykka ApS, it will later
focus on the furniture industry only. However, to gain broader insights into how RL enables CE, an
exploratory review across the manufacturing industries was initiated to gain insights into the various
levels of RL integration within the industries.
2.1
Exploratory literature review
Based on the topic of interest, supporting research questions were formulated to determine relevant
areas of contribution and to develop the overall research plan for these contributions:
1. How can RL be an enabler for the CE within different manufacturing industries?
2. What are the key drivers and barriers for implementing an RL system within the manufacturing
industry?
3. How can the economic and environmental performance of RL be measured through indicators?

of search terms related to RL, CE, and the manufacturing industry (Blessing and Chakrabarti, 2009).
The search terms were designed to map the literature related to the application of RL within various
manufacturing industries. Based on these terms, search strings were formulated in Figure 2.2 to guide
the literature search process to answer the research questions.

2.2
RL as an enabler for CE within the Manufacturing Industry
In the climate of growing environmental awareness and resource constraints, the manufacturing in-
dustry is increasingly looking to integrate CE into the current practices (Mishra et al., 2023). The
paradigm shift requires a fundamental change in manufacturing behavior across companies as well
as industries. To achieve the shift, numerous practices will need to be implemented to enable CE
within manufacturing industries (Mishra et al., 2023).
At the core of this paradigm shift is the CE practice of RL, which serves the purpose of redirecting
products from their conventional end-of-life (EOL) phase toward pathways of reutilization or respon-
sible disposal (Mallick et al., 2023). RL covers a wide range of activities, from facilitating customer
returns to the strategic recovery of resources, aiming to increase resource efﬁciency within manufac-
turing ecosystems (Erol et al., 2010). The term is usually used within the umbrella term of circular
supply chain, describing a system that integrates forward and reverse logistics to enable a closed-loop
process (Mallick et al., 2023).
RL consists of many activities but can be grouped into two main areas: take-back and value recovery.
Take-back involves transferring used products from the customers’ possession to the recovery site
(Mallick et al., 2023). Value recovery encompasses activities related to the chosen EOL strategy for a
given product, including inspection, reprocessing, disassembly, and reassembly (Abdessalem et al.,
2012). The value recovery could encompass various options such as reuse, recycling, and remanu-
facturing. Typically, multiple options are implemented for a single product (Abdessalem et al., 2012).
The different activities within RL systems are highlighted in Figure 2.3, where the relation between
circular supply chain (CSC), RL, take-back, and value recovery is illustrated.
==PAGE:17==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 11
Figure 2.3: Reverse logistics activities within the Circular Supply Chain indicating the position of
take-back activities and value recovery options (Abdessalem et al., 2012)
Within the manufacturing industries, take-back systems and value recovery options serve as a key
process to enable CE within industries (Bockholt et al., 2020).
2.2.1
Reverse Logistics within the Manufacturing Industry
As companies realize the importance of integrating RL to enable CE, they often face signiﬁcant chal-
lenges in designing and implementing these systems. There is a variety of reasons for this, often due
to a lack of experience with prior integration of RL systems (Mallick et al., 2023). Within the manufac-
turing industry, the adoption of RL is widely recognized as a way to enhance competitiveness. The
competitive aspect is seen as a risk to the supply chain risks, which can be mitigated by implement-
ing RL. Comprehensive strategies are developed to address the integration of RL in manufacturing,
involving collaboration with suppliers, distributors, and customers (Mathiyazhagan et al., 2020).
The integration of RL is also seen to offer advantages to the manufacturing industries due to its focus
on differentiated market segments, where companies within manufacturing can provide new eco-
nomic value-creation mechanisms (Larsen et al., 2018). To provide this value creation the companies
need to establish a tactical alignment between customers and information mechanisms (Larsen et al.,
2018). Furthermore, product acquisition cost and availability are tied to the possibilities of accessing
the value creation (Larsen, 2017).
An increased pressure provided by environmental regulations, such as EPR within the EU, has en-
abled a rise in the usage of RL within manufacturing (Mishra et al., 2023). Although RL can provide
an increase in products into the supply chain, the reverse supply chain also brings a higher degree
of uncertainty (Larsen et al., 2018). As companies are often not the OEM of all product parts the
activities within the CSC, become increasingly complex (Mishra et al., 2023).
A key step in providing an RL as a business model is laying out the customer education journey. By
focusing on conveying that recovered products still retain the same value as new products (Singhal
et al., 2019).
2.3
CE Within Manufacturing Industries
To gain further insights into RL from different manufacturing industries an uncovering of selected
industries is provided. The industries investigated are Automotive, Electronic, Plastic, and Fast-
Moving Consumer Goods (FMCG). These industries are chosen based on the review by Mishra et al.
(2023) who highlights the industries as having various levels of CE maturity.
==PAGE:18==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 12
Automotive
The automotive industry is on many fronts leading the implementation of CE strategies, with sig-
niﬁcant efforts focused on EOL product recovery. The recovery of automotive parts, especially fer-
rous materials, has for a long period been seen as economically feasible for the automotive industry
(Mishra et al., 2023). The value recovery options of remanufacturing have also gained traction due to
its focus on reclaiming warrants and providing products part quality as new. The remanufacturing of
car components has reached a level of maturity, that can provide part quality resembling new parts,
which has greatly reduced the risk of buying and selling remanufactured parts within the industry
(Wang and Hazen, 2016).
Due to the 2000/53/EU policy mandating recycling and recovery of car components to 95% of the
infrastructure the value recovery companies within the automotive industry have thrived (Mishra
et al., 2023). However, challenges to the industry are still present, including the complexity of CSC
for vehicle parts and supply chain issues related to core availability, and point of origin for product
parts (Mishra et al., 2023).
Electronics
E-waste management is a critical concern within the electronics industry, with efforts focused on
recovering reusable materials by using RL systems. Collection centers for E-waste are critical for
managing waste, and optimizing regional collection networks is needed to improve efﬁciency and
transparency within RL. The shift within the industry from linear to circular has many barriers due
to the fast development of electronics and the lack of re-usage between components in product gen-
erations (Flygansvær et al., 2018).
Plastic
The plastic industry has large potential due to the recyclability of the materials with the possibility of
remolding the plastic into new products. The complexities within plastic products consisting of many
types of plastic materials that are often glued together are still challenging in value recovery (Mishra
et al., 2023). Even with a single-stream plastic material collection, ensuring the quality of material
properties are on par with new has introduced uncertainties for the usage of used materials for new
plastic products (Bendix et al., 2022).
Mechanical recycling is seen as a main enabler of value-recovering post-consumer plastic but has its
main usage within construction and agriculture, where the product quality is lower. However, the
main challenges for RL lie in product complexities, quality assurance, and unpredictable shipment
volumes of plastics. (Mishra et al., 2023).
Fast-Moving Consumer Goods
The FMCG industry, which generates signiﬁcant plastic waste, emphasizes a need for strategies to
promote value recovery strategies. Circular-inspired closed loops, driven by circular design and
business model innovation, offer opportunities for creating value within the FMCG supply chain.
However, high collection costs and complex treatment methods inhibit the establishment of efﬁcient
RL practices for FMCG (Mishra et al., 2023).
Furniture
RL within the furniture industry presents a potential multi-billion-dollar market, with innovations
in the industry focusing on durable bio-based products and remanufacturing activities Mishra et al.
(2023). An example is the extension of lifetime for durable products like ofﬁce furniture through re-
manufacturing, which is shown to offer economic and environmental beneﬁts for companies (Krystoﬁk
et al., 2018). However, challenges in terms of lack of a resilient supply of materials and earlier reman-
ufactured components with high durability and quality assurance are still largely present (Krystoﬁk
==PAGE:19==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 13
et al., 2018). Despite the innovative segments the furniture industry is still primarily dominated by
low-quality products in a linear economy with CE models applied within exclusive product cate-
gories. To change this, a paradigm change across several levels of manufacturing and design opera-
tions has to be addressed (Hartini et al., 2022).
This section shows how RL can be an enabler for CE within the manufacturing industry, illustrating
how RL is integrated differently across various manufacturing industries based on their maturity,
challenges, and the potential to provide value creation through RL.
2.4
Key Barriers and Drivers for RL
The following section aims to present the drivers and barriers associated with RL in the manufactur-
ing industry by answering the following research question:
• What are the key drivers and barriers for implementing a reverse logistics system within the
manufacturing industry?
As RL has been considered an enabler in the transition to CE practices for the manufacturing industry,
the barriers and drivers from the RL perspective are considered to encapsulate barriers and drivers
to the CE (Mallick et al., 2023).
General drivers for Reverse Logistics
The drivers for RL are described as motivation and enablers1. They are not differentiated in this
review as they are all understood as concepts providing incentives or positive effects by reverting to
RL practices.
Mallick et al. (2023) has collected and described different types of drivers for RL established for com-
panies. The different types of drivers are classiﬁed into: Legislation, Economic, Social and Environ-
mental Sustainability, Customer Expectations, and Corporate Image (Mallick et al., 2023).
• The legislative drivers refer especially to the EPR by the EU that ensures that the generator of
waste also are responsible for handling the waste at EOL.
• Economic driver refers to the potential advantages possible from a successful implementation
of RL: the saved cost of virgin materials as well as the new offered value through more customer
interaction.
• Social and environmental sustainability driver refers to the responsibility of the company as a
moral value to take care of the environment it operates in and inﬂuences directly and indirectly.
More related to the internal self-perception of the company.
• The customer expectations driver is the external increasing demands from the consumers for
companies to ensure that their products are sustainable socially and environmentally.
• The corporate image driver describes how the company can use RL in their pursuit of CE to
promote its brand. In this way, manufacturers can utilize RL practices to gain a competitive
advantage, by appealing to sustainable-conscious customers and gain a competitive marketing
advantage.
General context categorization of barriers for RL
The barriers of RL are also referred to as challenges or obstacles2, which have been considered in-
terchangeably, as they all describe negative effects preventing companies from transitioning to RL
1The terms motivation and enablers are used interchangeably with drivers in this review, as they all represent factors that
create an incentive or a positive effect by transitioning to CE practices in companies.
2The terms challenges and obstacles are used interchangeably with barriers in this review, as they all represent factors that
create negative effects preventing companies from transitioning to CE practices.
==PAGE:20==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 14
practices.
Barriers for RL have been well researched and organized into different categories encapsulating them
into generalized areas. A literature review by Ayati et al. (2022) grouping the barriers into relevant
categories will be presented, supplemented by literature contributing with additional aspects of the
barriers within the speciﬁc categories.
• Technology barriers describe current technological limitations regarding enabling the circular
design of products and enabling the required activities in RL, such as sorting, tracking, qual-
ity, recovery, and data integration (Ayati et al., 2022). Additionally, Kirchherr et al. (2018) has
identiﬁed a lack of circular design and a lack of data on impacts.
• Information, knowledge, and skills barriers refer to the lack of reliable information across the
supply chain and know-how on how to adopt and train for recovery approaches inside the
company (Ayati et al., 2022).
• Economic and ﬁnancial barriers are related to the difﬁculties of both implementing and oper-
ating circular activities regarding their high potential cost and required investment (Ayati et al.,
2022). Additionally, access to loans and funding for ﬁnancing is a barrier identiﬁed by Mallick
et al. (2023)
• Market barriers are related to the current market both from the perspective of customers and
brand issues, but also lack of standards and the length of the supply chain. Unpredictable ﬂow
and quality of returned products also pose challenges (Ayati et al., 2022).
• Organization barriers come from organizational structure and hierarchies, where the current
operations, leadership, and strategy are not aligned inside companies and across the value chain
and would have to be so simultaneously to be successful (Ayati et al., 2022). Additionally,
Mallick et al. (2023) also has found the lack of knowledge and experience to be an organizational
barrier.
• Government and regulation barriers describe the lack of policies, standards, government trust
in the quality of recovered products, support, and encouragement in adoption towards circu-
larity (Ayati et al., 2022).
• Society and culture barriers refer to the cultural acceptance, awareness, and price sensitivity
of customers for recovered products (Ayati et al., 2022). Additionally, Mallick et al. (2023) has
found the consumers’ awareness of returning products to enable recovery to be a barrier.
Barriers from different perspectives
Upon reviewing the research of different barriers for RL an abundance of literature reviews collecting
and categorizing barriers into categories were found. However, it was noticed that different literature
reviews had different perspectives on barriers concerning how the barriers were investigated and
their implications.
In the Table 2.4, different perspectives on barriers observed in literature have been listed along with
a description of the identiﬁed barriers.
==PAGE:21==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 15
Figure 2.4: Overview of different barrier perspectives found in the literature. Sources: (1) (Mishra
et al., 2023), (2) (Ayati et al., 2022), (3) (Trevisan et al., 2023), (4) (Mhatre et al., 2023), (5) (Aguiar et al.,
2022)
The different perspectives on barriers for RL stem from each of them focusing on speciﬁc circum-
stances within the research. Some literature reviews have highlighted the size and industry type of
the organization investigated, while others have focused on examining the geographical location of
the company concerning its speciﬁc barrier. This suggests hidden bias in the literature on barriers
for RL but has also given a preliminary insight into which aspects of an organization and its prod-
ucts inﬂuence the types of barriers most prevalent. Additionally, barriers are incredibly complex and
situational, making it difﬁcult to apply general barriers directly, as they vary based on contextual
tendencies.
The review by Mallick et al. (2023) showed how drivers within RL are focused on legislation, eco-
nomic, social, and environmental sustainability, customer expectations, and corporate image.
The barriers to RL are viewed as more complex. Although there are several reviews categorizing the
main barriers, it has also been found that barriers are highly contextual. The key barriers are therefore
highly dependable on the speciﬁc context from which perspective barriers are considered.
==PAGE:22==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 16
Some general barriers have been identiﬁed, but they are not necessarily applicable or have the same
importance across all manufacturing industries, products, and organizations.
2.5
Measuring the economic and environmental performance of RL
To enable RL within manufacturing, the performance of RL has to be measured to provide indications
of the feasibility of the RL systems integration. Based on the project’s focus on the economic and
environmental performance of RL, the relevant measurement areas for RL will be reviewed through
the following:
• How can the economic and environmental performance of RL be measured through indica-
tors?
Indicators to measure RL performance
There are various goals for companies to engage in RL activities. The grounds on which to engage
in RL are often based on the RL system’s ability to improve the economic and environmental perfor-
mance of products (Ravichandran et al., 2024). It is therefore important to understand how companies
can measure the performance of these areas to achieve set goals. Mallick et al. (2023) has conducted
a review of different Key Performance Indicators (KPIs) providing insight into how performance can
be understood and measured for different goals. The ﬁndings of Mallick et al. (2023) on economic
and environmental KPIs are summarized in Figure 2.5.
Figure 2.5: Environmental and economic KPIs found by Mallick et al. (2023)
Other than economic and environmental indicators Mallick et al. (2023) has also covered RL KPIs
related to the following aspects:
• Social indicators relate to the customers and employees in the organization, such as customer
satisfaction and excessive working hours
• Operational indicators relate to measuring the performance of the RL operations by considering
the return rate of products, packaging recycling, recovery efﬁciency, etc.
==PAGE:23==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 17
• Management indicators measure the support and commitment by management for RL activi-
ties.
These categories of KPIs are not in the scope of the thesis, as the focus is on economic and environ-
mental factors, but are highlighted to show the m the many possibilities and complexities existing in
measuring the performance of RL.
Selection of KPIs
In a workbook developed by Kravchenko et al. (2020b) the process of selecting KPIs for measuring
CE initiatives is elaborated and split into three steps:
1. The scope is deﬁned to help carve out relevant indicators for KPI selection.
2. The indicators are then selected, making them the KPIs for the given scope. It is highlighted that
prioritization is required for selection and guiding questions are formulated, which can help
determine the most suitable indicators. Finally, the customization of KPIs and the inclusion of
new ones is conducted.
3. The indicators are applied, which constitutes the collection of the relevant data and using the
data with the selected KPIs to benchmark different alternatives to support decision-making
Along with the workbook, an activity tool has been developed, supporting the selection of relevant
KPIs. A part of the activity tool is a database consisting of over 270 indicators related to economic,
environmental, and social performance (Kravchenko et al., 2020a).
Measuring the economic and environmental performance of RL can only be done by determining the
goal and scope of the RL initiative. There are several ways to measure economic and environmental
performance, which is shown by the variety of KPIs identiﬁed by Mallick et al. (2023). The process
of selecting KPIs based on goal and scope is described by Kravchenko et al. (2020b) in three different
steps, helping companies select KPIs related to their speciﬁc goals for RL.
2.6
Conclusion of Research Clariﬁcation
Based on the exploratory literature review of RL and CE across various manufacturing industries
revealed the roles, practices, drivers, and barriers associated with RL as a facilitating practice for CE.
RL is found to be an enabler in transforming linear production models into CSC model systems by
utilizing take-back and value recovery of products at their EOL stages. These activities are seen to
have varying maturity across different manufacturing industries. Industries such as the automotive
is seen as leading the RL integration whilst furniture and plastics have implemented RL, but are seen
as less mature and developed (Mishra et al., 2023). The maturity of RL within the industry is found
to be due to a multitude of factors such as product design, supply chains, legislation, and market.
Exploring the drivers for RL includes legislation, economic beneﬁts, social and environmental sus-
tainability, customer expectations, and corporate image (Mallick et al., 2023). Various barriers have
also been identiﬁed, ranging from technological limitations, lack of information and skills, ﬁnancial
constraints, market challenges, organizational challenges, regulation, and cultural acceptance pro-
vide a hindering the widespread adoption of RL and CE practices within manufacturing (Ayati et al.,
2022). The barriers have been identiﬁed as being highly contextual and vary signiﬁcantly across in-
dustries, regions, and company types, stressing the importance of developing RL solutions catered to
speciﬁc industries (Mishra et al., 2023).
Additionally, companies engaging in RL need to specify the goals of their systems. While economic
and environmental performance are key measurement points for RL, they can be assessed using var-
ious indicators. By establishing a speciﬁc goal for a CE initiative and pairing it with the KPIs most
suitable for measuring economic and environmental performance companies can reach their set goals
(Kravchenko et al., 2020b).
==PAGE:24==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 18
2.6.1
Industry in focus
The ﬁndings from the RC indicate varying levels of maturity in RL, with barriers differing in relevance
across industries, geographic locations, and other contextual factors. By reviewing various manufac-
turing industries an understanding has been obtained of their drivers and contextual barriers, which
highlight different potential goals to overcome those barriers, and how the selected indicators align-
ment to these goals is important.
To provide a goal of RL relevant to the case company selected, this thesis scope focuses on the Euro-
pean furniture industry as outlined in Section 1.2. This focus aims to identify barriers, factors, and
KPIs speciﬁc to RL systems within the furniture industry in Europe.
2.7
Overall research plan
The exploratory literature review has provided the foundation to conclude the research clariﬁcation,
which has resulted in an overall research plan for the project. The research plan includes the research
focus, deliverables, goal, and research questions.
Research focus and goal
The focus on the furniture industry has been based on the industry of the case company. The lack of
RL maturity found through cross-industrial analysis highlights potential gaps in the furniture indus-
try. The industry has signiﬁcant potential to reduce environmental impact and product costs through
RL practices. With several enablers being introduced within the EU, the European furniture market
is seen as an industry with potential expanding RL integration. The thesis will seek to co-develop a
support assessing the environmental and economic performance of RL, in collaboration with Stykka
ApS by using their insights as a source of empirical data. This will include exploring various rele-
vant factors for the furniture industry that can be key in assessing the economic and environmental
performance of RL. Based on this, the overall thesis goal is:
"Develop a support that assesses the factors inﬂuencing the environmental and economic
performance of a reverse logistic system for furniture products within Europe"
Research Questions
The exploratory literature review used to answer the questions in the Research Clariﬁcation has high-
lighted different areas in need of further investigation. This section has highlighted the potential of
the furniture industry; however, there is a need to further understand the industry concerning the CE
before developing a support with Stykka ApS.
To create a support tool to assess the performance of RL, it is necessary to uncover the factors inﬂu-
encing the performance. To do this a systematic literature review has been conducted to outline the
relevant factors. To develop a support tool relevant to the industry it has to be integrated with, it is
also necessary to investigate which types of support already exist in the literature to understand the
current gaps.
To investigate the need of developing a support for the furniture industry the case company is uti-
lized. This is done to gain insights from a representative of the industry for which the support will
be developed. With them, the selection of KPIs, along with the requirements for usability and useful-
ness of the tool, will be determined. To understand how useful and usable the support tool is without
introducing developer bias, the support will be tested with two external furniture companies.
To support the process of these activities, the following research questions have been formulated:
• RQ1: What are the existing barriers to reverse logistics practices in the European furniture
industry?

take-back and value recovery systems?

environmental performance in relation to reverse logistics?
• RQ3: What are the requirements and success criteria for a support tool that can assess the en-
vironmental and economic performance of furniture products in relation to reverse logistics?
• RQ4: How useful and usable is the support in assessing RL in relation to economic and
environmental performance?

Descriptive Study I
This section aims to investigate the barriers to RL speciﬁc to the European furniture industry. It will
assess the critical environmental and economic factors inﬂuencing the performance RL and identify
the relevant success criteria needed to develop support tools for the European furniture.

take-back and value recovery systems?
– SRQ 2.1: What support tools are currently offered to investigate a product’s economic
and environmental performance in relation to reverse logistics?
• RQ3: What are the requirements and success criteria for a support tool that can investigate
the environmental and economic performance of furniture products in relation to reverse
logistics?
3.1
CE within the European Furniture Industry
The furniture industry is characterized by its large contribution to both the economy and employment
within Europe, as the European market constitutes 28% of the global market share in furniture and
sustains one million jobs (Dagilien˙e et al., 2023). The industry primarily consists of SMEs, with an an-
nual market value of 84 billion EUR and a waste generation of 10.78 million tons (Silvius et al., 2021).
Despite the industry’s realization of the relevance of CE principles, studies show that engagement in
CE activities is still minimal (Barbaritano et al., 2019).
A review by Silvius et al. (2021) found that recent studies have begun to address CE in the furniture
industry, focusing on the transition of strategies towards CE models and their connection to sus-
tainable development goals. The key topics include optimizing the furniture supply chain through
component standardization and design strategies to enable create closed-loop systems. Other re-
search highlights the best practices at industrial and institutional levels and examines how policy
measures and regulatory frameworks can further enable CE practices within the furniture industry
(Silvius et al., 2021).
Maintaining high-quality materials is crucial for extending furniture life-cycles through value recov-
ery options, even though enabling value recovery is currently seen as less favorable compared to
incineration, or landﬁll (Silvius et al., 2021). Accurate environmental impact assessments are vital
to undercover the potential of using value recovery options, this includes whether to value recovery
products or purchase new items. Additionally, aesthetic value and quality contribute signiﬁcantly
to the prolonging of product lifespans, and enhancing material and energy efﬁciency (Silvius et al.,
2021). This is one of the main reasons why high-quality furniture products are currently more utilized
within RL practices, and regular products are still largely not utilized (Ecores et al., 2017).
3.2
Barriers to CE in the European Furniture Industry
This section examines the barriers within the European furniture industry to understand what in-
hibits the implementation of CE practices within the industry. By identifying relevant barriers, this
section aims to gain insights into the industry’s dynamics and the challenges that need to be overcome
to enable wider adoption of RL.
Barriers in literature
The European Environment Bureau identiﬁed several barriers hindering the transition of the furni-
ture industry from a linear to a CE (Ecores et al., 2017).
Concerning materials and design, the furniture industry within Europe has moved away from solid
wood and metal furniture to cheaper materials, restricting the potential for products to be value
recovered (Ecores et al., 2017). The barriers related to products are the durability level and the lack
of design for disassembly, repair, reuse, remanufacture, and recycling. Additionally, the Chemicals
==PAGE:28==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 22
(REACH) regulation presents challenges due to the ban on hazardous substances, which adds costs
in recycling processes and complicates the management of chemicals in products (Ecores et al., 2017).
Furthermore, consumers are lacking guidance on how to maintain and repair furniture. Furthermore,
the lack of availability of spare parts for furniture products further discourages the effort of repair and
encourages the purchase of new furniture. The price difference between new and second-hand furni-
ture is not signiﬁcant enough to drive sustainable purchasing behaviors yet (Ecores et al., 2017). This
barrier is also set to be due to the low awareness of the availability and beneﬁts of sustainable fur-
niture options in the B2B context. Additionally, end markets for value-recovered furniture materials
are underdeveloped and, in some cases, already saturated, restricting further investment in material
recovery facilities of furniture products (Ecores et al. (2017)).
Closing the loop of furniture products poses its own set of challenges. There is a limited collection
and RL infrastructure and a general under-investment in the collection and logistics for furniture
take-back. The producer responsibility mechanism is not widely applied in the furniture industry, as
many countries provide off-take for these waste streams, which hinders the development of larger
take-back initiatives (Ecores et al., 2017). Europe also generally has high transport and labor costs
making repair and refurbishment less economically feasible (Ecores et al., 2017).
The exploratory study by Silvius et al. (2021) of 30 EU furniture companies with EU aims to under-
stand barriers. The study revealed four distinct patterns of perception regarding CE barriers seen in
Figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Barrier comparison between Ecores et al. (2017) and Silvius et al. (2021) to the furniture
industry in relation CE activities
==PAGE:29==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 23
Figure 3.2 compares the barriers found between the two studies. The study from Ecores et al. (2017)
has a top-down perspective on the barriers where whereas Silvius et al. (2021) provides a bottom-up
perspective on the barriers of the industry. Ecores et al. (2017) points to materials, consumer behavior,
logistics, and lack of infrastructure, as the overarching barriers whereas Silvius et al. (2021) points to
the business challenges faced by SMEs, such as resource constraints, investment barriers, market
competition, and difﬁculties in adopting new technologies and practices.
Barriers with Case Company
Jarl Vindnæs, CEO of Stykka, outlined key barriers for the furniture industry in Europe regarding CE
practices from the perspective of a SME.
First, the CEO highlighted the lack of information resources available to conduct decision-making
for furniture companies concerning integrating of CE practices. This includes insufﬁcient data points
at the product level to measure for feasibility of RL, but also a lack of supply chain insights. Addi-
tionally, furniture companies struggle with a lack of supply chain overview, both for forward and
backward logistics, due to the complexity of sourcing components from various OEMs and the un-
certainty of receiving used products.
The CEO also highlighted the uncertainties tied to the quality of remanufactured products and how
these often are seen as subpar to new products by B2B customers. Furthermore, the high cost of
durable materials poses a signiﬁcant barrier, resulting in lower proﬁt margins compared to furniture
companies with lower-quality materials. The importance of marketing CE practices to provide value
creation is currently difﬁcult for furniture companies due to a lack of certiﬁcations and regulations.
The barriers to implementing the CE within the European furniture industry are multifaceted. Top-
down perspectives call for further investments to enhance the possibilities of RL, while bottom-up
perspectives focus on how resource constraints and market conditions hinder feasible RL integration.
Lastly, the CEO of Stykka highlighted the main barriers related to the lack of information available to
understand if RL is a feasible business model for furniture products, furthermore, he highlighted the
high costs of materials and the lack of strategic marketing for RL of furniture products.
3.3
Systematic Literature Review of Economic and Environmental
Factors
This systematic literature review explores the literature regarding take-back programs and value
recovery strategies, focusing on which key economic and environmental factors are inﬂuencing the
performance of these RL activities. Through the literature used in the review is following question is
raised:
• What are the critical environmental and economic factors affecting the performance of take-
back and value recovery systems?
As described in Section 2.5 there are many ways that economic and environmental performance can
be measured, depending on the speciﬁc indicators one wants to measure. In this literature review, no
speciﬁc measure for environmental and economic performance has been chosen yet, as it is desired
to get a broad understanding of various factors described in the literature, which might focus on dif-
ferent environmental and economic measures. Instead, the goal is to understand the factors affecting
the indicators to gain an overview of the most relevant indicators to utilize.
A search was carried out to collect literature to answer the research question. It includes a combi-
nation of different blocks of similar terminology, to ensure different use terms and use of deﬁnitions
in the literature was encompassed. The search string along with the resulting articles can be seen in
Figure 3.3. The systematic literature with an overview of the ﬁnal 41 articles and their themes can be
found in Appendix B.
==PAGE:30==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 24
Figure 3.3: Search string used and process of narrowing down the relevant articles
The goal of the literature review was to identify critical economic and environmental factors affecting
the performance of RL. Getting an overview of and categorizing economic and environmental factors
and their relation to RL is necessary to select the appropriate factors to be used in the support tool. As
the support seeks to assess the environmental and economic aspects of RL the critical factors relevant
must be outlined. First factors related to take-back will be presented, followed by a presentation of
the value recovery factors.
3.3.1
Take-back factors
This section of the literature review focuses on the identiﬁed literature dealing with the take-back of
products and the associated factors affecting the economic and environmental performance of taking
back products from the user at EOL and returning them to the manufacturer. Literature that has
found or developed environmental factors is described brieﬂy along with their identiﬁed factors.
Environmental factors
Saidani et al. (2019) has created a model that frames the circularity performance, comprising 3 differ-
ent dimensions, economic proﬁt, environmental savings, and the EOL regulations along with given
determined CE objectives. Together these constitute the overall circularity performance of recycling,
remanufacturing, and reuse at both product, material, and part level. Saidani et al. (2019) uses CO2
equivalent emissions as the sole indicator for environmental performance and has through literature
identiﬁed this to be the primary means of assessing environmental savings. An important metric de-
ﬁned is the transport required from the customer or collection center to the manufacturing company.
The transportation during take-back is also described by Zhang et al. (2020), who through a review of
the manufacturing industry has considered remanufacturing activities and also included take-back
of the products. Here transportation’s impacts are described by considering the effects of fuel con-
sumption, exhaust emissions, and noise pollution during the transportation of products for remanu-
facturing (Zhang et al., 2020).
The environmental factors have through the literature review been found to primarily relate to vari-
ous types of environmental impact of transportation. The economic factors have a more granulated
==PAGE:31==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 25
approach to exactly what determines the transportation distance and efﬁciency.
Economic factors
Bockholt et al. (2020) deﬁnes ﬁnancial performance as the result of resource effectiveness and resource
efﬁciency, which are general categories encapsulating all economic factors allocated by Bockholt et al.
(2020). Here resource effectiveness refers to how much of the product value can be recovered through
take-back, either material or functional value. This is closely related to value recovery, which will
be covered in the next section. Resource efﬁciency is about using current resources to the best ex-
tent possible by minimizing the cost of logistics and handling for recovering value. Here Bockholt
et al. (2020) has found the business model, legislation, and consolidated capabilities to impact the
logistics costs of product take-back. Here consolidated capabilities refer to the possibility of grouping
shipments into bigger volumes during take-back or using 3rd party collectors. The framework for
resource efﬁciency and effectiveness by Bockholt et al. (2020) can be seen in Figure 3.4
Figure 3.4: Framework for factors affecting the ﬁnancial performance of take-back. Adapted from
Bockholt et al. (2020)
The literature review by Larsen et al. (2018) looked into creating a factor network map showcasing
different factors and sub-factors and how they are related to the economic performance of RL. This
is done through a literature review exploring different factors identiﬁed, with both some in-house
manufacturing of components and also buying materials and ﬁnished components from suppliers.
The study focuses on manufacturers that sell to the end market and do sales of spare parts as an
aftermarket activity (Larsen et al., 2018).
The total ﬁnancial contribution to the company from RL is made up of four variables, which are:
added beneﬁts, variable process cost, revenue from the resale, and acquisition cost. Larsen et al. (2018)
has deﬁned take-back as acquisition. The network map for each variable contributes either positively
==PAGE:32==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 26
or negatively to the overall reverse supply chain, which can be seen adapted in Figure 3.5(Larsen
et al., 2018).
Figure 3.5: Factor network map showing the product acquisition cost and how it is inﬂuenced by
other factors factors. Only the factor network for acquisition cost is shown. Adapted from Larsen
et al. (2018)
Each variable has an associated factor network map that showcases different factors that directly or
indirectly have a positive or negative inﬂuence on the given variable, which is at the center of the
map.
Concerning ﬁnancial factors, Uhrenholt et al. (2022) has investigated the ﬁnancial performance of
product take-back. In the article, three different layers of factors are identiﬁed: Macro, meso, and
micro factors, each relating to different levels of inﬂuence the company has over the factors. The
macro factors are the context within which the company has to operate, such as the legislation and
other geographical determinants. The meso factors relate to the supply chain of the company, where
the company has some degree of inﬂuence, but not full control. Finally, the micro factors relate to
company-speciﬁc matters, where there is more or less full inﬂuence and control, such as product
design and internal processes (Uhrenholt et al., 2022).
Uhrenholt et al. (2022) argues that even though the macro and meso factors are outside the control
of the companies, they need to be considered internally to optimize the choices at the micro level.
This aids the company to operate within the given context and supply chain. It is proposed that a
product take-back system is designed outside in, meaning the internal factors and design choices for
a take-back system should be adapted to the external context and supply chain. Additionally, it is
proposed that a ’cost and value trade-off’ is made in the design of product take-back. High-value
capturing of take-back poses higher operational cost while low-value capturing, such as material
value capturing, poses lower operational cost, which suggests that an optimum exists for how much
cost it is worthwhile to put into a take-back system concerning the value possible to be recovered.
That is products with low functional value have a lower probability of being worthwhile making a
take-back system to recover the functional value rather than the material value.
From the macro, meso, and micro factors identiﬁed by Uhrenholt et al. (2022) here are some related
to activities related to take-back:
Macro
• Legislation can have a negative effect through increased documentation of EOL of products
that have to cross national borders, but it can also have a positive effect if it supports the estab-
lishment of municipal-driven collection systems.
==PAGE:33==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 27
Meso
• Partnerships and collaboration which concerns the use of 3rd part waste collectors to exploit
economies of scale and general collaboration in the supply chain.
• Reverse logistics which relates to factors concerning the frequency, dispersion, and volume of
product shipments. Additionally, early sorting can inﬂuence the cost and the the proximity of
company and collection point
Micro
• Customer awareness of product take-back can ease the collection of products
3.3.2
Value recovery
This section of the systematic literature review assesses the value recovery strategies across vari-
ous manufacturing industries, focusing on relevant economic and environmental factors inﬂuencing
these strategies in the existing literature.
Value recovery strategies in manufacturing are primarily considered within waste management and
the CE, focusing on minimizing waste and maximizing resource utilization. The literature often orga-
nizes these strategies into models such as the 3Rs, but has also been expanded to the 9Rs framework
by Vegter et al. (2020), presented in Figure 3.6. The many different ways of approaching value recov-
ery highlight that it is not a straightforward process, as each strategy has a different focus, outcomes,
and processes for achieving those.
Figure 3.6: The 9R value recovery framework identiﬁed by Vegter et al. (2020)
Environmental factors
In Gupta et al. (2021)’s investigation into the relationship between Industry 4.0, environmental im-
pact, and value recovery, the study outlines a framework to assess the sustainability performance of
manufacturing companies. The ﬁndings underscore the signiﬁcance of ’Supply Chain Traceability’,
’Reuse and Recycling Infrastructure’, and ’Natural and Clean Environment’ as relevant factors. The
study advocates for the adoption of 4.0 practices within value recovery methods to enhance perfor-
mance within these areas (Gupta et al., 2021).
==PAGE:34==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 28
By assessing the environmental impact of value recovery strategies, Deng et al. (2015) 12 key factors
for evaluating the ecological performance of remanufacturing activities, which encompasses both the
economic and environmental aspects. The ﬁndings from environmental factors were based on prior
studies on relevant factors related to remanufacturing, which was split into external and internal
factors inﬂuencing ecological performance. The environmental factors were based on the resource
efﬁciency level of the manufacturing facility which was linked to internal aspects such as pollution
control, remanufacturing equipment, key technologies, and recycling systems. For external factors,
the focus is on design for remanufacturing, policy guidance, and standards within the industry (Deng
et al., 2015).
Struck and Flamme (2023) proposed the development of an independent evaluation system for as-
sessing the resource efﬁciency of manufacturing, focusing on recyclability and reusability as essential
metrics. In the article, a set of eight criteria is outlined. These factors include the degree of disas-
sembly and the amount of harmful content in materials. The system also looks into the reusability
potential and separability potential of materials, the effectiveness and spread of recycling methods
available within the context industry, and the quality of the recycled material concerning the over-
all recycling rate. These factors highlighted in the article provided to understand the interrelations
between product design, recovery systems, and attention to the end quality and usability of the re-
covered product (Struck and Flamme, 2023).
In a study by Mesa et al. (2020) a set of six sustainability indicators for product families are pro-
posed to optimise for circular paths through value recovery. Within the model, environmental factors
related to product family structure such as modularity, assembly-disassembly efﬁciency, reconﬁgu-
ration feasibility, and accurate measurement of potential are seen as key. They are meant to support
the development of product aspects such as durability, upgradability, and adaptability within the
product families. By enabling these product factors entire product families become better adapted to
value recovery thereby decreasing environmental impact (Mesa et al., 2020).
In the investigation of Circulation Factory (CF) systems, Rickert et al. (2021) highlights several key
product factors essential for improving the environmental performance of remanufacturing and recy-
cling activities in CF systems. The CF is a theoretical manufacturing system aimed at optimizing the
value recovery of products. Through the analysis, the main factors identiﬁed impacting environmen-
tal performance are handle-ability, complexity of products, materials used, durability, backward and
forward compatibility, diversity of product variants, and demand cycle of products. These factors are
both focused on product-speciﬁc factors but also market-focused such as the demand cycle. In the
article the focus in optimizing incoming products before entering the facility, as well as managing the
variations and differences in product portfolios (Rickert et al., 2021).
Hapuwatte et al. (2022) looked into how aligning products across generations can impact the environ-
mental performance of value recovery. The article aims to tackle the challenges posed by the rapid
new product launches, which contain different materials and structures that require new value re-
covery strategies. The article proposes a method that forecasts and optimizes Product Sustainability
Performance (PSP) through component commonality, seen illustrated in Figure 3.7. The method is
based on factors such as product complexity across generations and similarities between materials,
components, and ﬁttings (Hapuwatte et al., 2022).
==PAGE:35==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 29
Figure 3.7: Visualization of design for improved environmental performance across product genera-
tions (Hapuwatte et al., 2022)
Economic factors
Grabot et al. (2014) has provided a focus on the importance of developing a business decision support
to assess economic implications in various value recovery strategies, utilizing an automotive engine
as a case study. The study focuses on evaluating economic costs associated with the remanufacturing
activities of engines. The main economic factors evaluated were the treatment process, recycling
process, avoided manufacturing, and avoided raw materials extraction. Additionally, a calculation of
the beneﬁts and revenues associated with each option was evaluated, including the selling of value-
recovered parts (Grabot et al., 2014).
In the article by Bradley et al. (2018) the Total Life Cycle Cost Model was used to uncover economic
and environmental impacts throughout a product’s entire life cycle. The model’s structure encom-
passes two main actors: the manufacturer and the customer, emphasizing that the economic impact
is interrelated between the two. At the top level is the life cycle cost of a product which is further
detailed in subsequent tiers of the model. One aspect is the manufacturer cost which incorporates
factors such as material reuse and recoverability, while the customer cost accounts for areas such as
acquisition incentives and logistical expenses. The model further breaks down manufacturer costs
into speciﬁc equations related to value recovery methods, including material costs, post-recovery
stream allocations, usage costs, and damage repair costs of products. These equations contain vari-
ous factors, including product requirements for value recovery, program costs for product recovery,
and potential repairs of damaged parts (Bradley et al., 2018).
The article by Kwak and Kim (2017) points out that a driver for reducing costs within remanufac-
turing is the planning for dis- and reassembly, amount and variety of spare parts, and production
quantity and types of remanufactured products, seen illustrated in Figure 3.8. These factors are seen
as internal factors possible to be controlled by the OEMs including factors such as the number of
types of products remanufactured and the number of steps needed to re- and dissemble the products.
==PAGE:36==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 30
Figure 3.8: Cost model for value recovery based on insight from Kwak and Kim (2017)
Kwak and Kim (2017) also highlights external factors that affect buyback and selling prices. Here it
is highlighted that even though prices signiﬁcantly impact the proﬁtability of value recovery opera-
tions, they are often controlled by the market dynamics.
In a structured literature review Uhrenholt et al. (2022) investigates the ﬁnancial performance of
product take-back systems, where various factors were explored affecting the economic viability of
value recovery strategies, categorized into three dimensions of factors: context (macro), supply chain
(meso), and company (micro). Here factors impacting the value recovery methods were found to
be wear and tear, as products have different levels of wear through usage, which affects the value
recovery effort and approach (Uhrenholt et al., 2022).
The material composition is also seen as having importance, as mixed materials require higher ﬁl-
tering and separation precision in value recovery. Lastly, the lack of product information on the
recovered products is seen as a cost factor, as detailed product and material information enables a
better possibility of increasing quality value recovery (Uhrenholt et al., 2022).
Duberg et al. (2020) looks into OEMs regarding factors to consider before engaging in re-manufacturing
activities, perceived from an economic perspective. In total four overarching main factors were iden-
tiﬁed along with supporting factors in determining whether engaging in re-manufacturing activities
makes sense for the given manufacturing company. Here ﬁve main areas are perceived to drive cost
in value recovery through sourcing, labor skill and availability, facilities, process, and product design,
seen illustrated in 3.9. Here the focus is on how factors such as the complexity of the value chain, and
product complexity are affecting remanufacturing differently depending on several of these factors
simultaneously. Duberg et al. (2020) points to enablers such as ’Design for remanufacturing and infor-
mation feedback’, ’Remanufacturing process improvements’, ’Remanufacturing market knowledge’,
and ’Organization, planning and control’, to reduce costs of value recovery.
==PAGE:37==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 31
Figure 3.9: Framework showing how interrelations between value recovery facilities and competen-
cies affect economic factors in value recovery. Adapted from Duberg et al. (2020).
Bockholt et al. (2020) highlights the difference between the material and functional value, as strongly
inﬂuencing the possible selling price of value-recovered products. The material value is the non-
functional value of the materials the product consists of if taken apart. The functional value is the
value of the product design that delivers a certain functionality. To assess this functional value, Bock-
holt et al. (2020) points to the obsolescence of the product, as the reason why the product loses value
to the customer. Sierra-Fontalvo et al. (2023) has collected and described six different types of obso-
lescence to examine the reasons for obsolescing:
• Technological obsolescence refers to when products become outdated due to technological
developments, which makes the product incompatible with developing new technology and
outdated.
• Functional obsolescence happens when requirements for the functionality a product deliv-
ers change, rendering the current product unable to deliver functionality corresponding to the
changed requirements.
• Psychological obsolescence happens when a product’s life is shorter than its potential due to
aesthetic and psychological reasons, such as changing trends or a desire for something new.
• Economic obsolescence happens when it no longer makes economic sense to continue using a
product, which can be due to the operating cost it or the cost of maintaining its functional value
through repairs and maintenance. Replacing the product is more economically feasible.
• Diminishing manufacturing sources and material shortages happen when the market and
supply chain no longer, or only limited, supports and produces material, technology, or parts
corresponding to the product.
• Planned obsolescence occurs when the manufacturer deliberately designs the product to be
worn out after a certain use time or usage cycles, which can both be a software integration or
planned part failure, among other means.
3.3.3
Conclusion of Systematic Literature Review
In this literature review, various approaches and frameworks for modeling and describing the eco-
nomic and environmental performance of take-back and value recovery have been described.
==PAGE:38==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 32
Many of the economic and environmental factors have been seen to be used in literature both having
an economic or environmental perspective, such as the factor regarding transport distance, which
both can be considered an economic or environmental factor, as both have a cost associated with the
distance and capacity usage, while several environmental performance aspects can also be considered
such as pollution, noise, emissions, etc.
From the literature, it can be considered there are both economic and environmental aspects of the
same factors inﬂuencing the performance of RL. Again, the choice of performance measure is impor-
tant, as the literature investigated has covered different types of factors and performance measures.
It is seen that to investigate the relationship between factors and performance, it is necessary to select
speciﬁc factors and performance to investigate.
Different methods have been uncovered to describe factors inﬂuencing the performance of RL. Bock-
holt et al. (2020) described factors as either inﬂuencing the efﬁciency or effectiveness of take-back,
while Larsen et al. (2018) has considered factors as contributing to acquisition cost, process cost, rev-
enue, and added beneﬁts. Another method of categorizing key factors has been bone by Uhrenholt
et al. (2022), who grouped factors into micro, meso, and macro levels depending on the level of in-
ﬂuence the company has over the factors. The literature review unveils how the focus on different
inﬂuence levels affects which factors are relevant to consider concerning the performance of RL. The
review also showed how take-back factors are mostly related to transport whereas value recovery
factors are highly related to aspects of product design and production facilities.
Through the systematic literature review, it can be seen that take-back factors are primarily assessed at
the meso level, focusing on dispersion and collaboration in the supply chain which the company only
has limited inﬂuence over. Value recovery is observed across all levels. At the micro level, product
design and operations are outlined. Meso factors focus on the capabilities in the supply chain, product
demand cycle, and standards, while macro factors are related to aspects such as legislation and the
market.
To gain an overview of the different types of factors identiﬁed, the level of inﬂuence classiﬁcation
used by Uhrenholt et al. (2022) is exploited to outline key factors inﬂuencing the economic or envi-
ronmental performance of RL summarized in Figure 3.10.
Figure 3.10: Factor overview based on the conclusion of SLR for economic and environmental factors
inﬂuencing RL
==PAGE:39==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 33
Figure 3.11: The goal set in relation to relevant factors and indicators
The systematic literature review has provided insights into the economic and environmental factors
impacting the performance of RL but has also shown how these can be viewed from different levels
between the micro, meso, and macro perspectives. The review indicates that there is a large array of
perspectives on factors determining RL performance. To provide a support, a focus has to be selected
on the level and category of factors, which are to be used in assessing the economic and environmental
performance of RL.
3.4
Scoping of research focus
In answering Research Question 3 2.4 and 4 2.5 in Research clariﬁcation 2 it was found that the bar-
riers for RL are numerous and contextual while the economic and environmental performance of RL
depended on the choice of KPIs. Additionally, it has been found that even when speciﬁc KPIs have
been selected, numerous factors at the macro, meso, and micro levels affect the KPI performance.
To develop a useful support it was decided to focus on speciﬁc factors inﬂuencing the performance of
RL. A scoping of the support was therefore conducted in collaboration with the case company. This
was done to establish the goal for the support, which was to assess the environmental and economic
performance of manufacturing through micro-level factors. As Stykka has a goal of designing fur-
niture products optimized for remanufacturing, illustrated in Figure 3.11. The case company saw it
essential to couple furniture product designs to economic and environmental performance to gain
decision-making capabilities for RL. The barrier of product design was also outlined in the barrier
section for European furniture, underlining that it is not a problem area perceived by Stykka only.
The focus of the thesis can be seen in Figure3.12.
