3.5
Review of Micro CE Indicators
As the scope of the thesis was narrowed to the relation between furniture product design and reman-
ufacturing performance, this section will focus on uncovering which micro-level circular indicators
(CI) are currently offered in terms of environmental and economic performance. CI is a group of
support tools used to monitor and assess the status and progress of CE initiatives across various im-
plementation levels. Saidani et al. (2017) deﬁnes CI as “the circularity of products, components, and
materials, included in three wider systemic levels, all along the value chain and throughout their
entire lifecycle.
• What support tools are currently offered to investigate a product’s economic and environ-
mental performance in relation to reverse logistics?
The literature review by Lindgreen et al. (2020) looks into the different types of micro CI tools avail-
able highlighting that there is a variety of different approaches to micro tools, both looking at com-
pany structure, products, value chain, etc. The review stresses that the term micro CE indicators
covers a broad array of indicators that encapsulate many aspects of CE aspects of companies.
As the deﬁnition of micro tools is quite broad and the current focus of the thesis is on the prod-
uct design of furniture the project will focus on the nano-level indicators, which speciﬁcally assess
individual products and design choices, separating their impact from overall company circularity
de Oliveira et al. (2020). The different levels of CE indicators are illustrated in Figure 3.13.
==PAGE:41==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 35
Figure 3.13: Illustration of the different levels of CE indicators deﬁned by de Oliveira et al. (2020)
Through a review of micro and nano indicators de Oliveira et al. (2020) focused on environmental and
economic performance. Here it was stated that environmental indicators focused on outlining the use
of materials, components, energy, and water. Many circularity indicators, such as REAPro, MESCS,
CEI, and CEV, primarily focus on material recirculation and resource efﬁciency but fail to capture
the complexity and trade-offs of circularity concerning energy and other resources. de Oliveira et al.
(2020) found the economic indicators, often paired with environmental factors, to addresses resource
ﬂows in terms of monetary value. It was also discovered that combining economic indicators with
environmental provides a more coherent evaluation of the value recovery process (de Oliveira et al.,
2020).
To understand how nano indicators evaluate product design in relation to remanufacturing perfor-
mance, three supports from the automobile industry were selected due to the high maturity level of
the usage of remanufacturing in the industry.
The support by van Loon and Wassenhove (2018) focuses on evaluating the economic and environ-
mental impacts of remanufacturing in the automotive industry, illustrated in Figure 3.14. It looks
into return ﬂows, product design, and remanufacturing operations to assess their combined effects,
identifying critical cost components and variables.
The tool focuses on inputs from companies, but has chosen to exclude costs relating to establish-
ing a remanufacturing system, and instead focused on operational costs. The model is based on the
company’s ability to know how the volume of products eligible for remanufacturing, to provide an
assessment(van Loon and Wassenhove, 2018).
A nano indicator for automobile systems was developed by James et al. (2021) to enhance EOL per-
formance by evaluating the ease of remanufacturing based on the technical, environmental, and eco-
nomic feasibility, through the method Analytical Hierarchy Process (AHP) seen in Figure 3.14. The
tool focuses on identifying design attributes that inﬂuence remanufacturability. The attributes were
found through a review of relevant designs for remanufacturing. To scale the 5 attributes with sub-
attributes, the values were created ranging from low to high impact on economic, technical, and en-
vironmental impact. This methodology, applied during the design stage, can aid automobile system
designers evaluate and enhancing the remanufacturability of their products.
Chakraborty et al. (2017) also illustrated in Figure 3.14 developed a micro tool that identiﬁes and
==PAGE:42==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 36
presents the product and design characteristics in a diesel engine suitable for remanufacturing. The
tool prioritizes the design attributes and selects the most suitable product alternatives for remanufac-
turing. The selection was created concerning relevant product design characteristics of diesel engines,
and was done using the Fuzzy AHP methodology. Chakraborty et al. (2017) then worked in coop-
eration with an automobile engine remanufacturing company to properly rate characteristics with
experts. The characteristics were then scored concerning the different stages within remanufacturing
to offer impact scoring of the different design characteristics. This model then provided a way to
compare different product designs for remanufacturing (Chakraborty et al., 2017).
Figure 3.14: Illustration of three micro CI tools focused on remanufacturing based on economic and
environmental facts (Chakraborty et al., 2017) (James et al., 2021) (van Loon and Wassenhove, 2018).
In a micro tool review by Lindgreen et al. (2020) it is highlighted that current CIs lack focus on a
speciﬁc level, and are often not industry-speciﬁc, which limits their ability to identify risks. Also, a
lack of development with the end-user is seen, omitting potential insights the end user can provide
to the design process (Lindgreen et al., 2020).
In the review from Saidani and Kim (2021) on the CI to improve product design for circularity also
found the following gaps: Relating circularity CI to industrial practices and connecting circularity
with proﬁtability and environmental impacts. Key actions include further experimenting with CI
and tools with industry practitioners, linking product circularity performance with company practice,
integrating CI with design tools, and combining complementary methods to measure sustainability
impacts (Saidani and Kim, 2021).
3.6
Requirements for support
To outline relevant requirements for the support, the following research question had been formu-
lated:
• What are the requirements and success criteria for a support tool that can assess the environ-
mental and economic performance of furniture products in relation to remanufacturing?
==PAGE:43==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 37
To generate requirements for the support that would align with the needs of the furniture industry in
Europe, a workshop was established to co-create and prioritize requirements with relevant stakehold-
ers from the case company. The goal of the workshop was to identify and prioritize input and output
requirements essential for providing usable support for furniture design. The process, structure, and
outcomes of the workshop can be seen in Appendix C.
The workshop setup included a list of pre-made requirements based on the exploration of the fur-
niture industry within Europe and interviews previously conducted with stakeholders from Stykka.
The purpose of the workshop was to determine the requirements the case company found most rele-
vant for developing the support.
To ensure Stykka’s participation, the COO and the Head of Circular were invited to participate in the
workshop. The workshop was then structured by categorizing the requirements from the pre-ﬁlled
list into "Nice to Have" or "Need to Have" and then prioritizing them within these sections in low to
high relevance, illustrated in Figure 3.15. Furthermore, the tool requirements were split into output
and input requirements to ensure Stykka’s reﬂection on both aspects.
The workshop was set up as a discussion between different stakeholders of Stykka to gain insights
into how requirements could be observed differently from the different perspectives of the furniture
company.
Figure 3.15: Structure of requirement workshop, split into input and output requirements using pre-
ﬁlled requirement long-list
Based on the ﬁndings from the workshop with Stykka, key requirements were identiﬁed. To ﬁnd
the ﬁnal requirements the workshop was held up against the needs and barriers identiﬁed from in-
vestigating the European furniture industry and CI literature review. This led to the following ﬁnal
requirements illustrated in Figure 3.16. The success criteria were later deﬁned based on the require-
ments, which can be found in Section 4.9.
==PAGE:44==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 38
Figure 3.16: The ﬁnal requirement for Support. Separated into requirements for the inputs and out-
puts of the support
3.6.1
Selection of KPIs for support
Based on insights from reviewing various indicators used for measuring the economic and environ-
mental performance of RL, it became evident that speciﬁc indicators had to be selected as KPIs for
economic and environmental performance to be assessed by the tool.
The KPI overview for RL created by Mallick et al. (2023) was reviewed to determine which would
ﬁt the support, ensuring alignment with the needs identiﬁed at Stykka. In dialogue with Stykka, the
relevant KPI’s were presented, to select relevant measures. During this selection process, the KPIs
suggested by Mallick et al. (2023) were presented to the COO and Head of Circular.
In the selection process, the COO and Head of Circular stressed the importance of relating the mea-
sures to operational aspects of the remanufacturing activities. The operational aspects were seen as
the data that could be acquired directly from the operation of the remanufacturing processes. Another
aspect was to use indicators utilised by Stykka for measuring their own remanufacturing activities.
This led to outlining energy and cost as the main measures.
Some of the indicators related to the energy consumption of manufacturing processes in the database
developed by Kravchenko et al. (2020a) highlight that the energy indicator should be used together
with an indicator for material consumption as well.
To challenge the case company, the material consumption indicator was presented as an additional
environmental indicator. The COO expressed here, how Stykka inlays the material consumption of
new material entering the remanufacturing process within their energy indicators, to encapsulate
both indicators. This is done as most companies look primarily to cost and energy to gain an under-
standing of performance. To align with these aspects the project chose to scope to energy and cost as
indicators.
The KPI’s selected from the workshop were:
Economic KPI: Cost of remanufacturing activities within RL. Considering cost as the operational
expenses within remanufacturing: labor hours, machine hours, kWh hours consumed, storage, tests,
==PAGE:45==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 39
and spare parts which are deﬁned as activity-based costing by Abdessalem et al. (2012) who proposed
this method for computing the total cost of activities for value recovery.
Environmental KPI: Total energy usage was chosen as the measurement. This considered the direct
operational energy consumed and used in the various remanufacturing activities, as well as indirect
energy coming from producing additional parts or materials consumed (Li et al., 2013).
3.7
Selection of Product Characteristics
To develop the support it was necessary to select the product characteristics, which would be used
as the basis for the support. The process described by Kravchenko et al. (2020b) was used to select
relevant characteristics. The process was originally developed to help KPI selection but is adapted to
be used for the selection process of product characteristics to be used in assessing the cost and energy
usage within the support.
Step one of the process was to establish the initial set of product characteristics from which the most
suitable and ﬁnal design characteristics are selected.
Many product factors have already been identiﬁed in the literature reviews, but to structure the pro-
cess for establishing the initial set of characteristics, it was decided to use the characteristics identiﬁed
by James et al. (2021), who has created a support based on product characteristics to evaluate the re-
manufacturability of automobile systems, illustrated in Figure 3.14. Despite the characteristics being
used for a different product type, it is assumed that many characteristics are still relevant for furni-
ture products. Additionally, the indicator database developed by Kravchenko et al. (2020a) was used.
More speciﬁcally, the category of indicators in the database named ’Product architecture’ was used,
as they ﬁt the product design focus.
The next step was the selection of the relevant environmental and economic indicators, which have al-
ready been selected to be the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing, as described in Section 3.6.1.
The third step was selecting the suitable characteristics. Here Kravchenko et al. (2020b) highlights
various guiding questions to help select the right characteristics. Among these guidelines are: the
amount of data required, uncertainty of data collection, resource requirements for obtaining charac-
teristic data, and ease of characteristic use and understanding (Kravchenko et al., 2020b). Based on
these questions the product characteristics were selected.
The fourth step involves the customization of selected characteristics and the creation of additional
characteristics, to compensate for any potential aspects not covered by the selected indicators from
step 3. Going through this process resulted in the ﬁnal selection of 21 different product characteris-
tics, which were used to represent furniture product design for remanufacturing (Kravchenko et al.,
2020b).
The selection process described by Kravchenko et al. (2020b) is depicted in Figure 3.17 and can be
found described in greater detail with the initial and ﬁnal characteristics set along with the selection
process in Appendix D.
==PAGE:46==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 40
Figure 3.17: Outlined selection process deﬁned by Kravchenko et al. (2020b) for the 21 ﬁnal product
characteristics.
3.8
Discussion DSI
Limitations of narrowing the scope in the impact model
After selecting the scope of product design characteristics in relation to remanufacturing activities in
furniture, the focus has been narrowed to speciﬁc factors of the impact model. This signiﬁcantly limits
the possibility of providing a holistic view of the cost and energy usage of introducing RL. Other fac-
tors related to take-back activities are not considered, and many other relevant value recovery options
have been omitted. This scope limits the project’s ability to address all the different barriers, KPIs,
and factors relevant to RL. However, it allows for greater depth and detail in the support eventually
developed for the narrowed scope of product design and cost and energy usage in remanufacturing
for furniture. The scope also enables co-development of the support with the case company by gain-
ing relevant empirical data on the selected focus within the industry. The importance of collaborating
with actors in the industry engaged in the activities, which a CI is developed to assess, is outlined as
key to providing a usable support by Lindgreen et al. (2020) and Saidani and Kim (2021).
The narrowed scope and its impact on making companies engage in RL activities is shown in Fig-
ure 3.18.
Limitations of Empirical Data
The case company has been included in the initial design aspects of the support, namely in the scop-
ing to focus on factors related to product design, ﬁnal requirements, and choice of environmental and
economic performance indicators: cost and energy usage of remanufacturing.
The availability of a case company engaged in the development of a support tool can provide a unique
opportunity to connect literature and empirical data to scope and develop a useful and usable support
tool for the industry. The choice of collaborating closely with a case company also limits the general
applicability of the support as it is scoped to ﬁt the needs of one company.
Namely, the choice of KPIs for the support, being cost and energy, are inﬂuenced by the current
practice of the case company. This practice draws insights on current indicator evaluation within
furniture, but also limits the environmental aspects considered, such as material consumption.
The inclusion of multiple furniture companies early in the process would have been preferable to
ensure a more comprehensive understanding of furniture manufacturers’ needs and requirements to
be reﬂected in the scope of the support tool.
However, the ﬁnal scope is also supported by the literature discovered through the exploratory and
systematic literature review, which can help ensure the applicability of the ﬁnal scope to the general
furniture industry. Additionally, the usability and usefulness of the developed support will also be
==PAGE:47==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 41
Figure 3.18: Impact model showing the limited impact the tool will have on the decision-making for
reverse logistics for manufacturing companies
==PAGE:48==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 42
tested in the DSII with other furniture companies, which will help to investigate the general applica-
bility.
3.9
Conclusion of DSI
This chapter has systematically examined the CE within Europe’s furniture industry, focusing on the
primary barriers. Furthermore, a systematic literature review of economic and environmental factors
inﬂuencing RL performance and CI tools provides key insights to providing the scoping and focus of
this thesis.
The review of barriers to CE practices within the furniture revealed that while there is a growing
interest in CE within the furniture industry, signiﬁcant barriers remain. These barriers include the
high costs of durable materials and the complexity of RL infrastructure.
The systematic literature review identiﬁed critical economic and environmental factors affecting the
performance of take-back and value recovery systems. The relevance of these factors depends on
the area and level assessed within RL. The factors are summarized into three levels: macro, meso,
and micro. At the macro level, factors include legislation and infrastructure. At the meso level, they
involve supply chain collaboration and capabilities. At the micro level, factors encompass product
design and operations.
To address the ﬁndings from the systematic literature review, the research scope was narrowed to
focus on micro factors related to furniture products with the scope of economic and environmental
performance of remanufacturing. This decision was driven by the need to provide actionable insights
and support Stykka, who emphasizes the importance of product design in their RL practices. A de-
tailed review of CI also highlighted a gap within CI literature focused on product design for furniture
products in remanufacturing.
A workshop with relevant stakeholders from Stykka further reﬁned the requirements for the support,
prioritizing ease of use, applicability across furniture types, and detailed product characteristics. The
ﬁnal requirements emphasized the need for comprehensive insights, actionable recommendations,
and user-friendly results. Additionally, the economic and environmental performance indicators
were chosen to be the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing. Lastly, a four-step process was
followed to select the 21 product design characteristics, which will form the base of the support tool
development.
3.9.1
Problem statement and selection of focus
After laying out the gaps the support would have to ﬁll, the focus area for the support, must sub-
sequently be deﬁned. This was based on the ﬁndings of the systematic literature review, but also
heavily inﬂuenced by the requirements and needs set by Stykka.
To deﬁne the relevant problem area for the furniture industry and RL, a problem statement has been
created:
“Develop a furniture-focused support tool that can analyze product design characteristics to pro-
vide performance parameters on energy and cost when remanufactured, to enhance decision-
making capabilities in product design change”
In the research clariﬁcation, it was found that RL is an enabler of the CE and holds the potential to
reduce environmental and economic impacts related to furniture production. To address the issues
identiﬁed throughout DSI and to push forward to the future state shown in Figure 3.19, it is necessary
to ﬁll the gaps identiﬁed in the literature and case studies with the company. These gaps may prevent
the integration of a support tool in furniture product design, which must be addressed to reach the
future state.

Chapter 4
Prescriptive Study
In this chapter, a support is developed to address the gaps identiﬁed in the furniture industry. The
process followed product development principles, by using ﬁve iterations illustrated in4.2, with the
development outputs from each iteration presented. The iterations were based on insights gathered
from Stykka throughout the design process. The iterations within PS are therefore generally struc-
tured as such; 1. Developing support iterations, 2. gaining feedback from Stykka, and 3. gaining new
insights to develop the next iteration. This process is illustrated in 4.1.
Figure 4.1: The development process of PS iterations is divided into three steps
The chapter begins by clarifying the intended support, followed by the development process, and
ends with a presentation of the ﬁnal support tool. The presentation outlines the intended use and
implementation plan. Finally, an evaluation plan was created to test the support was relevant users
in DSII.

==PAGE:51==

4.1
Method of Prescriptive Study
The design process in the prescriptive study was conducted with several iterations allowing the
project to go back and assess the development based on feedback gathered. The iterative approach
was used to provide an agile approach and to allow for co-creation through the use of participatory
design methodology (Halskov and Hansen, 2014). The participatory design approach was used, as it
positions the designer as a facilitator of the collaborative efforts and negotiations among stakeholders
(Pedersen and Clausen, 2019). The choice of method allowed for co-creation with relevant stakehold-
ers from Stykka, who has expert knowledge within the ﬁeld of design for the remanufacturing of
furniture.
The participatory design emphasizes the direct involvement and inﬂuence of end-users and a broad
array of relevant actors across the value chain in the design process (Buur and Matthews, 2008). The
method helped the project to understand the needs of several users within the furniture value chain.
Stykka has therefore been used throughout the design process and their involvement is outlined in
Figure 4.3. The choice of method was also in line with the development method recommended for
CI, highlighted by Lindgreen et al. (2020), stressing the importance of developing with end-users.
==PAGE:52==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 46
Figure 4.3: Actors from Stykka mapped throughout the development process
While participatory design is highly collaborative, it also has a political nature within design pro-
cesses. Different concerns and perspectives are negotiated, making design a process of change that
requires facilitation and orchestration (Halskov and Hansen, 2014). To mediate these negotiations
throughout the design process the usage of intermediary objects was applied to share needs and
ideas between the designers and users, illustrated in Figure 4.4. The use of developed the concepts
and prototypes therefore become instrumental as intermediary objects to gain insights and feedback
from stakeholders within Stykka.
Figure 4.4: Illustration of the development process through the use of Participatory Design
The design activities used were mainly workshops and semi-structured interviews with Stykka. These
were facilitated by the designers where stakeholders got involved in the development of the support
in order to gain ownership of the support development.
4.2
Clariﬁcation of intended Support
As the selection of product characteristics concerning remanufacturing had been conducted, it was
relevant to outline where in the design process the tool would become valid and which breakdowns
the support could focus on.
Through DSI it was shown that the industry is dominated by low-quality products, and complex
supply chains that all hinder furniture companies from engaging further in RL. Addressing all these
==PAGE:53==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 47
areas is relevant when developing a support tool for the furniture, but given time limitations it is
necessary to establish a focus. To understand the barriers more integrally, a workshop was created
with the COO and CEO of Stykka to understand factors that can hinder design for remanufacturing.
To investigate the breakdowns of product design change for remanufacturing in the furniture indus-
try, the workshop was centered around the intermediary object of a ﬂow diagram. The object was
used to outline the different breakdowns during the product design process. The workshop can be
found described in greater detail in Appendix E. Through the workshop, it became evident that the
most relevant areas were found in the conceptualizing phase of product design. The barrier pre-
venting design changes is the designers’ ability to understand which product characteristics impact
remanufacturing of furniture products. This is crucial as this limits the designer’s action on these
product characteristics and in designing differently.
Figure 4.5: Breakdowns in the furniture design process related to design for future life cycles
Both the Head of Design and COO also highlighted the importance of understanding and measuring
relevant product characteristics early in the design phase, as this is where the largest possibility for
design change is found. This is also outlined by Aguiar et al. (2022) who states that early product
design is crucial for increasing product value recovery potential. The COO also stressed the impor-
tance of prioritizing the improvement of such characteristics. Time is also a critical factor in furniture
design, as this is usually allocated to only focus on the development of products and not explorations
of new design strategies. Furthermore, furniture product characteristics are often focused on making
the product manufacturable (Mishra et al., 2023). Insights into product characteristics that would im-
prove the remanufacturing of products would allow for a more focused product development, as it
could provide KPIs for product designers.
4.3
First Iteration - Concept development
After identifying areas of interest within the design process with the current gaps, the concept de-
velopment of the support tool was initiated. This development process was structured into ﬁve key
phases.
==PAGE:54==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 48
Firstly, in the conceptualization phase, two different concepts were created based on the requirements
deﬁned in collaboration with Stykka, both aimed at addressing the identiﬁed gaps and user needs.
Following a concept evaluation involved presenting these concepts to Stykka for their insights.
Next, a requirements and criteria rating was conducted, where both concepts were rated based on
their ability to fulﬁll the established requirements and resource constraints.
Finally, in the concept selection phase, the concept best aligned to the requirements was chosen.
4.3.1
Conceptualization
The conceptualization phase of the product development was primarily centered around determining
the content of the output of the support tool.
In other micro factor support tools, as reviewed in Section 3.5, it was found the support tools primarily
provide a relative output in the form of an index, such as the support developed by James et al. (2021),
which provides an index on product design in its suitability to be remanufactured.
Another perceived option for outputs is to provide absolute measures, such as kWh and DKK, rather
than relative output. Giving a prediction of the potential cost or cost savings and energy usage or
energy savings by remanufacturing compared to virgin production. This led to the two concepts seen
in ﬁgure 4.6.
An assumption with these two concepts was that signiﬁcantly more time would be required to collect
data for the absolute output concept, which would limit the possibility of also focusing on developing
actionable recommendations for improvement.
Figure 4.6: The two concepts developed: The Absolute and Relative Support
==PAGE:55==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 49
4.3.2
Concept Evaluation with Stykka
The two different concepts were presented to the CEO and the Head of Circular in the format depicted
in Figure 4.6. This was done to get their evaluation on the most useful support concept, to guide the
ﬁnal selection of the concept to develop.
When evaluating the concepts, the CEO ﬁrst noted that is it important to understand which product
characteristics are relevant and why. Concerning the two concepts he mentioned that the relative
index would be less useful than an absolute output, but that he would be more cautious and critical
of an absolute scoring. Another important aspect was identifying the relevant characteristics of the
design to help design teams focus on improving these areas. While the relative support was seen
as less useful in terms of the cost and energy index outputs, the focus on recommendations and
improvement was considered relevant.
The Head of Circular highlighted the usefulness of the absolute support in terms of the output being
concrete data and therefore easier to relate to support data-driven decision-making. He also acknowl-
edged the signiﬁcant challenges related to data quality required to make such a model reliable. It was
highlighted that a relative index would be preferable if absolute results are uncertain, due to the risk
of making decisions based on inaccurate data information.
With his experience in providing EPDs and LCAs for Stykka, the head of Circular was aware of the
difﬁculties in offering insights on the absolute impact of remanufacturing based on product charac-
teristics.
Both the CEO and Head of Circular saw potential in both concepts and expressed interest in develop-
ing them further. They appreciated the idea of obtaining absolute results but criticized the viability of
connecting design characteristics to absolute cost and energy usage ﬁgures. They also noted issues re-
lated to the relative model, stating that it could become challenging for a design team to integrate and
comprehend the output of the model. They both stressed the importance of selecting and prioritizing
relevant characteristics to improve the quality of design changes.
4.3.3
Requirements and Criteria Rating and Selection
As Stykka had expressed interest in both concepts each with its drawbacks and beneﬁts. Stykka did
not deﬁne any preference between the two concepts. It was therefore up to the designers to prioritize
which concept to develop further.
To facilitate a selection between the two concepts a set of criteria and associated weights were out-
lined, as seen in Figure 4.7. Each criterion was assigned a weight according to its perceived impor-
tance, and both tools were rated on a scale from 1 to 5 for each. The rating was based on the concepts
of reliability, usefulness, time, data availability, and competency match of the designers.
These aspects were seen as crucial due to their importance highlighted by the participants from
Stykka. Reliability was essential for the integration of the support system. Data availability was
relevant since only certain data could be obtained within the project’s time frame. Finally, the compe-
tency of the designers was based on their backgrounds and their ability to create the support system.
The criteria, scoring, and weights were not conducted with Stykka, but only by the designers, which
poses bias in the ﬁnal selection.
==PAGE:56==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 50
Figure 4.7: Requirement and Criteria rating of the two support concepts
Estimated Reliability: The relative evaluation tool scored 4, while the absolute evaluation tool scored
2. This difference was because the required reliability level was perceived to be signiﬁcantly bigger
for the absolute support, whereas support with a relative output was not seen as having to live up to
as strict reliability performance, as the focus would rather be on recommendations.
Usefulness: The relative evaluation tool scored 3, compared to the absolute evaluation tool’s perfect
score of 5. The absolute tool is expected to have a higher potential usefulness in its absolute output
rather than a relative index.
Time Intensity: The relative evaluation tool was scored 5, representing its perceived feasibility to
develop, given the time frame of the thesis. In contrast, the absolute evaluation tool was scored 2, as
it was expected that a substantial amount of time was required to gather and treat the data necessary
for giving an absolute output.
Data Availability: The absolute concepts were evaluated to only score 2, due to the problems with
gathering enough data of high enough quality. Problems not deemed to be of equal importance for a
relative concept which was scored 4.
Competency Match: Both the relative and absolute evaluation tools scored 4, which reﬂects the de-
veloper’s own perceived capabilities to develop both support tools.
Final Scoring and Selection
Based on the result of the selection process, the relative support was seen as the preferred support
tool concept considering the set criteria. Despite the absolute tool’s high usefulness, its lower scores
in reliability, data availability, and time intensity made it less feasible to develop.
4.4
Second Iteration - MVP Development
The second iteration focused on creating a MVP of the support to gain feedback from Stykka. By
having developed an MVP the possibility of testing the tool with Stykka and giving them a full walk-
through of the user experience was enabled. To create the MVP, the structure of the support had to
be created.
The content the MVP development consisted of:
1. Explanation and selection of assessment method for the support
2. Development of the FAHP hierarchy
3. Weighing of Characteristics and Remanufacturing Activities
==PAGE:57==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 51
4. MVP development - Integration of FAHP model into a support tool
5. Feedback on MVP from Stykka
4.4.1
Explanation and selection of assessment method - Fuzzy AHP
To create a connection between the chosen characteristics and the total cost and energy usage, the
Analytical Hierarchy Process method (AHP) was chosen.
AHP is traditionally a decision-making method, that utilizes a set of different criteria to evaluate
among a range of alternatives (Saaty and Vargas, 2012b). An example of usage could be the decision
between different suppliers in a tender process, where AHP can be utilized to establish a hierarchy
of criteria by establishing an overall problem, supplier selection, split into different criteria such as
cost, delivery time, and quality. Each of these criteria might then again have different weights in the
overall evaluation and themselves be considered as sub-problems, consisting of other sub-criteria.
Such as cost, which might consist of sub-criteria such as the possibility of credit, total cost, currency
risk, etc. (Ishizaka, 2014).
After establishing a hierarchy, weights need to be assigned to different levels. It must be determined
how important cost, quality, and delivery time are compared to each other. Additionally, the sub-
criteria must be assessed to determine the weight of ’possibility of credit,’ ’total cost,’ and ’currency
risk’ in evaluating the sub-problem of ’cost’ (Ishizaka, 2014).
The weights are calculated using relevant experts to make evaluations that can be used to determine
the weights of the criteria and sub-criteria. The evaluations from experts can then be converted into
weights, which then enables a quantiﬁcation of the evaluation between alternatives. In this way, AHP
mimics the evaluation of the experts through a structured process (Ishizaka, 2014).
To gather the expert evaluation and enable quantiﬁcation of evaluations, a variation of the AHP was
used, which is called FAHP, which is a combination of the AHP and Fuzzy logic (Ishizaka, 2014).
FAHP has been argued to be beneﬁcial over AHP, since AHP forces the experts to express themselves
in numeric values, and does not take into account the subjectivity and uncertainty, that might exist
between different experts in the understanding of different levels of importance of various criteria.
Using numeric evaluations omits taking into account this uncertainty and varying perceptions of the
experts. Therefore, fuzzy logic can be used in connection with AHP, allowing experts to evaluate
the different product processes and characteristics with linguistic terms, rather than assigning them
direct numeric values (Ishizaka, 2014).
Unlike statistical literature, the number of samples collected, conﬁdence levels, probability distribu-
tions, etc. is not relevant in the AHP method. Rather, the AHP method entails selecting the appro-
priate experts, whose evaluations are then included in the model. The selection of the appropriate
experts depends on two factors: The consistency in the experts’ evaluation and their validity within
the practice. Validity refers to the experts having appropriate experience, education, and how well
their knowledge on the subject. Consistency entails how the experts provide their evaluation, mean-
ing that the different criteria should consistently be rated the same when compared to each other.
This is done through the transitivity principle, where if criteria A is evaluated higher than criteria B
and criteria B is evaluated higher than criteria C, then criteria A should also be evaluated higher than
criteria C. This means that the experts exert the same weight on a linguistic term across the evaluation
(Saaty and Özdemir, 2014).
AHP can also have other uses than choosing among alternatives. James et al. (2021) used AHP
to make a remanufacturability index tool for automobile systems based on product characteristics,
which helps designers quantify suitability for remanufacturing and the effect of changing product
design. This was done by the creation of 5 main characteristics groups: Handling, Accessibility,
Separability, Recoverability, and Re-assembly characteristics, which were used to create the remanu-
==PAGE:58==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 52
facturability index. Here for each characteristics group James et al. (2021) has then identiﬁed several
sub-criteria.
Chakraborty et al. (2017) has made a similar FAHP model based on the remanufacturing steps as
the criteria and product characteristics as the sub-criteria. The model aimed to identify the most
suitable design alternative for remanufacturing. Where cost, time, quality, and environmental impact
were problems being evaluated, not only making an overall index of remanufacturability but also
considering the remanufacturability concerning different parameters (Chakraborty et al., 2017). This
is the same aim desired to be achieved for the support tool of this thesis, just focusing on cost and
energy usage as KPIs.
Inspiration from the usage of the AHP methodology by James et al. (2021) and FAHP in Chakraborty
et al. (2017) was taken and adapted into a model that ﬁts the speciﬁc goal of this thesis with the
support tool, to help a structure of the hierarchy, that could create an index for remanufacturing cost
and energy usage based on product characteristics.
Establishing the AHP hierarchy - determining the criteria
The overall criteria used in the AHP model were set as the remanufacturing activities. All the cost
and energy usage in the remanufacturing activity should able to be allocated to the selected activities.
To deﬁne the remanufacturing activities, the deﬁnition used by (Bras, 2007), was used, which are
disassembly, inspection, cleaning, restoring and replacing, reassembly, and quality check.
The 21 product characteristics identiﬁed in Section 3.7, were then used as the sub-criteria in the AHP
structure to link the remanufacturing activities and product characteristics. To understand how the
21 product characteristics would be related to the remanufacturing activity they had to be paired up
against the activity. The initial hierarchical structure can be seen in Figure 4.8.
Figure 4.8: The structure of the AHP hierarchy for the support tool. Consisting of: the two goals
that are determining the Cost and Energy usage. The criteria are the remanufacturing activities. The
sub-criteria are the 21 product characteristics.
==PAGE:59==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 53
4.4.2
Weighing of Characteristics and Remanufacturing Activities
Having outlined the hierarchical structure of the AHP, the next step was to determine the weights of
both the manufacturing activities and the weight of the product characteristics. To create the weights,
then data had to be collected from the experts, which was done through surveys.
The use of pairwise comparison is more precise than direct rating, making it the preferred choice of
method for rating criteria (Ishizaka, 2014). This would entail evaluating each characteristic against
the other characteristics, allowing for consistency to be calculated in the evaluation of the expert. This
can help ensure that the expert’s true opinion and expertise are reﬂected in the ﬁnal weights that are
being calculated. This evaluation can then be redone until the expert is consistent in their answers
(Saaty and Vargas, 2012a). See Section 5.1.4 for further explanation and calculation.
Pairwise comparison requires a large number of evaluations to be created. The six criteria would
require 15 evaluations for cost and energy usage respectively for a total of 30 evaluations. However,
if all 21 characteristics have to be pairwise compared to determine their weight in determining the
cost or energy for one remanufacturing activity, it would result in 210 evaluations. If this was done
for all characteristics and each activity for both cost and energy usage, it would require a total of 2520
evaluations.
To bring down the number of evaluations necessary, it is decided to only use pairwise comparison for
evaluating the remanufacturing activities and to use direct rating on product characteristics, which
means only a single evaluation is required for each sub-criteria, bringing down the number of total
evaluations required to 282.
This was still deemed to be too many evaluations to realistically ask an expert to do, which is why
some of the remanufacturing activities were grouped. Disassembly was combined with Inspection
and Reassembly with Quality Check. This reduces the number of criteria from six to four and the
total required evaluations to 180.
To reduce this number even further, it was decided to create an initial model of which product charac-
teristics should be considered for each remanufacturing activity. This was done as it was anticipated
that some characteristics were relevant to more activities than others.
To provide a check on the initial model on which speciﬁc characteristics are relevant to individual ac-
tivities a series of three workshops with the Head of Circular, the Head of Design, and the Fulﬁlment
& Logistics manager was created. This is described in further detail in Appendix F. This resulted
in a determination of which product characteristics are deemed relevant in inﬂuencing either cost or
energy usage, which can be seen in Figure 4.9.
With some characteristics being pre-excluded from being considered relevant in some of the remanu-
facturing activities, the total amount of evaluations required was brought down even further to 138.
This was still deemed a high number of evaluations to ask for in a survey, but neither was it deemed
feasible to simplify the AHP model further, without signiﬁcantly reducing the output quality of the
model.
With the structural hierarchy of the AHP model established for the support, the surveys required for
data collection could be created and sent to the relevant experts. The survey Introduction, mail text,
and results can be found in Appendix H. Building on the results of these surveys it was then pos-
sible to calculate the weight of each characteristic concerning the remanufacturing activities and the
remanufacturing activities weight in determining the overall cost and energy index. The calculation
of the activity weights and the characteristics weights can be found in AppendixI and AppendixJ
respectively, following the FAHP methodology for doing so Kaganski et al. (2018); Ishizaka (2014).
The overall inﬂuence that the characteristics have on cost and energy was found to vary from 7.7% to
1.5%, which can be seen in detail in Appendix K
==PAGE:60==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 54
Figure 4.9: Overview of which product characteristics have been found to have an inﬂuence on cost
or energy usage in the respective remanufacturing activities. Note that this is the ﬁnal version where
’Complexity of Design’ was removed and ’Durability’ added, which is described in the following
sections

For the evaluation of the remanufacturing activities, four experts at Stykka participated in making the
evaluations required for the weighting in the AHP model, which can be seen in Figure 4.10. Based on
the experience, role, and knowledge from working in the respective positions in the case company, it
is argued that they were valid to use in the AHP model.
Figure 4.10: The four experts involved in the weight evaluations for the AHP model
4.4.3
Creation of data ranges
As quantitative and qualitative characteristics would be evaluated through the support, an additional
customization of the AHP method had to be implemented. To do this a data range was inserted into
the support for the different product characteristics, which can be found in Appendix G.
The quantitative characteristics were given ﬁgures determining the range, while the qualitative char-
acteristics have ﬁve different statements. These statements would determine if the product character-
istics will be determined as ’Very Low’, ’Low’, ’Medium’, ’High’, or ’Very High’ with a corresponding
index weight of 0, 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, or 1 respectively. The index weight could be used to multiply with
the characteristic weight and the activity weight to calculate the individual characteristics contribu-
tion to the overall cost and energy index.
The weight was then used to calculate the cost and energy index, which was done through four
separate calculations, one for each of the four remanufacturing activities: D&I, C, R&R, and R&QC.
This was done by multiplying the index weight by the characteristic weight and activity weight.
Conducting this calculation for all the different 21 characteristics and summing the results results in
the index for cost and energy. An example of this calculation can be seen in Section 4.8.2 where the
determination of the index weight is also shown.

To go from the conceptual level of the support to a developed MVP the goal was to test the support
on key elements with stakeholders in Stykka. The key features to develop within the support were:
• Product Characteristic Input
• Cost and Energy Index Output
• Recommendations for further design work
The three key elements of the support for the tool were then created, which entailed (i) Input of
product characteristics, (ii) Generation of Cost and energy index results, and (iii) Recommendations
for design and future work to be initiated, illustrated in Figure 4.11.

Recommendations and future work
To provide an easier overview for the user the product characteristic data input was split into a section
for quantitative and qualitative product characteristics. This was also included due to the stress put
by the COO, CEO, and head of circular of Stykka on emphasizing the relevance of quantitative metrics
to measure product design Appendix M.
The generation of results to provide an index for cost and energy usage concerning the product char-
acteristics was paired with a spider diagram showing the highest impacting product characteristics.
The future work and recommendations was a section providing recommendations based on product
design paired with a link to relevant CE tools to improve the product characteristics falling within
the ’High’ or ’Very High’ data range.
Feedback on MVP
After developing the MVP it was presented to the relevant stakeholders within Stykka. This was done
in a workshop evaluating the support tool, where the support acted as a boundary object facilitating
the feedback. The workshop can be seen described in Appendix M.
During the review of the MVP, the CEO and COO highlighted several areas for improvement to
enhance its usability and usefulness of the support. The CEO emphasized the need for more detailed
descriptions and examples of each characteristic used in the support. By explaining why each factor
is important, and exactly why this characteristics is relevant to remanufacturing.
Both the CEO and COO also suggested working on tailored recommendations focusing on only pro-
viding recommendations to the most critical design characteristics to improve remanufacturing per-
formance.
Both the CEO and COO stressed the incorporation of the durability of the material, to further investi-
gate the ability of materials to be remanufactured. They noted that the current version did not include
the aspect of materials in furniture, which is an essential aspect of assessing the remanufacturability
of products.

2. Tailored recommendations
3. Provide insights on material durability

4.5
Third iteration - Prototype I
Based on the feedback from the MVP workshop, the new more detailed prototype I was developed.
This involved getting more descriptions of the characteristics providing an introduction to the tool,
looking into the durability of materials as a factor, and providing tailored recommendations to de-
signers.
4.5.1
Interview with RL Tool Expert
To gain an expert perspective on the development of support for RL, an interview was conducted with
Parvin Mallick, a Ph.D. from DTU specializing in Circular Economy and Take-back Systems, with a
focus on the development of conﬁgurators (Mallick et al., 2024). The purpose of the interview was to
review the tool and gain insights from his experience creating a conﬁgurator, speciﬁcally regarding
how to set up the support to engage the user group. The interview can be found in greater detail in
Appendix O.
Upon showcasing the tool, Mallick’s main feedback was to focus on explaining the relevant product
characteristics identiﬁed. When building the take-back conﬁgurator, a key element in the conﬁgurator
was the creation of a playbook for the terms used (Mallick et al., 2024). The function of the playbook
was to explain how the terms were related to take-back systems and why they should be prioritized
within a company. This playbook was created based on background literature reviews created by
Mallick combined with insights from prior industry interviews.
Furthermore, Mallick emphasized an increased focus on the structure of the tool concerning the user
journey. His experience with creating the conﬁgurator provided him with insights into having a strin-
gent and easy-to-follow user journey outlined from the beginning of the conﬁgurator by introducing
an overview of steps within the conﬁgurator.
4.5.2
Tailored Recommendations in Support
Another focus area to improve was recommendations for product design. These were identiﬁed as
key features by the CEO and COO to enhance the tool’s usefulness for product designers. The full list
of recommendations are located in Appendix L.
The recommendations were developed based on the relevant literature reviewed in RC and DSI re-
lated to product characteristics and circular design. This ensured that designers only received ap-
propriate recommendations for product designs with the highest impact on the cost and energy of
remanufacturing. Additionally, a link to relevant CE tools and pertinent literature was inserted for
additional guidance and recommendations in starting the redesign process.
4.5.3
Characteristics Explainer
To increase the usability of the support tool, feedback from Mallick was integrated by providing
detailed explanations of each product’s characteristics. These explanations not only deﬁne each char-
acteristic but also show their connection to remanufacturing.
Explainers were created for all 21 product characteristics, highlighting the relevance of each and its
relation to the product’s remanufacturability. This effort aimed to ensure alignment between the
product designer and the product semantics used to describe the product characteristics. An example
of one of the explainers can be found in Figure 4.12.

The explainer of the 21 product characteristics can be found in Appendix N.
4.5.4
Durability index of materials
The feedback from the test of the MVP highlighted a need for providing insights on the durability
of furniture materials as a characteristic, which began a search for a method focused on measuring
material durability.
The durability of materials has been investigated by Mesa et al. (2020), who developed a Material
Durability Indicator (MDI), based on 11 CE support tools. The MDI identiﬁed three primary factors
to constitute durability: chemical durability, mechanical durability, and environmental performance
reﬂecting a trade-off between these factors for overall material performance (Mesa et al., 2020).
Minding the scope of this project and to lessen complexity mechanical durability was chosen. Me-
chanical durability measures the material’s resistance to mechanical loads under both static and dy-
namic conditions, indicating a longer life span concerning fracture, cracks, and plastic deformation.
It is calculated as the geometric mean of yield strength and fatigue strength, with a maximum value
of 100 shown in Figure 4.13 (Mesa et al., 2020).
Figure 4.13: Mechanical Properties from Mesa et al. (2020), selected to describe mechanical durability
Based on these mechanical properties the main materials used in the furniture industry were iden-
tiﬁed which were found to be various types of materials, taken from the EU EcoLabel material list
(Lifestyle & Design Cluster, 2024). These were then grouped by their yield strength and Fatigue
strength and based on this given a Dm-factor. The feature was integrated with a drop-down menu
where the user would be able to choose the main material of their products.
==PAGE:65==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 59
This feature would be a low-ﬁdelity version of the MDI support tailored to the furniture industry
integrated into the support as "main material" of the furniture product. Furniture products usually
consist of several materials, the main effort was on establishing an understanding of the primary
material in the product.
4.5.5
Workshop and feedback
To gather feedback on prototype I, a workshop was organized with Stykka’s CEO and COO found
in Appendix P. The purpose was to gain insights into the usability and usefulness of the support
tool. For the workshop, an arbitrary chair was selected to be tested within the tool. This was done
by the workshop users’ providing random inputs based on estimations of the characteristic data of
the chair. This was done as the focus of the workshop was not to test the calculation, but rather on
understanding, the ﬂow of the support and recommendations created.
The COO highlighted the importance of making weights and data ranges customizable, allowing
users to tailor the tool to their speciﬁc needs, thus increasing its ﬂexibility and relevance. Additionally,
the COO emphasized the need for an introduction page to outline the tool’s purpose and relevance
and how best to use it.
Both the CEO and COO agreed that the recommendations should be concise yet actionable, making
them easy to understand and implement in the product design process. They suggested splitting
the index result page between the product owners and designers. Product owners should be able
to quickly identify key performance indicators to work with, while designers should have detailed
results to channel their efforts during the design phase.
Hotspot analysis for characteristics was suggested to identify the most impactful characteristics and
their cost and energy usage index. This was because the most impactful characteristics were not
highlighted and the user of the tool had to identify them themselves, by comparing all the individual
characteristics contribution to the index.
The COO also requested the possibility of comparing multiple products, such that the index would
be more useful, as it would show the difference between products instead of a number without any
context. This was also to enable the designers to spot products that have been well designed for
remanufacturing, such that learnings could be drawn from these designs.
The feedback based on the workshop from the CEO and COO was found to be:
• Customization of weights and data ranges to tailor the tool to speciﬁc needs and furniture types
• Introduction to Support Tool
• Split results for project leads and designers
• Hotspot analysis to identify impactful characteristics
• Comparability between furniture products in the portfolio
4.6
Fourth iteration - Prototype II
The fourth iteration involved developing a mature version of the support. The goal was to develop
and present Prototype II for evaluation at Stykka. The feedback on Prototype I was used to lay the
groundwork for developing the support at a higher level of detail. The creation of customized data
ranges for speciﬁc furniture products and the comparability between different products was found
to require a substantial amount of time and data to develop, which is why the other feedback points
were prioritized in this iteration.

4.6.1
Overview of Key Design Characteristics
To gain an overview of the highest-impacting design characteristics, the COO and CEO of Stykka re-
quested a feature that would provide key stakeholders, such as heads of design and Product Owners,
with an overview of key product characteristics to change for the relevant furniture designs.
An overview section was created, highlighting a top-three selection of product characteristics with
the highest impact cost and energy performance. This selection was based on the highest-impacting
characteristics from the FAHP evaluation of remanufacturing activities.
The support tool results were then divided into two user groups: the product owner and the product
designer illustrated in Figure 4.14.
Figure 4.14: Output split between the two selected user groups in Product Manager and Product

To goal of the feature was for the product managers to gain an understanding of the key characteristics
to change concerning the index scoring of the furniture. The product designer would then receive the
means to further work with the characteristics.
4.6.2
Design hotspot analysis
Another feature that was created to enhance the usefulness and usability of the support tool was a
design hotspot analysis. This analysis provided implications of each characteristic, shown in per-
centages, providing an overview of which characteristics had the largest and lowest potential for
improvement.
This feature offered insights into how changes in product characteristics could have different cost
and energy usage effects on remanufacturing. It enables companies to focus on either cost or energy
usage. Additionally, it could help designers gain a preliminary understanding of how changes in
certain product characteristics could impact the product entering a new life cycle.
4.6.3
Workshop and Feedback
An evaluation of Prototype II was conducted with the case company by simulating a real use case of
a furniture product with the CEO of Stykka. Since the CEO has also been a co-developer throughout
the project, the CEO took the perspective of how the tool could be received by other intended users
and what problems they might have with using the tool.
For this test, a product had been chosen before the workshop, due to time limitations of the CEO’s
availability, which meant the quantitative data had been collected beforehand, which limited the
evaluation of the quantitative characteristics input.
==PAGE:67==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 61
The ﬁnal evaluation was conducted as a workshop, which can be seen in further detail in Appendix
Q, with in-depth notes on the feedback. Following is a description of the CEO’s usage of the tool
separated into the input and output parts.
Several clarifying questions had to be answered for the CEO to understand and evaluate the qualita-
tive aspects of the product. Despite the graphical illustrations and written explanation, it took time
and explanation for them to be interpreted as intended. Some terms were unknown and new to the
CEO, but when explained, had other variations of the terms they referred to.
The CEO noted that the qualitative evaluations had a negative tone to them and expressed concern
that the self-evaluation aspect would bias the product designers and make them unable to give an
objective evaluation. Concern about the possibility of faulty input due to bias or misunderstanding
of the characteristics was also expressed by the CEO.
The amount of text was also commented on as being distracting, especially the text required to un-
derstand new terms and academic deﬁnitions. The CEO followed the intended procedure and there
were no doubts about what the next step would be and how to paste in the required data. Difﬁculties
mainly seemed to lie in understanding some of the terms and exactly what evaluation was required.
The CEO had some difﬁculty in understanding and relating to the cost and energy index, as no indi-
cation was given as to what a high or low index exactly meant for the performance of the product.
Furthermore, the results and recommendations seemed a bit overwhelming in the amount of infor-
mation that was provided. The CEO needed time to ﬁgure out how well the product performed and
interpret the results. Many recommendations were shown at the same time and the CEO that it would
be nice with a quick overview of the main hotspots to focus on, as all recommendations could not be
feasibly implemented at once, but rather need to be considered with a priority.
4.7
Fifth iteration - Final version
Based on the simulated test of the support tool in Section 4.6, ﬁnal changes were implemented into
the ﬁnal iteration of the support tool.
’Complexity of design’ was decided to be removed from the model, as it confused, and the aspects
it tried to encapsulate, as the CEO highlighted, should already be reﬂected in other independent
characteristics, such as parts and fastener count among others. Additionally, it was deemed hard to
evaluate and receive an objective evaluation.
The terms and explanations used in the tool were directly taken from literature, several of which the
CEO had difﬁculties understanding. This caused erroneous inputs and evaluation. As this risk would
have a too great negative effect, it was decided to rephrase some of the qualitative characteristics. One
of the rephrased characteristics was ’Homogeneity of parts of fasteners’ which instead became ’Simi-
larity of parts and fasteners’, making the tool easier to use, by eliminating potentially new terms and
academic expressions. Additionally, the characteristic regarding customization was also simpliﬁed to
make it easier to understand.
Some visual improvements were implemented to better communicate what the results mean and
highlight where the user is supposed to be looking.
The CEO had also expressed some confusion as to why only some characteristics recommendations
were shown. To circumvent such confusion in the future an explanation was added, to tell the user
that they are performing well in the characteristic, rather than let them deduce this by themselves by
the lack of recommendations.
With these ﬁnal changes, the support tool reached its ﬁnal edition before the DSII phase.

Master Thesis
DTU
Page 62
4.8
The support
As a result of the iterative development process highlighted in the previous chapter, the ﬁnal version
of the support was developed and will be presented in the following section.
A tool to asses furniture products design in relation to the cost and energy of remanufacturing
The support serves as a furniture product design assessment uncovering hotspots in product charac-
teristics with relation to remanufacturing activities, based on cost and energy performance indicators.
The support is tailored to usage for furniture product designers. The primary objective is to enhance
the quality of design changes and support the decision to engage in RL activities, in relation to op-
timization of product design for remanufacturing. The output of the support is a cost and energy
index based on the product characteristics, that can aid designers in understanding the interrelation
between product characteristics and remanufacturing. Furthermore, it can also encourage furniture
companies to gain knowledge on the relevant product characteristic data. This approach can help
designers identify relevant changes to product design and provide an indication of how the product
design aligns with a potential future remanufacturing scenario.
The tool consists of three main steps: (I) Input of Characteristics, (II) Results and Key Improvement
Areas, and (III) Recommendations and Future Work, illustrated in Figure 4.15. These areas guide
product designers in a structured, step-by-step process to evaluate and enhance their designs.
Figure 4.15: Illustration of the main features of the support
By addressing the need for remanufacturing insights based on furniture product characteristic data,
the support tool aids designers in understanding and prioritizing relevant product characteristics.
It offers actionable recommendations for product design improvements and provides insights into
product performance for remanufacturing. In essence, the tool can help enhance the quality of de-
sign change in furniture product designs through product characteristic data, focusing on improving
design for remanufacturing.
4.8.1
Support activities
The support consists of three activities that that make out the general support, which are illustrated
in Figure 4.16.
==PAGE:69==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 63
Figure 4.16: Three main activities of the support
The purpose of these activities is to uncover the relationship between product characteristics and
remanufacturing activities.
The support was built in Excel, a format that effectively handles the multiple functions and analyses
required. Excel’s versatility allows for detailed data entry, calculation, and visualization, but is also
assumed to be a known data platform for the intended user group. Screenshots of the Excel sheets in
the support can be seen in Appendix R.
Activity 1 - Insert data areas
Firstly the designer has to choose a speciﬁc furniture product to investigate. Then the designer has
to enter qualitative evaluations and quantitative product data. An example of quantitative data and
qualitative evaluation is depicted in Figure 4.17.
Figure 4.17: The ﬁrst activity of Support: Insert Characteristic Data
Illustrations and descriptions of the product characteristics are provided, such that an alignment
between the designers can be made on the semantics of the different product characteristics. This re-
quires the designer to measure the relevant design characteristics and gain integrated insights into the
product. The quantitative characteristics are based on inputs of product metrics and the qualitative
are based on self-assessment related to statements. This educational component can help designers
become aware of how product design inﬂuences remanufacturing activities and also ensures data is
collected on the furniture designs
==PAGE:70==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 64
Activity 2 - Results and key product characteristics
Once the relevant characteristics are entered, the tool evaluates the characteristics to provide an index
on their impact on remanufacturing expressed in cost and energy usage, which is considered through
the four key value recovery activities: disassembly & inspection, cleaning, renewing & replacing, and
reassembly & quality check.
The key characteristics with the most impact are then highlighted, to provide a focus for the designer.
The index and top characteristics can also be viewed by the product manager, which can be useful
to align with the designers on key characteristics to be changed and identify design KPIs. By under-
standing the key characteristics and their link to remanufacturing, the designers can prioritize design
characteristics for current or future products. Furthermore, an illustration of the general performance
of all the product characteristics is visualized through the usage of the spider diagram.
An example of the product structure is illustrated in ﬁgure 4.18.
Figure 4.18: Second Activity: Index and Key Product Characteristics
Activity 3 - Recommendations and future work
Based on the AHP calculation, if the input to the speciﬁc characteristics is placed into the ’High’ or
’Very High’ range, the tool offers speciﬁc, actionable design recommendations paired with sugges-
tions for design methods for the key characteristics. These recommendations can guide designers on
how to modify their designs to enhance cost and energy performance during remanufacturing.
This approach can help to ensure that designers are given an indication of the current performance of
their designs but also have a clear path forward to enhance design for remanufacturing. A hyperlink
to design methods or relevant literature for further work on the speciﬁc design characteristic is also
outlined. Figure4.19 provides an insight into the link between the recommendations and require-
ments.
==PAGE:71==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 65
Figure 4.19: Third Activity: Recommendations and Future Work
4.8.2
Structure of Index System - Fuzzy AHP and Direct rating
To uncover how the AHP model calculates the cost and energy index, a step-by-step explanation can
be seen in Figure 4.20, which is explained in greater detail in this section through an example.
A hypothetical product containing 37 parts is used for this, which is pasted into the support tool. The
37 parts translate into the ’High’ category of the speciﬁc data range for part counts, which is trans-
lated into the index weight (2) ranging from 0-1.
In this example (3) the part count is 37, which is between 30 and 55, resulting in this product charac-
teristic being determined as ’High’ and a corresponding index weight of 0.75.
By multiplying the index weight of 0.75 with the characteristic weight and each of the activity weights
of remanufacturing, as shown in (4 and 5) for cost and energy respectively, and summing the num-
bers, then the total cost and energy contribution from part count to the overall indexes can be calcu-
lated.
Repeating this calculation for each product characteristic and adding them together (6) will result in
the overall cost and energy index for the furniture product put into the support tool.
As seen in (7), any product characteristic that falls into the ’High’ or ’Very High’ data range triggers
that the respective product characteristics are considered to be a key characteristic as described in
Section 4.8.1, which means that a recommendation for how to deal with the part count is going to be
shown in the results page in this example, as 37 falls into the ’High’ range.
==PAGE:72==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 66
Figure 4.20: Process for calculating the cost and energy index
