4.9
Evaluation plan for support
To enable testing with users from external furniture companies, an evaluation plan was outlined. The
ﬁnal version of the support was planned to be tested with two users from external companies, who
had not been part of the development process, to gain an unbiased evaluation.
The companies selected were two relevant furniture companies within Danish furniture design, which
were chosen based on their differences in design, but also as they represent widely different sub-
industries within the furniture industry. Both the companies, the test users, and the products tested
have been kept anonymous. The companies are therefore referred to as Company A and B and a
generic product was used to illustrate the type of product tested.
Furniture Company A is a new furniture company focusing on sustainable interior product design.
They are a relatively small company primarily producing furniture seating and tables. The user from
company A was the lead designer of the company. Company B is a large furniture company in
Denmark focusing on bathroom furniture design. Company B is a much more traditional furniture
company with a limited focus on sustainability initiatives. From company B a sustainability manager
working with product design and a product manager was participating.
The test of the tool was conducted as an observational usage of the support tool, where notes on re-
ﬂections, observations, and discussions were noted down. Answers to clarifying questions, that the
users might have, were provided throughout the usage of the tool. This ended in a general discus-
sion on overall impressions, feedback, concerns, doubts, and suggestions for the tool from the users’
perspective.
A test guide was developed to be used to gather data from the two tests of the tool. The template
for the guide can be found in Appendix S. The test guide was used as a way to structure notes,
observations, comments, reactions, etc., and provides the basis for a more in-depth overview of the
support test and the ﬁndings.
The test guide includes a survey with seven questions that represent the success criteria for the tool.
The test user must evaluate the support tool on a scale of 1-5, which are related to usability, usefulness,
and the users’ perception of the reliability of the tool which can be seen in Figure 4.21. The success
criteria have been developed based on the requirements identiﬁed in DSI.
Figure 4.21: The different success criteria that the tool will be evaluated on after testing the tool. The
ID deﬁnes how these criteria will be referred to in Section 5.2.
The results from the survey were interpreted as follows:
==PAGE:74==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 68
• Success (score 4-5): The tools meet or exceed the success criteria
• Needs improvement (score 3): The tool works as intended, but has areas that need improve-
ment
• Needs redesign (score 1-2): The tool does not work as intended and it needs redesign
The test was intended to follow the process shown in Figure 4.22, which does not follow the usual
and intended use of the tool, instead, it was expected that some of the product characteristic data
necessary to use the tool would require time to ﬁnd. Therefore, the user company was instructed
beforehand to select a product and ﬁnd the required quantitative information on that product. This
was not optimal, as the user might have questions about the data being requested beforehand, and in
general it would have been better to test the tool, without the user having any prior knowledge of its
content, as this would be the experience of real intended users. However, this was not feasible due to
the time consumption of such a test.
Figure 4.22: Overview of the timeline for the test
4.10
Discussion of PS
Stykka’s inﬂuence on the support development
As the support was co-created with Stykka ApS, a furniture company with a speciﬁc product design
focus for remanufacturing, the insights used in the development of the support were aligned with
these design principles. This helped the project to focus on relevant needs for designers and also pair
characteristics with cost and energy, as Stykka has its own remanufacturing operation of its kitchens.
The close co-creation also provides limitations to the tool, as the requirements and development are
highly inﬂuenced by the view Stykka has on circular furniture design. As Stykka has a high focus on
modularity and product durability for their kitchens, these aspects are also seen integrated within the
support. Furthermore, Stykkas is a start-up in furniture that has a certain view on product develop-
ment. This questions the usability and usefulness of the support for larger more traditional furniture
companies, that have not had the same focus on circularity within their product portfolio as Stykka.
Another requirement was to make the support usable for all furniture types, but as the support was
developed based on insights from a kitchen company, there might be bias in the tool development due
to the inﬂuence of Stykka, which could affect the applicability to other product types widely different
from Stykka’s. As furniture is a broad industry with many product types, the ability to cater the tool
to each of these product types would provide a more broadly applicable tool. The characteristics are
selected from literature related to other industries, which makes this aspect of the tool less affected
by the co-creation process with Stykka.
==PAGE:75==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 69
Limits to Usage of FAHP
The FAHP method provides a method for structuring a problem into smaller components, criteria
(Remanufacturing Activities), and sub-criteria (Product Characteristics), which has made it possible
to break down a complex task of considering cost and energy usage in remanufacturing depending
on the remanufacturing activities and the product characteristics of a product to be remanufactured.
Using FAHP obliviously creates a simpliﬁcation of reality and relies on the assumption that product
characteristics can be evaluated individually in how important they are in driving cost and energy
usage of remanufacturing, while in reality product characteristics most likely will have a synergistic
effect among each other, as illustrated in Figure 4.23.
To consider an example, it would be fair to assume that the importance of modularity in a product de-
sign becomes increasingly important the more parts a given product consists of. The synergistic effect
between characteristics, where the total effect is greater than the sum of the individual characteristics
effect, brings limitations to the FAHP as it is unable to account for these complexities.
Figure 4.23: Illustration showcasing that product characteristics, in reality, are dependent on each
other and ideally should not be evaluated in isolation
Quantiﬁcation, dependency, and subjectivity
FAHP is an effective method for quantifying complex problems but is not made to perfectly include
qualitative criteria. All the characteristics that were selected to be included in the model have been
extensively researched for a way how to quantify them, but was eventually not possible to do so in
a reasonable way for all. This is why eventually some of the characteristics do not have a numeral
input but rather a statement, that then is translated into a numeric value.
The generation of statements provides a huge simpliﬁcation and causes obvious issues regarding
losing nuances in these qualitative characteristics as well as the risk of the subjectivity of the tool user
inﬂuencing how the product will be evaluated.
For example, the product characteristics ’Quality testing’, which is used to evaluate how easy it is to
quality check the product. This is not directly a quantiﬁable aspect of a product, but perhaps rather
a combination of other quantiﬁable characteristics such as tolerance, surface treatment, parts count,
geometric complexity, etc. as illustrated in Figure 4.24. Which is likely the case with multiple of the
==PAGE:76==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 70
qualitative characteristics. However, time limitations restricted the research in trying to quantify all
qualitative aspects.
Figure 4.24: Illustration of independent and dependent characteristics. Tolerance required is an ex-
ample and not an included characteristic.
This was one of the reasons why the characteristic ’Complexity of product design’ was removed, as
it was deemed too dependent on other quantitative characteristics to bring any new unique evalu-
ation into the model, as its inclusion would have caused double accounting in some aspects of the
calculation.
Data ranges
Data ranges were created which allowed for both qualitative and quantitative characteristics to be
used in the same AHP model. Five different ranges were used which determined the index weight
used in the ﬁnal index calculation. The creation of these ranges and determining which number
or statement should correspond to which weight has been a substantial task in the development of
the AHP model. What is considered e.g. a high part count for one type of product, from a speciﬁc
company, for a designer with a speciﬁc background, might vary a lot. Therefore the current data
ranges are quite uncertain and not applicable across furniture products, as every product type within
furniture ideally should have its own data range to make it comparable. This was highlighted by
the COO of Stykka but was not integrated into the support due to a lack of data availability on the
difference in data ranges between product types.
4.10.1
Implications of value recovery as the focus area
By leaving out activities within RL, such as take-back, and focusing on value recovery through re-
manufacturing, the support can aid designers, but the granularity and focus limit its ability to provide
decision-making generally for RL. If a company assessing their furniture product in the tool does not
understand the scope of the support, then they might misuse it and have misleading interpretations
of the results.
As take-back activities, customer incentives, and resell values of furniture haven’t been assessed
through the support, these would also be highly relevant aspects to assess before engaging in RL
as a furniture company. These aspects were also highlighted by Stykka, who asked for a possible cus-
tomer engagement strategy for their take-back systems. This highlights the limitations of the support,
as it can only provide insights on the product design’s ability to be value recovered with a focus on
remanufacturing. The limited scope requires integrated knowledge of circularity strategies to com-
prehend, and a very extensive onboarding for the furniture designers is needed before engaging in
the usage of the tool. This again questions the usability of the support, as it requires prior knowledge
to understand the purpose.
==PAGE:77==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 71
4.11
Conclusion of Prescriptive Study
In this chapter, the development of a support tool, intended to address the gaps and needs identiﬁed
in Research Clariﬁcation and the Descriptive Study, is described through the Prescriptive Study. The
support was co-developed with Stykka ApS to enable designers to understand how to improve the
design of furniture products for being remanufactured based on product characteristics.
The support was developed with two functions in mind, both providing an index and outlining rel-
evant characteristics for product designers to facilitate a link between product characteristics and re-
manufacturing. It can be a support for designers in the furniture industry by providing insights into
how product characteristics are related to impacts on cost and energy consumption in the remanufac-
turing activities. These insights address key gaps that gap of a micro tool focused on product design
concerning remanufacturing within the furniture industry.
By outlining speciﬁc key product characteristics for designers, the tool can create a more focused
design process, potentially leading to product designs better aligned for remanufacturing.
Another gap identiﬁed in DSI was the lack of tools to evaluate product characteristics concerning cost
and energy performance. The index provided by the support enables designers to track the design
process or gain insights into the current state of the portfolio concerning remanufacturing, with a
focus on cost and energy usage. Additionally, a gap for designers to change furniture product design
was identiﬁed in DSI. This has been integrated into the support as prioritized recommendations are
shown for the key product characteristics paired with links to relevant additional tools.
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 5
Descriptive Study II
This chapter focuses on evaluating the support outlined in the evaluation plan from the Prescriptive
Study. Firstly, a reliability check was conducted by comparing real remanufacturing data to the index
produced by the support. Then an edge case and faulty inputs within the support were assessed,
followed by a consistency test of the outputs from the expert surveys. Then the support was tested
with two different furniture companies. The tests were conducted to gain insights on the supports
reliability, usefulness, and usability. These activities conducted for this chapter are outlined here:
• Reliability check
• Edge Cases and Faulty Inputs
• Consistency Test
• Test support with two furniture companies
5.1
Testing developed Support
5.1.1
Reliability Check of Index
As described in the evaluation plan in Section 4.9 the users were asked about their opinion of how the
tool identiﬁes the key product characteristics to improve product design for remanufacturing. This
was done to test the reliability of the FAHP model in calculating the cost and energy index, but such
an evaluation would only outline the subjective perceived reliability of the support by the test users.
To test the reliability with less uncertainty and subjectivity, an effort was made to collect data from
companies practicing remanufacturing. The goal was to compare the cost and energy index from the
support tool with real-world data, to see which relation, if any, there would be between the support
tool indexes and actual remanufacturing cost and energy usage.
Preferably real-world data on the individual characteristics’ contribution to overall cost and energy
usage should be used for the reliability check. However, this data was deemed unrealistic to obtain,
as in the literature no means of tracing cost and energy performance back to individual product
characteristics had been observed. Therefore, the focus was scoped to only trying to compare the
overall cost and energy index, rather than index from all 21 product characteristics individually.
After conducting extensive desktop research, 15 companies practicing different variations of reman-
ufacturing and take-back for furniture were identiﬁed. It was only possible to ﬁnd relatively few
companies engaged in the activities relevant, as only companies stating they conducted remanufac-
turing or repairing of furniture with an ’as new’ warranty coverage were considered. The companies
contacted were located in Denmark, the UK, Switzerland, Canada, the EU, and the US. The list with
all companies contacted and the template mail sent to them can be found in Appendix T.
72
==PAGE:79==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 73
Only a few replied to the request for data, and those who replied did not have the time or resources
to prioritize a test and provide data. For this reason, the reliability test of the index could only be
conducted with the case company Stykka.
Reliability Check With Stykka MVP Kitchen
For this reliability test, data was drawn from Stykka on their MVP kitchen, as both product charac-
teristics and company data on remanufacturing energy and cost were available.
To compare the real-world data to the index results, all four remanufacturing activities of the support
had to be covered. This meant gathering cost and energy consumption data for disassembly and in-
spection, cleaning, restoring and replacing, and reassembly and quality check seen in Figure 5.1. Cost
factors were gathered through interviews and data given by the Fulﬁllment and Logistics manager.
Figure 5.1: Energy and cost factors related to Stykka’s remanufacturing activities for the MVP Kitchen
To obtain data on energy usage, data was mainly taken from Stykka’s EPD on their MVP kitchen
(Stykka, 2024). Energy usage factors related to modules C1 and C3 were used EPD from the EPD, as
C1 is the dismantling where whereas C3 is the waste processing associated with the product. Addi-
tionally, speciﬁc processes were chosen from Ecoinvent 3.10 to support processes such as cleaning,
restoring, and quality checking these are all seen in 5.1. The full outline of the information used and
the calculation method is found in Appendix U.
The calculations of cost and energy usage were then compared with the production of new kitchens
in terms of manufacturing costs and energy demands to establish a ratio between them. The overall
comparison showed a 2.7 times cost reduction from virgin production to remanufacturing and a 4.2
times reduction in energy consumption. The kitchen received an index of 31 and 33, which is a
relatively good index compared to the worst case of an index of 100, which reﬂects the outcome of
the data points available. Based on a single data point, it is not possible to show the reliability of the
support, both due to only having one furniture product type and the data being native to the speciﬁc
remanufacturing process used at Stykka. More of such reliability checks with different product types
would have to be conducted to test the reliability of the index.
It must also be stressed that the reliability check between the index and real-world reductions is
not directly comparable, as the impact of design characteristics is not entirely relatable to real-life
reductions due to the complexity of other inﬂuencing factors. The check also revealed a misalignment
between the real data and the index results, with signiﬁcant differences in cost and energy levels,
whereas the index results showed similar values for both.
==PAGE:80==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 74
5.1.2
Edge case
It is expected that the support is not able to support all types of furniture and has limitations in its
applicability. To investigate this, the tool was tested by inserting a product that could be considered an
edge case, a one-piece chair, which is a piece of furniture having vastly different characteristics than
most other types of furniture, especially since the tool focuses heavily on part and fastener count in
products.
The resulting output index and key characteristics can be found in Figure 5.2. The product performs
well due to only consisting of one part and zero fasteners, but this also inhibits the possibility of
replacing parts of the chair if e.g. one of the legs breaks, in which case a whole chair would have
to be made to replace it. Another aspect is that the case company only operates with wood-based
furniture. It would be fair to assume that the remanufacturing activities of plastic-based furniture are
vastly different from wooden-based.
The support tool most likely cannot be used to evaluate a product such as the one-piece chair, as it is
unable to take into account its vastly different design principles and characteristics, therefore it can
be considered fair to assume that the support tool is limited to evaluate wood-based furniture.
Moreover, a shortcoming of the support tool is that it does not sufﬁciently punish products with
extremely few and more complex parts. As a product consisting of a few but highly complex parts
might be not considered to be especially suitable for remanufacturing.
Figure 5.2: Strata Once Piece Chair was a corner case test
5.1.3
Faulty input
The support tool was tested on how it would react to faulty input by the user, which could be pasting
in a letter rather than a number or using ’.’ as the decimal marker rather than ’,’. Both types of
errors were tested and if they happened they caused a signiﬁcant effect on the results, without it
being obvious to a potential user that an error had occurred. The tool has quite intricate methods of
calculation, which might cause a potential user to be unable to detect the error simply by seeing the
results, therefore a usability feature was added. This would ensure that any faulty input to the tool is
highlighted and the user is made aware that they have to take corrective action. This is depicted in
Figure 5.3.
==PAGE:81==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 75
Figure 5.3: Illustration of output from faulty inputs in Support
5.1.4
Consistency
For collecting the data on activities, and their relative importance in determining total cost and en-
ergy usage in remanufacturing, a pairwise comparison method was used. This is to ensure some
consistency in the evaluations made by the experts. The consistency check is done to ensure that the
experts compare the activities equally across their ratings following the transitivity principle.
By doing a pairwise comparison, it is possible to mathematically calculate the consistency in the
answers of the expert, which can be used to ensure consistent evaluations by repeating the evaluation.
The consistency of the expert’s evaluation is shown in Table 5.4 in column 3.
The method for doing so is described by Saaty and Vargas (2012a) and involves calculating the in-
consistency of the experts’ evaluation. Since the evaluation of experts is subjective, it is expected that
there is some inconsistency in the weighting calculated using the pairwise method, but with a limit
to how much inconsistency is acceptable. The weighting is considered consistent if the consistency
ratio is less than 0.10. From table 5.4 it can be seen that only a single of the 8 different consistency
calculations is below 0.10 in the ﬁrst expert evaluation.
The procedure outlined for dealing with inconsistencies is revisiting the evaluation that the experts
have conducted (Saaty and Vargas, 2012a). This was possible to do so for three out of four of the
experts, who retook the survey another time and achieved a consistent result. As it was not possible
to redo the evaluation with one of the experts, this expert’s evaluation of the activities was decided
to be left out of the ﬁnal support tool.
The calculation and procedure of determining the consistency ratio of the evaluations can be found
in detail in Appendix V and follows the methods and calculations described by Saaty and Vargas
(2012a) and following the steps outlined by Kaganski et al. (2018).
Having redone the evaluation ensured that most of the ﬁnal evaluations of the experts were consis-
tent. Not all the ﬁnal evaluations were above 0.1, but were included anyway as it was not possible to
redo them a third time, which would have been the ideal situation. This increased the true reﬂection
of the experts’ evaluation in the ﬁnal model. The results from the consistency calculation can be seen
summarized in Table 5.4
==PAGE:82==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 76
Figure 5.4: The consistency ratio should be above 0.10 before the evaluation can be considered consis-
tent (Saaty and Vargas, 2012a). It was not possible to get the Head of Design to redo the evaluation,
which is why their evaluations were omitted.
5.1.5
Cost and Energy Evaluation
An assumption required by the FAHP method is that it was possible to use the experts to evaluate
cost and energy separately. This was necessary for two different levels in FAHP methods, regarding
remanufacturing activities and product characteristics.
First, it was assumed possible to determine the different remanufacturing activities’ respective im-
portance in driving overall cost and energy usage in the remanufacturing of furniture.
This assumption was argued as reasonable since the experts are well-experienced in a furniture
kitchen company. Stykka specializes in remanufacturing kitchens and is the only company in Den-
mark practicing this, according to themselves, and have continuously improved their product design
according to design for remanufacturing principles.
In the survey collecting data on the product characteristics’ importance in determining the cost and
energy usage of the remanufacturing activities, the experts had to make direct evaluations of the
characteristics’ importance in determining cost and energy usage. It would be expected that there
are some correlations between cost and energy usage, as the cost of energy should be included in the
evaluation of the overall cost.
The resulting evaluations showed a high degree of similarity in the evaluations of characteristics. In
Table 5.5 the percentage of evaluations that are exactly the same for both cost and energy is shown,
split out into the different remanufacturing activities.
To interpret this, consider the top left cell with a percentage value of 94.7%. This means that for all 18
characteristic evaluations for cost and energy in the D&I remanufacturing activity, the COO gave the
same evaluation for cost and energy in 94.7% of the evaluations.
==PAGE:83==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 77
Figure 5.5: The percentage shows how many times the respective expert evaluated the same impor-
tance for the product characteristics in driving cost and energy across the different remanufacturing
activities
The COO expert and Head of Design expert have almost exclusively given the same evaluation for
each characteristic regarding cost and energy. Despite that some correlation is expected, this reﬂects
some error in the evaluation process.
It was possible to get a comment from the Head of Design expert, that expressed that they found
difﬁculty in distinguishing between cost and energy and their relation to the characteristics, as he
saw the two to be interlinked through product design and its impact on remanufacturing.
The inability to distinguish how product characteristics differ concerning cost and energy respec-
tively, might reﬂect one of two things. Either it is really difﬁcult to infer speciﬁc KPIs, such as cost
and energy usage, to individual product characteristics given the complexity of their relation is too
difﬁcult for a even expert to evaluate. The other reason could be that the experts chosen might simply
not be valid enough to make this evaluation. The Head of Design mostly works with designing and
optimizing the product design and is not close to the actual remanufacturing activities. The COO
mostly deals with the supply chain and general management of products and might also be too far
from the actual remanufacturing activities. This begs the question of how a more ﬁtting candidate
could be found, as personnel closer to the actual remanufacturing activities lack experience within
product design, such as the Fulﬁlment and Logistics manager, thereby having another key lack in
knowledge.
The remanufacturing activities were expected to be easier to evaluate as they more directly could
be associated with the various activities that drive cost and energy usage. In Table 5.6 the resulting
weights based on the Experts’ evaluations are shown.
The ﬁnal evaluations show that the experts were able to distinguish between the energy and cost
aspects of the remanufacturing activities, based on the fact that they are not evaluated to be exactly
equal. They seem, however, to be somewhat correlated, as expected.
==PAGE:84==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 78
Figure 5.6: The different weights that have been calculated for the remanufacturing activities are
based on the Experts’ evaluation. Note that the weight from the Head of Design is not included due
to lack of consistency, see Section 5.1.4 for further explanation
5.2
Testing developed Support with companies
In this section, the different tests conducted with Company A and Company B will be presented.
The tests followed the structure described in Section 4.9 and the applied test guide can be found in
Appendix S.
The test guides were used as a way to structure notes, observations, comments, reactions, etc., and
provide the basis for a more in-depth overview of the support test and the ﬁndings. The template
for the guides can be found in Appendix W and Appendix X for Company A and Company B re-
spectively. Both test sessions with the companies will be described individually in the following two
sections.
Company A - Designer furniture
The test with Company A was conducted with the lead product designer, who is an intended user
of the support tool. The user had been tasked with choosing a product before the test workshop and
ﬁnding the quantitative data on the product. Despite the written instruction a week beforehand with
conﬁrmation from the user, the data had not been collected and prepared. However, the product cho-
sen was a relatively simple chair with few parts and fasteners, where the required data was relatively
easy to obtain.
The overall evaluation by the user has been summarized and can be seen in Tabel 5.7 along with
the main points retrieved from the feedback and test guide. Following, there will be an in-depth
explanation of the test workshop, which is based on the test guide found in Appendix W.
==PAGE:85==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 79
Figure 5.7: The evaluation score by Company A and related feedback for the different success criteria.
The relation between ID and the success factor they represent can be seen in Figure 4.21
Input - Product Characteristic Data
The user could relatively easily locate the required data despite not having found the data beforehand.
The user used the customer manual of the product to locate the necessary data, this caused the data
to reﬂect the perspective of the ﬁnal customer rather than the manufacturer.
Company A’s chair is delivered to the ﬁnal customer in a semi-assembled state, which makes it take
up less space during delivery. Some parts of the product are pre-assembled as multiple parts are
glued together into one part. This caused the user to put the wrong amount of parts into the tool
since glued-together parts were counted as one part.
When the user understood that they needed to use the perspective of the manufacturing company,
a point was raised about components bought from suppliers. Hereby also stating if they should be
considered as one part or if they had to be considered in their disassembled state, which was an aspect
not previously considered in the project.
Taking the perspective of the ﬁnal customer also caused an error in the number of fasteners, as some
fasteners in the chair have been glued to ﬁt the parts permanently, so the user only inserted the
fastener count that the ﬁnal customer had to assemble.
Another error was regarding the number of unique parts, as the user inserted similar parts as being
the same part type. The intention is that if parts have the slightest differences, then they should be
inserted as separate parts, which was not understood by the user. In general, the user had many
questions regarding the different deﬁnitions used in the input page, which were both regarding the
quantitative and qualitative characteristics. This caused many clarifying questions to be asked before
the user could paste in the correct information.
The user noted multiple times that their company used the terms within the support with slightly
different interpretations.
The user also found that some of the qualitative characteristics were hard to evaluate by selecting the
statement most accurately describing the product.
Output - Results and Recommendations
The ﬁnal cost and energy index and key characteristics the user then was presented with are depicted
in Figure 5.8.
==PAGE:86==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 80
Figure 5.8: The Company A product tested in the support tool along with the resulting key character-
istics, cost and energy index
The user liked the overview the tool gives of the key product characteristics that the tool user should
focus on, as this can provide initial guidance as to what should be focused on for product develop-
ment if a company wants to engage in remanufacturing activities.
The user also found the recommendations useful but would have liked even more depth to them.
This is especially concerning material properties and limitations that come with using speciﬁc mate-
rials and associated coatings and treatments. This could guide the designer in making choices and
understanding certain limitations and possibilities with various materials and treatment possibilities
in product design.
The user also found that some of the recommendations were not directly applicable to their chair, as
they were developed to be generic and therefore broadly applicable to different furniture products.
Therefore, some adaption and critical reﬂection had to be conducted before some of the recommen-
dations could be applied.

working with product design and a Product Manager heading the department they both came from.
They had, as instructed a week prior, prepared a product to test in the tool and found the required
quantitative data and the test could be conducted as planned.
The overall evaluation by the user has been summarized and can be seen in Table 5.9 along with the
main points retrieved from the feedback and test guide. Following this is an in-depth explanation of
the test workshop, which is based on the test guide found in Appendix S.
