Input
The users had to prepare the quantitative information required and could relatively quickly insert the
data into the tool. The users showed difﬁculties in interpreting some of the characteristics and had
made some assumptions and interpretations of their meanings, which were discussed initially. An
example is that they had been unsure whether materials such as glue or labels should be included or
not or if fasteners should be included as parts or not.
During the qualitative evaluation, they had difﬁculties evaluating themselves in multiple aspects.
Rather than objectively consider their own product and how it performed, they openly stated that
their product probably performed better in relation to competitors in the industry. This statement
dominated their evaluations, that they did not do worse than competitors. They only rated them-
selves in the ’Very Low’ to ’Medium’ range, which made them conduct a hypothetical comparison
with similar products of competitors rather than striving towards doing an objective evaluation of
their product in isolation.
An observation was also that the manager mostly was telling the product designer what to ﬁll out
and provided comments about not wanting to perform badly in the evaluation. In this way, the
product designer was not contributing with many inputs regarding the qualitative evaluations. It
was observed that the participants might have been trying to make their results better by gamifying
the tool, in order to make their product perform badly.
Output - Results and Recommendation
The ﬁnal cost and energy index and key characteristics the user then were presented with are depicted
in Figure 5.10.
==PAGE:88==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 82
Figure 5.10: The Company B product tested in the support tool along with the resulting key charac-
teristics, cost, and energy index
The users had difﬁculties interpreting the index in the results as there both is an overall index and a
percentage for each characteristic on how much they percentage-wise contribute to the overall index.
When they were explained the index they expressed that they liked the overview and that they had
recently had a strategic meeting in the company, where they had discussed the amount of parts and
materials used in their products and that these exact areas were their focus. These were also the key
product characteristics the tool identiﬁed, which they found relevant for their exact situation. How-
ever, they questioned whether the scoring and tool results were fact-driven enough to base strategic
and design changes for products.
They liked the recommendations and saw the use of such a tool in the design process for new prod-
ucts, but also found them to be a bit general and hard for a designer to implement directly during a
design process. Additionally, they were in doubt about how such a tool should be properly integrated
into a design process for it to best support a designer.
Summarizing of tool test
The overall impression of the test results suggests that the tool generally lives up to the success criteria
and works as intended, with the intended usability and usefulness for the user, however, there are
areas of improvement. This is especially true for the usability of the tool where only one out of the six
usability scores is 4, where the rest got a score of 3. This indicates that the usability of the tool should
be improved. This was especially concerning the understanding of the semantics of the characteristics
that disrupted the ﬂow.
The support tool was mostly found to meet the success criteria for the usefulness of the tool, especially
in using the tool as a hotspot indicator for further work. Interest was expressed in developing the tool
even further with more focus on structural design and material properties, with recommendations
more aligned with the workﬂow of the product designer.
5.3
Discussion of DSII
Reliability of Index
To test the reliability of the support, it was not possible to ﬁnd other companies with remanufacturing
data on cost and energy for furniture products who also had time and resources to test the tool.
==PAGE:89==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 83
If data from other companies had been acquired it would have given more insight into how reliable
the index is at inferring anything about actual remanufacturing cost and energy usage. The single
data point from the case company was not enough to infer anything conclusive about the index reli-
ability.
Given that the index could not be inferred to have a direct indication of actual cost and energy usage,
the index might be best used for comparison between different product concepts, as suggested by
one of the test participants from the external companies. Alternatively, the index can be considered
as an ideal remanufacturability index, such as James et al. (2021) has done with a similar model for
automobiles, with an index inferring the ideal product design (index of 0) and least ideal product
design (index of 100) regarding cost and energy.
However as seen from the tests, the index is difﬁcult to understand, as the intended users cannot relate
to how their products potentially could perform in terms of cost or energy for remanufacturing. It
might be hard for users of the tool to use a cost and energy index without having any prior experience
with remanufacturing activities.
At its current state, the tool might mostly be useful as an initial hotspot indicator, which can be
used to identify the potential most costly and energy-intensive characteristics of a product design for
remanufacturing, which then can be investigated further to be afﬁrmed.
Semantic variance
During the test of the support tool, it was necessary to explain several of the characteristic terms to
the users during the tests. This was found to be both due to some expressions being new to the users
and because they had different understandings of the terms used. This is despite the graphic and text
explanation included in the support tool. This variance in the use and understanding of terminology
can cause erroneous input in the tool, which consequently will produce a false output. To ensure
proper use of the tools it might be necessary to include a more thorough explanation of the character-
istics, to ensure misinterpretations are avoided. This could be in the form of a lengthy introduction
or an associated playbook with the tool, that functions as a glossary for product designers.
This variance in semantics might suggest a general lack of common classiﬁcation and terminology
across the furniture industry. The lack of this could be a barrier in further work trying to quantify
product characteristics and their impact on remanufacturing activities.
Experts used
It was found that some of the experts used for evaluation in the FAHP model could not differentiate
between cost and energy usage regarding the individual product characteristics and their importance
in inﬂuencing cost and energy usage. This affects the usefulness of having two distinct indexes. It
should be investigated in further detail to determine why some of the experts were not able to make
a distinction between cost and energy usage. If the experts could not differentiate between cost and
energy usage due to a lack of knowledge and experience, then the FAHP model can be improved by
ﬁnding experts outside the case company, who might have more validity in giving their evaluation.
Another way to improve the model could be to separate some of the evaluations from each other. It
might be unreasonable to expect an expert to have valid knowledge related to all remanufacturing
activities. Instead, experts could be found to evaluate only the speciﬁc activities they are engaged
in, and even split these for cost and energy usage. E.g.: The Fulﬁlment & Logistics manager could
evaluate only the cost of D&I and R&QC, as those are the activities they are directly involved in, the
COO could evaluate the cost of R&R, since they are working closely with the supply chain and cost
of parts and processes, and the Head of Circular could evaluate the energy usage of R&R since they
are working with developing LCA’s for products, processes, and spare parts.
==PAGE:90==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 84
It would be valuable to revisit all the experts and discuss their perspectives on this, to determine if
either is the case and what to do to improve the model.
Variance in Target Users
The two testers of the tool had different levels of engagement and understanding of the support when
testing it. Company A was more oriented and aware of how the different characteristics inﬂuenced
the remanufacturing activities, while Company B was unsure how some of the characteristics could
impact the remanufacturing of products.
The usability and usefulness of the tool might therefore depend on the background knowledge of
the users concerning circular design principles. This could indicate that companies that have lower
knowledge of circular design strategies have less usability of the support.
Both users found that they would have liked more details in the recommendations, with more focus
on material properties and structural design. This was considered to make the outputs of the tool
more directly useful and implementable in the product designer’s work.
The tool could be further developed with these material and structural details, but this would require
a more detailed analysis of how furniture product designers work, to adapt to tool to better ﬁt that
process. In its current state, the support tool is therefore more useful in aiding companies to make
more strategic design choices and setting the direction for the development of product portfolios, as
it does not provide enough detail for direct and continuous usage in the actual design process.
The support tool was also designed to be applicable to furniture products in a broad sense, however,
it was also seen that it is not suitable for all types of furniture and therefore in its current state is best
used on wooden furniture.
The tool could be further adapted to be more speciﬁc to different furniture product types. Further
work could be done in deﬁning different furniture product categories, where the tool then is adapted
to address the unique design aspects and product characteristics of speciﬁc furniture types.
5.4
Conclusion of DSII
By testing the support’s reliability, edge case, and faulty inputs the support was investigated to assess
usability and reliability issues. A reliability test was created showing that the results of the index
might have some correlation with real remanufacturing performance in the instance of Stykkas data,
but as the data was gathered from the company the tool was developed with, it was not viewed as a
conclusive test. Many more reliability tests would have to be performed to test the index. The edge
case provided an aspect of the limits of the support and the faulty input highlighted the possible user
issues within the support.
As a result of applying the developed support tool to two different furniture companies, it was pos-
sible to conclude that the tool can support designers in identifying potential product characteristic
hotspots in relation to remanufacturing. This is primarily due to the tool’s ability to highlight rele-
vant characteristics to measure, provide key characteristics to change, and outline the hotspots related
to the impact of these characteristics. The support also showed room for improvement, as users had
issues understanding the deﬁnition of the characteristics and the output index for cost and energy. In
this way, the support became more valuable as a hotspot indicator for potentially relevant product
characteristics than as an actual indicator of the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing.
To enhance the support, further investigation into semantics in the furniture industry would be neces-
sary. Additionally, data gathered from external companies on their remanufacturing activities would
also need to be collected to increase the usefulness of the index.
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 6
Discussion
Intended users
The decision to engage in RL is manifold. It depends on several aspects, starting from the goal for a
company to engage in RL, which KPIs are chosen to measure it, the recovery strategy pursued, and
the macro, meso, and micro factors that affect the take-back and value recovery activities.
Considering all the aspects inﬂuencing the decision to engage in RL activities, this thesis has focused
on addressing product design by supporting the quality of product design changes for remanufac-
turing measured through cost and energy usage. The narrow focus was inﬂuenced by the empirical
data availability through the case company resulting in a speciﬁc and detailed tool. The narrow focus
from the case company affects the broad applicability of the tool as seen in company tests, where
Company B had difﬁculties in understanding aspects of the tool and their implication for remanufac-
turing. However, Company A requested even more details about the materials and structural design
and their relation to remanufacturing, suggesting a need for an even more detailed and narrowly
focused tool. A more broadly applicable tool could have been developed, which could have focused
more on general circularity aspects of product design and educating how it can help enable circular
activities, but would have come at the cost of less usefulness for Company A.
The narrow focus of the support tool can therefore seen as decreasing its usability and usefulness
for the general furniture industry, but increasing the usefulness for the speciﬁc companies that are
engaged in circular design activities. These companies can therefore be considered to be the most
suitable intended users of the support.
The difference in perception of the support between Company A and Company B calls for further in-
vestigation into the understanding and integration of CE within different furniture companies. Since
Company A and Company B are widely different companies with distinct goals and levels of circular
design adaption, their perspectives varied on the possible use cases, usefulness, and usability of the
support.
Prior knowledge of circular principles is seen as key to understanding how to correctly use circular
indicators as described by Jerome et al. (2022), which corresponds to the issue observed in the support
test.
The education of circular design principles has not been an integrated part of the support. Since
knowledge of circular strategies is crucial for utilizing the support effectively, designers need to have
prior insight into these principles before using the support. The journey of circular adaption contains
multiple steps and the tool only focuses on supporting design improvement for remanufacturing,
but as more companies start to become educated on circular principles, this speciﬁc support might
become more useful and usable to the broader furniture industry.
85
==PAGE:92==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 86
Implementation of the support
The support intends to enhance quality design changes for remanufacturing, but as the tool has not
been tested during an actual design process, it is unknown whether it can improve the quality of
design change. The test users expressed concerns about how the tool should be implemented into the
current workﬂow of the designers. Therefore, introducing the tool to designers without any modiﬁ-
cation may cause disruptions and cause the tool adaption to fail, as the integration of the support has
not been tested in a product design process.
Implementation of the tool should ideally be integrated with the current workﬂow, tools, and meth-
ods that designers are currently utilizing in product development. The workﬂows would therefore
have to be examined in greater detail to understand how the tool can be properly integrated.
A method already used to measure environmental performance is LCAs, which are widely adopted
for assessing the environmental impacts of products. When exploring barriers within the furniture
design process, companies highlighted resource constraints in terms of time and resources. The CEO
of Stykka further emphasized the lack of time when designing furniture products. Integrating LCAs
within the furniture product design process would be difﬁcult, as LCAs require expensive data and
expert insights, which are not compatible with furniture design processes. This is why the assump-
tion is that the usage of CIs can become useful, as they do not require expert knowledge and usually
provide low amounts of data (Lindgreen et al., 2020). Designers could therefore use CIs, like the
support developed, to gain indications of a product’s impact on the performance of remanufacturing.
While LCAs enable a detailed understanding of the environmental impact of products, the CEO of
Stykka noted that LCA outputs are challenging to correlate directly with actionable design recom-
mendations. Providing recommendations within CIs can aid designers in iterating on designs within
a product design process that is restricted on resources and time.
6.1
Impact of support
Impact on Micro-Level
The impact of the support is only targeting the product design, which is one of many micro fac-
tors that need to be assessed when deciding to engage in RL activities. A product design focused
on enabling remanufacturing activities has low usefulness if the business model, knowledge level,
operations, and other micro factors within a company are not aligned to enable remanufacturing.
The impact of the support on a micro level therefore depends on the user company’s engagement in
aligning micro factors towards remanufacturing practices.
Using the support in its current form and adopting the tools’ understanding of the product charac-
teristics can help product owners and designers understand potential hotspots in product design for
remanufacturing. The support requires the user to critically evaluate the outcomes and recommen-
dations and to align them with other micro factors within the company that the support does not
assess. The support outputs can serve as a foundation for innovation by designing products that an-
ticipate future RL engagement. This approach might spark efforts to optimize other factors, such as
operations and business models, aligning them with product design to enhance RL activities.
Through DSII, it was discovered that the product characteristics described in the tool were interpreted
and understood differently by the test companies. This misalignment impacts the usefulness and us-
ability of the support. The usefulness and usability of the support could be increased if companies
adapted the support to ﬁt their understanding of different product characteristics and terminology.
The exact characteristics used in the support might not ﬁt the goal and preferred mean of measuring
by any given user company. Here the support might instead be able to help designers by providing
a focus on relevant product characteristics that can create the foundation for KPIs for design for re-
manufacturing. Where the principles of characteristics can be learned through the usage of the tool,
==PAGE:93==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 87
but have to be adapted by the company to ﬁt its speciﬁc goal.
Impact on Meso-Level
Multiple factors at the meso level were found to be relevant in the eventual decision on whether to
engage in RL activities. Although the support does not directly address factors on the meso level, the
product design for remanufacturing is highly dependent on several meso level factors.
For companies in the furniture industry to engage in remanufacturing, the systems surrounding the
company must enable these processes. Currently, only a single manufacturer in Denmark can support
Stykka’s remanufacturing operations, showing the lack of remanufacturing facilities currently avail-
able. Furthermore, signiﬁcant issues related to setting up effective and efﬁcient take-back schemes
across Europe are needed to facilitate a large-scale transition into RL practices (Ecores et al., 2017).
Since furniture companies largely consist of SMEs, the ability of systems around them to provide
support for engaging in circular practices is key to seeing a transformation within the industry.
Without a supply chain supporting remanufacturing practices, it might in many cases be futile to
spend resources on designing products for remanufacturing. Although the tool does not address
meso level factors directly, it might indirectly be able to highlight what is required of the supply
chain to realize the opportunities a product design for remanufacturing provides.
6.2
Limitations of Support
Several limitations were identiﬁed upon developing the support in PS and subsequently testing it
throughout DSII.
Index
DSII showed the challenge in ﬁnding relevant data to provide a reliability test of the index for cost
and energy, which presents a signiﬁcant limitation to the index, concerning its ability to reﬂect actual
remanufacturing performance. This causes limited usefulness of the index in isolation, as seen by the
test companies who found it hard to evaluate the performance of the product design based on the
index alone. This might leave the index best used as a means of comparison, to evaluate and compare
different furniture product design alternatives.
Furthermore, some of the experts used to calculate the weights in the AHP model showed a high
similarity between their evaluations of cost and energy. The experts might have been unable to dis-
tinguish between cost and energy and therefore given them the same evaluation. This limits the tool’s
ability to differentiate between the cost and energy usage aspects of a product design in remanufac-
turing.
Catering to different furniture type
The data range’s ability to cater to different product types within the furniture industry is uncertain.
Since the support was developed based on insights from a kitchen company, it is unclear how rep-
resentative these insights are for the rest of the industry. Different furniture companies might have
different perceptions of e.g. what is considered a high or low amount of parts concerning their spe-
ciﬁc remanufacturing goals and other factors. The pre-set data ranges might therefore have limited
applicability for other furniture products and perhaps require customization to circumvent this.
Varying semantic
The test also showed how the users had difﬁculty understanding the characteristics correctly, as the
semantics and understanding of terms related to product characteristics vary. This limits the tools’
==PAGE:94==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 88
broad applicability and the support might have to be adapted to the semantics used within a company
before being used by the product designers.
6.3
Reﬂections on the Design Process
The thesis was structured around the DRM, providing a framework that structured the research pro-
cess, enabled effective management, and helped identify and address critical gaps. The collaboration
of the project has been with the furniture company Stykka Aps.
The project began with an exploratory literature review that identiﬁed relevant studies and provided
a basis for the research plan, which outlined how and when each question would be addressed in the
Descriptive Study I.
In Descriptive Study I, the focus was on the initial research questions by reviewing academic litera-
ture. This phase identiﬁed key environmental and economic factors relevant to take-back and value
recovery systems and pinpointed existing gaps in the literature.
In the Prescriptive Study, a support tool was developed to address the gaps found in Descriptive
Study I. This development was conducted in collaboration with Stykka Aps, whose involvement was
crucial for deﬁning the necessary support and shaping the tool’s content. Regular prototype test-
ing during this phase allowed for continual reﬁnement by identifying missing elements and making
adjustments to better meet user needs.
The project concluded with Descriptive Study II, with the evaluation of the usefulness and usability
of the support tool. The feedback from this phase was positive, helping reduce resistance to change
within organizations and facilitating easier implementation of the tool. This stage provided valuable
insights into the tool’s practical application and informed the ﬁnal adjustments to its design.
6.3.1
Limitations of the design process
A signiﬁcant limitation to the process was the number of companies used in the process, as co-
developing with Stykka introduced a bias to the project, as their perspective on circularity inﬂuenced
the development process. This focus limited the diversity of insights and potentially constrained the
tool’s applicability across different companies with varying insights on circular product design. By
only co-creating with a single company, their approach to circularity signiﬁcantly shaped the support
tool, which might not fully address the diverse needs and practices of other companies within the
industry. While the participatory design approach provided valuable insights, it also meant that the
support tool development was largely driven by Stykka’s methods and perspectives.
Throughout RC a broad scope was applied by the project to gain an understanding of manufactur-
ing industries’ adoption of RL. This provided insights into different barriers in manufacturing, RL
maturity within different industries, and performance measures for RL. However, RC could have
been utilized differently if the scope of the thesis had been narrowed earlier in the process. If a fo-
cus on furniture and product design had been used from the start, a more detailed understanding
of furniture products could have been achieved through RC. Hereby the systematic literature review
could have been on the micro aspects of factors for the furniture providing a better focus on factors
related to product design. However, this could also have resulted in leaving out the insights from the
automobile industry, which were valuable and heavily inﬂuenced the development of the support.
In the Prescriptive Study, the focus was to rapidly develop an MVP to enable testing of the support
features. This provided feedback which laid the foundation to further develop the support. The
choice of an early development of MVP determined many aspects of the general structure and con-
tent of the support, upon which the next iterations were based. This allowed for many iterations to
improve the support, but if more time had been spent exploring the needs and barriers of the design-
ers in their workﬂow, it might have inﬂuenced the focus of the support. Which could have resulted
==PAGE:95==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 89
in a support better aligned to the design process used within furniture design. This could have been
explored by detailing the communication channels and touchpoints the designer uses in the design
process to understand where the support would best aid in providing insights and how the format
could best ﬁt the workﬂow.
6.4
Recommendations for further work
Semantics and classiﬁcation
A lack of common understanding of product characteristics was observed through the company tests.
Therefore it might be worthwhile to investigate this further and provide an understanding of how
product characteristics are understood differently within the furniture industry. The lack of semantic
coherence between different companies is something the CEO of Stykka also highlighted as a major
barrier to measuring the circularity of design.
The support tool in its current state, with the lack of this common semantic, could beneﬁt from having
an associated playbook along the support tool itself. This could provide an introduction to the circular
economy for users new to this and function as an in-depth glossary of the product characteristics, to
highlight how terms are deﬁned in the tool and that users might have their own understanding or
be using other terms for the same thing. This could also act as an educational component providing
knowledge on circular principles, which has been discussed as a prerequisite for using the support.
Product type adaption
Furthermore, specialized product characteristics and data ranges focused on different product types
could be created. The ability to specialize outputs and relevant characteristics based on the speciﬁc
furniture product types. This would enable a customization of the tool concerning the product types.
By creating a support that is usable for the furniture industry, the broad scope also puts limitations
on recommendations, where an investigation into the product types in furniture could provide more
precise design recommendations suited to that product type.
Data ranges
The data range in the current FAHP model has been found in the literature and developed in collabo-
ration with Stykka. Further work could be done to reﬁne these data ranges. The ranges could also be
adapted to the product type adaption, where a data range could be developed for each product type
of furniture, which would increase the possible level of detail in the input and output and provide
more speciﬁc recommendations.
Expert evaluation
The choice of experts heavily determines the indexes and as the developed FAHP model simulates a
complex issue, there is room for more iterations to ensure an accurate reﬂection of the opinions of the
experts. An investigation could also be made into ﬁnding more valid experts with speciﬁc knowledge
about exact remanufacturing activities, rather than using one expert to evaluate all remanufacturing
activities, as it was noticed some experts could not differentiate between cost and energy.
Revisiting the experts with their results could help determine whether it would be more appropriate
to require speciﬁc experts to only evaluate parts of the remanufacturing process. Creating an index
based on the relationship between product characteristics and cost and energy usage is a challeng-
ing task, and it might be worthwhile to explore alternative methods that can be used to assess this
relationship.
==PAGE:96==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 90
Quantiﬁcation of product characteristics
Many of the characteristics in the tool are qualitative characteristics that have been quantiﬁed by
associating a statement with an index score. This leaves subjectivity having a big inﬂuence on the
input and output of the tool. Further research could be done trying to quantify more of the qualitative
characteristics so that they can be objectively evaluated and the support tool could consist solely of
independent characteristics.
6.5
Fulﬁllment of Learning objectives
1. Describe how reverse logistics can be an enabler for the circular economy within different
manufacturing industries.
In the Research Clariﬁcation, an exploratory literature review on RL as an enabler for CE across var-
ious manufacturing industries was conducted. The enabling role of RL was uncovered through se-
lected industries. These industries were compared to understand the varying levels of RL integration
and grounds for adapting CE practices.
2. Understand and describe barriers to reverse logistics within the European Furniture industry.
In Descriptive Study I, an exploratory literature review of the furniture industry was combined with
empirical insights from the case company. The approach provided an understanding of the barriers
within the furniture industry from both academic and empirical perspectives. This was used to un-
derstand which barriers were most relevant for the furniture industry to integrate RL further. The
review and description of barriers for the manufacturing sector in Research Clariﬁcation helped to
understand that barriers are highly contextual and do not depend on just the type of industry alone,
but on other aspects as well.
3. Conduct a systematic literature review researching key factors inﬂuencing the environmental
and economic performance of reverse logistics.
A systematic literature review was conducted within Descriptive Study I to map out the key factors
inﬂuencing the economic and environmental performance of RL. To gain an overview of the factors
identiﬁed, they were grouped into the macro, meso, and micro levels depending on the inﬂuence
a company has on them. The overview highlighted the range of different factors that inﬂuence RL
and the need to be speciﬁc as to which level and category of factors the support should assess. The
systematic review also revealed how the same factor can inﬂuence both economic and environmental
performance.
4. Determine and outline the needs and requirements of a reverse logistics support tool within the
furniture industry through appropriate data collection in collaboration with relevant stakeholders.
Using insights from exploratory and systematic literature reviews conducted within Research Clari-
ﬁcation and Descriptive Study I, a co-creation workshop with the case company was applied to gain
insights on relevant requirements for a reverse logistic support tool in the furniture industry. The
workshop provided valuable insights into the needs of the stakeholders, which were then paired up
against the ﬁndings from the literature reviews to provide the needs and requirements of the support.
This gave insights into the need to provide a support tool that focuses on product characteristic data
and provides actionable design change recommendations.
==PAGE:97==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 91
5. Assess and select relevant factors and measures for economic and environmental performance
to be used in the support development.
Based on the identiﬁed needs and requirements the scope was narrowed down to addressing the
micro focus of ’product design’. Here the relevant factors representing product design were identiﬁed
to be product characteristics. The selection of the characteristics was based on the establishment of an
initial set of potential characteristics, from where suitable characteristics were to be selected and used
in the tool. The characteristics were selected depending on their relevance, data availability, resource
requirements, and ease of use. Finally, the characteristics were adapted to ﬁt the tool and a few extra
were created, resulting in a total of 21 relevant characteristics.
The performance measure used in the tool was chosen based on the needs of the case company and
which was most applicable within their product processes. Activity-based cost of remanufacturing
operations and operational energy usage were selected as the most relevant economic and environ-
mental performance measures.
6. Co-develop a support tool that can enhance the cost and energy usage performance of remanu-
facturing for furniture products with relevant stakeholders.
Within the Prescriptive Study the participatory design method was used to provide a collaborative
development process, the support tool was developed with the case company through ﬁve iterations.
The focus was on providing cost and energy usage insights based on product characteristics, with
continuous feedback workshops conducted after each iteration to form the ground for further de-
velopment on the prototypes. Additionally, workshops were held to evaluate the tool with relevant
stakeholders from the case company. This led to the development of a support that was structured
around the needs of the intended user. A semi-structured interview was also conducted with an
expert in support tools structure to gain insights into the user journey of the support.
7. Select and use relevant methods to assess chosen economic and environmental performance
measures for relevant factors of reverse logistics.
The AHP method was discovered through the literature review of uncovering current micro indica-
tors used for CE. The method was selected as it allowed for the assessment of various criteria, such as
product characteristics, regarding their inﬂuence on the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing.
The method was adapted to establish a link between product characteristics and remanufacturing
activities that provides an actionable output and performance overview.
A hierarchy was constructed according to the AHP method, where the cost and energy usage of re-
manufacturing activities, which are determined by the 21 characteristics, were used to determine the
overall cost and energy usage of remanufacturing through two separate indexes. The fuzzy method-
ology was used to gather evaluations from valid experts in the case company which were calculated
into weights to be used in the AHP model.
8. Develop and present actionable indications of key areas of improvement in product design that
can reduce cost and energy usage related to remanufacturing based on relevant user inputs.
By leveraging the collaborative nature of the Prescriptive Study, inputs from the case company were
used to outline key design improvement indicators. These were based on various workshops fo-
cused on implementing features to enhance the quality of design changes for furniture designers.
The user inputs provided the foundation for developing features that offer designers insights to en-
able a potential reduction in cost and energy consumption of remanufacturing activities by altering
key aspects of product design. The key improvement areas were used to provide educational support
for designers by explaining the interrelation between the product designs’ impact on the performance
of remanufacturing activities.
==PAGE:98==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 92
9. Test the usability, usefulness, and reliability of the support tool with relevant stakeholders
within the furniture industry.
In Descriptive Study II a test of usability and usefulness was created through two workshops con-
ducted with relevant furniture companies, that had not been part of the development process. The
tests were structured around a test protocol designed to evaluate the support tool. The tool was
tested with the companies’ furniture products to mimic real-use scenarios. Insights on usability and
usefulness were gathered by distributing a survey with numeric rating scales for different aspects of
the support tool. The tests showed the tool being viewed as useful and usable but had improvement
potential in relation to product characteristic deﬁnitions and index communication. The reliability
of the index was tested with data from the case company, but as this is only a small segment of the
furniture industry, the reliability of the index is still largely uncovered.
10. Outline possible limitations of the support tool based on empirical data from intended users.
From the workshops with internal companies in the Prescriptive Study and external companies in
Descriptive Study II, an understanding of the support tool’s limitations was achieved. This showed
a limitation in the semantics used to describe the product characteristics, as the semantics described
in the support were differently understood within the furniture companies. Furthermore, a lack of
understanding of how to integrate the index was expressed by the furniture designers. Additionally,
an investigation of the survey results used in the Fuzzy AHP model revealed a lack of differentiation
between cost and energy. This ﬁnding highlighted further limitations in the tool’s ability to provide
accurate cost and energy indicators.
==PAGE:99==
Chapter 7
Conclusion
This thesis aims to assess how the economic and environmental performance of reverse logistics can
be measured to aid furniture companies in deciding to engage in reverse logistics activities. The study
follows the Design Research Methodology, which consists of four different design phases and has
been developed in collaboration with Stykka Aps as a case company by using participatory design to
integrate industry perspectives in the design process.
In the Research Clariﬁcation, it was found that reverse logistics can be an enabler of the circular econ-
omy in different manufacturing industries by providing an option for decoupling value creation from
material consumption. Reverse logistics provides the possibility for manufacturing companies to of-
fer new economic value-creation mechanisms through value recovery options. It was uncovered that
the integration maturity of reverse logistics varies between industries, where the furniture industry
is seen as having unexplored potential.
A variety of barriers can prohibit manufacturing companies from implementing reverse logistic ac-
tivities. These barriers stem from the context in which a company operates, such as the market and
government, and internally from the company, such as skills, organizational structure, and strategy.
The barriers were also found to be highly contextual and key barriers for reverse logistics therefore
depend on various aspects, such as industry, circular strategy, organization size, product design, and
geographic location.
Speciﬁcally within the furniture industry in Europe, the barriers were found to be a lack of infrastruc-
ture, product design, and customer behavior. Additionally, companies within the industry expressed
barriers related to a lack of ﬁnancial, human, and material resources to engage in reverse logistic ac-
tivities. Having outlined the barriers for reverse logistics it was then assessed how the economic and
environmental performance of reverse logistics can be measured through indicators.
It was found that to measure the performance of reverse logistics with indicators, the exact goal of
the reverse logistics initiative has to be deﬁned, as to ensure that the selected indicators are suitable
to measure that goal.
The overall thesis goal was then deﬁned to be ’Develop a support that assesses the factors inﬂuenc-
ing the environmental and economic performance of a reverse logistic system for furniture products
within Europe’, which guided the remaining research of the thesis. To understand which and how
critical factors inﬂuence the economic and environmental performance of reverse logistics a system-
atic literature review was conducted. The factors uncovered ranged from being within the control
of the company, such as product design and operations, to describing the supply chain collaboration
and capabilities, where a company has limited inﬂuence. Additionally, contextual factors beyond the
control of the company, such as legislation and infrastructure, were also found to be inﬂuential in
determining reverse logistic performance.
93
==PAGE:100==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 94
It was seen as necessary to narrow the scope of the support since the decision for a company to engage
in reverse logistic activities depends on both contextual barriers, the goal of the reverse logistic initia-
tive, the choice of performance measurement, and the critical factors inﬂuencing the performance.
The ﬁnal scope was narrowed to focus on product design factors and how they inﬂuence the cost and
energy usage of remanufacturing activities. The narrowing of the scope was primarily based on the
available industry insights through the case company.
To understand which supports are currently available in the literature, circular indicators with a prod-
uct focus were reviewed. This showed that tools have already been developed that can aid in reman-
ufacturing decisions for product design in the automotive industry, but circular indicators tailored
to furniture were not discovered. The literature also highlighted the importance of developing tools
together with the end user to incorporate valuable insight and ensure the tool is industry-speciﬁc. A
gap for a furniture tool developed with an industry partner was therefore identiﬁed.
To ensure the tool development was based on the needs and insights of the furniture industry, a
co-creation workshop was conducted with the case company to determine the input and output re-
quirements of the tool. This resulted in requirements focusing on using quantitative and qualitative
input to the tool, while the outputs should focus on highlighting remanufacturing improvements
through cost and energy measures.
A process was then conducted to select the most suitable product characteristic factors to be used in
the tool. A total of 21 characteristics were selected based on their relevance, data availability, and
usability.
Through 5 development iterations a support was co-developed with the case company, to ensure the
inclusion of their insights and needs as a representation of the furniture industry. The aim of the sup-
port was to aid furniture product designers’ decision-making capabilities in designing to decrease the
cost and energy usage of remanufacturing activities. The support is provided through an assessment
of how product characteristics inﬂuence the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing a product by
providing actionable outputs for improving the design.
The support consists of three activities. First, the user must input data on 10 quantitative product
characteristics and evaluate the product on 11 qualitative characteristics. This both makes the de-
signer aware of which characteristics can inﬂuence remanufacturing activities and also serves as data
collection support. Based on the input, the support assesses the characteristics and evaluates which
are key for a potential design change for remanufacturing. In the second activity, the user is provided
with an overview of the top three key characteristics to improve and an index evaluating the overall
product design concerning the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing. The last activity shows
a more detailed overview of all the key characteristics identiﬁed along with recommendations and
further design tools to work on.
The support was then tested with two different furniture companies to evaluate its usability and
usefulness. The test showed that although the support works as intended, there is a need for im-
provement in usability, especially related to understanding semantics describing product characteris-
tics. Companies vary in their use and understanding of different product characteristic terms, which
caused confusion, and were shown to cause erroneous inputs to the tool.
The test also revealed that the tool is useful for designers in providing recommendations and high-
lighting key characteristics inﬂuencing the cost and energy levels of remanufacturing activities. Feed-
back on future improvements included more details on material limitations and structural design
while also making the support speciﬁc to individual furniture types.
Reliability testing of the indexes was limited and not conclusive. Remanufacturing data from external
companies was not available, which made it difﬁcult to determine how the tool’s indexes related to
actual remanufacturing costs and energy usage performance. Therefore, the reliability check was
conducted solely using data from the case companies. In its current state, the support is best suited
==PAGE:101==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page 95
as an initial hotspot analysis guiding future design work in identifying how furniture products can
be improved for remanufacturing.
If properly applied and adapted by furniture companies, the support can help furniture designers
identify potential design changes to enhance remanufacturing performance. While user tests indi-
cated varying usability across the different types of furniture companies, the support has shown po-
tential in providing support to their transition to reverse logistics activities through product design.

==PAGE:115==
C
Requirement workshop
Objective
Was to identify and prioritize requirements for a support tool for the furniture industry that could
investigate the environmental and economic performance of products in relation to remanufacturing.
Participants
The following stakeholders from Stykka participated in the workshop:
• COO of Stykka
• Head of Circularity of Stykka
Agenda
• 9:00 - 9:15: Introduction and Objectives
• 9:15 - 10:00: Prioritization of Requirements
Set-up
The setup was centered around a boundary object, which was a Miro board created with and overview
of the pre-ﬁlled requirements. A scale provided, divided into "nice to have" and "need to have" cat-
egories. Participants were selected by dragging pre-ﬁlled post-it notes into the appropriate category,
as illustrated in the FigureC.1.
Figure C.1: Miroboard from requirement Workshop
Materials and Tools
• Miro (online collaborative whiteboard platform)
• Post-it notes (virtual, within Miro)
• Laptops
• Projector (for group view of Miro board)
Activities
• Introduction: A general intro to the concept of the workshop
• Understanding of Requirements: Given an understanding of the requirements ﬁlled out
==PAGE:116==
• Prioritization: Dragging requirements into Nice or Need to have boxes
• Relevant Users: Outlining the most relevant users of the tool.
Roles and Responsibilities
• Facilitator: Asbjørn, Magnus
• Participants: COO, Head of Circularity
Deliverables
A prioritized list of design requirements for the support to iterate from.
Outcomes
Input Requirements
• Users can complete tasks without assistance.
• The tool should be usable within 30 minutes to an hour.
• Applicable to all types of furniture.
• Clearly outline relevant product characteristics.
• Geographic sourcing of materials must be known.
• Ability to input data with ease.
• Support for 3D model automation.
Additional Features
• Include market price information for purchased products.
• Comparative economic inputs for better understanding.
• Input ﬁelds that offer extra precision.
• Consideration of transport modes.
• Knowledge integration from different company roles (Designer + Procurement).
• High conﬁgurability to meet speciﬁc company needs.
• Ability to insert and compare multiple product information.
Output Requirements
• Insights on economic and environmental effects of remanufacturing based on product design.
• Analysis of product design strengths and weaknesses.
• Actionable recommendations for product design improvements.
• Results must be easy to understand and interpret without assistance.
• Include potential for reselling products.
• Details on reuse at parts and component levels.
• Key metrics such as energy, water, and CO2 usage.
• Identiﬁcation of performance areas in product design and logistics.
• Insights on macro, meso, and micro levels.
• Compliance with European regulations.
==PAGE:117==
• Incentives to encourage product take-back.
• Feasibility analysis for product circularity.
• Considerations of secondary markets.
• Metrics for product performance within index 50-100.
• Comparative analysis of transport modes.
• Addressing issues with non-recyclable materials.
Additional Features
• Detailed environmental impact measurements (CO2 equivalent →17 impact categories).
• Relative output scores to highlight best practices in reverse logistics.
• Advice on optimal inspection systems for furniture.
• Scenario planning for user incentives.
• Mapping of high-impact product characteristics.
• Comparative results across multiple products.
• Integration of product, market, and regulatory information.
• Tools like CircularSEARCH for comprehensive analysis.
==PAGE:118==
D
Selection of characteristics
A process was followed to select the product characteristics that will be used in the development of
the support tool.
The process follows the one deﬁned by Kravchenko et al. (2020b), who has created a process for se-
lecting the right indicators for a CE initiative, which here have been adapted to be used to select the
right product characteristics to be used in the support tool that asses the remanufacturing of furniture
products.
The ﬁrst step described by Kravchenko et al. (2020b) is to select an initial indicator set. Here the indi-
cator list developed by Kravchenko et al. (2020a) is being used, who has created a tool to support the
indicator selection process, where an indicator list with over 270 indicators has been collected. In this
list is a category called ‘Product architecture’ which primarily describes different product character-
isticsKravchenko et al. (2020a).
Additionally, the article by James et al. (2021) will be used, who has made a tool based on prod-
uct characteristics of automobile systems to create an index of the manufacturability of the system.
Although an automobile system is a different product than furniture, there might be a lot of charac-
teristics that are relevant for both, which is why it has been included in the initial set of characteristics.
The initial set is split into two parts and can be seen in Table 3 and Table 4, which are the character-
istics identiﬁed by Kravchenko et al. (2020a) and James et al. (2021) respectively. The characteristic
name is in the ﬁrst column and a description of it is in the second.
The second step in the indicator selection is prioritizing the sustainability aspects(Kravchenko et al.,
2020b). These have already been selected to be the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing.
The third step is selecting the most suitable characteristics. Here Kravchenko et al. (2020b) highlights
several questions to guide the selection. The essence of these questions are relevance to the initiative
and company, the amount of data required, the uncertainty of data, resources required for collecting
data, how easy it is to use and understand the characteristics, and whether the use of experts is re-
quired (Kravchenko et al., 2020b).
Based on and in addition to these reﬂective questions by Kravchenko et al. (2020b), some other crite-
ria were deﬁned to help choose among the many possible characteristics:
• Criteria should preferably be available from the CAD ﬁle, BOM, or be knowledge easily obtain-
able or evaluated by a product designer of the product in question
• The criteria should relate to the product. It is not feasible to include characteristics that require
individual evaluation of each part of the product
• It should relate to the product design directly. E.g. ‘time required for disassembly’ is not directly
a product characteristic, but rather a consequence of the product design and characteristics.
• The characteristic should not require data that is only obtainable by already having a remanu-
facturing or other value recovery practices in place.
==PAGE:119==
• The characteristics selected should reﬂect unique aspects of the product design if multiple char-
acteristics exist that fully or partly refer to the same aspect, then a single characteristic evaluated
to be the most suitable should be prioritized
In column three of Table 3 and Table 4, it is indicated whether the characteristics are selected as
suitable or not along with a brief description of why.
Part 1 of the initial characteristics set consists of all the ’Product architecture’ indicators identiﬁed by
Kravchenko et al. (2020a) which are listed in Table 3.

The ﬁnal step of the characteristics selection is to customize and create new characteristics if some
are deemed to be missing from the initial set of indicatorsKravchenko et al. (2020b). In Table 5 the
overview of the selected characteristics from the two initial characteristics set in Table 3 and Table 4
can be seen, along with any comments indicating if and how they have been adapted.
Table 6 some added characteristics can be found, which were found during the systemic literature
review but have not been part of the initial characteristic sets. Here a completely new characteristic
has also been created based on input from the case company. Stykka expressed the need for an ad-
ditional characteristic that represented the similarity of the parts and fasteners in the product design,
which can cause time consumption in sorting and identifying the differences among similar parts and
fasteners that have different functions.
To describe this aspect in a product characteristic, the characteristic ‘Homogeneity of parts and fas-
teners’ was developed with the deﬁnition: Degree to which parts and fasteners are similar visually
but have different functions, this makes sorting and inspection complicated as parts can be mistaken
from each other.

==PAGE:127==
E
Clariﬁcation of support
Objective
To identify barriers within the the design process to enable design for remanufacturing. The main aim
was to gain insights into what areas the support tool should look into where in the design process it
would be relevant to do.
Participants
The following stakeholders from Stykka participated in the workshop:
• COO of Stykka
• Head of Design of Stykka
Agenda
• 10:00 - 10:15: Introduction and Objectives
• 10:15 - 10:45: Identiﬁcation of Barriers
• 10:45 - 11:00: Prioritization of Requirements
Set-up
The setup was centered around a boundary object, which was a ﬂow diagram that was used to iden-
tify the breakdowns within the design process in the furniture design process. The diagram was used
to facilitate discussion and identify critical points of focus for the support. The concept was for the
participants to speak from the model and use its structure to outline critical areas of interest. The
ﬁgure E.2 printed looked as such:
Figure E.2: Model used in the workshop on ﬁnding the issues
Materials and Tools
• Flow diagram (printed)
• Post-it notes
• Whiteboard and markers
• Laptops
Activities
• Introduction: Overview of the workshop’s purpose and objectives.
• Identiﬁcation of Barriers: Discussing and documenting the barriers to design change identiﬁed
in the ﬂow diagram.
Roles and Responsibilities
• Facilitator: Asbjørn, Magnus
• Participants: COO, Head of Design
Deliverables
Gaining an understanding of the breakdowns in the design process of furniture product design.
==PAGE:128==
Outcomes
The workshop revealed several critical insights and outcomes:
• Identiﬁcation of Key Barriers: The workshop identiﬁed the main barriers to implementing
design changes in the furniture industry, which include supply chain issues, lack of user incen-
tives, and the prevalence of linear product designs.
• Focus on Conceptualization: It was determined that the conceptualization phase of the design
process is the most relevant area to address for facilitating design changes toward remanufac-
turing. This phase offers the largest potential for integrating circular design principles.
• Knowledge Gaps in Product Characteristics: Both the Head of Design and COO highlighted
a signiﬁcant gap in understanding which product characteristics are crucial for circularity and
how to prioritize these characteristics in the design process.
• Importance of Data Insights: The COO expressed a strong interest in obtaining detailed data
insights on the product portfolio to assess its readiness for circularity. These insights would help
identify necessary interventions and provide key performance indicators (KPIs) for designers.
• Time Constraints and Quality Focus: Time is a critical factor in furniture design, and current
practices often prioritize manufacturability and economic feasibility over circularity. Enhancing
the quality of product data could lead to more informed and focused product development.
• Prioritized Design Requirements: The workshop resulted in a prioritized list of design require-
ments necessary for supporting design changes towards remanufacturing. These requirements
will inform the development of a support tool tailored to the needs of designers and product
managers.
==PAGE:129==
F
Characteristic relevance for the Remanufacturing activities
The determine which characteristics are relevant for the different remanufacturing activities, it was
decided to have three separate workshops. The workshop were centered around the initial assumed
relevance of different characteristics to the activities. This initial overview can be seen in Figure F.3.
The workshop then was conducted by explaining each of the characteristics as to how they are to
be deﬁned and their relation to potentially inﬂuencing remanufacturing. The participants then gave
their evaluation on whether they thought that the characteristics inﬂuenced the individual remanu-
facturing activities. This was continued one characteristic at a time.
The check mark indicates that the characteristics were considered to be relevant to the activity and
cost or energy usage of remanufacturing. The cross signiﬁes that the characteristic does not have a
signiﬁcant or no relevance to the activity and cost or energy usage of remanufacturing.
The yellow exclamation mark indicates that the workshop participant thought the characteristics
were relevant, but was not considered relevant in the initial model. The feedback from the differ-
ent workshops was then combined into a ﬁnal model.
If just one participant had found a characteristic to be relevant, then it was included in the ﬁnal model.
The resulting model from the workshop with the Fulﬁllment and logistics manager, Head of Circular,
and Head of Design can be found in FigureF.4, FigureF.5, and FigureF.6 respectively.

H
Introduction mail Surveys and results
This appendix presents the introductory email and the results of the three surveys conducted. The surveys
focused on the following aspects:
• Direct rating of the product characteristics in relation to energy and cost.
• Relative rating of the remanufacturing activities related to energy.
• Relative rating of the remanufacturing activities related to cost.
Introduction mail
Hello everyone
As you know, Asbjørn and I are writing a thesis on remanufacturing and creating a support tool to help de-
termine how feasible a furniture product is for being remanufactured, based on the product characteristics and
considering total cost of remanufacturing and energy consumption. We need your help in collecting some data,
as we want to understand which remanufacturing processes.
We ﬁrst ask you to evaluate which remanufacturing process has the biggest inﬂuence in determining the cost
and energy usage og the total remanufacturing. (Survey 1 and 2) We then would like to ask you to evaluate
which product characteristics and factors has the biggest inﬂuence in determining the energy usage and cost of
the remanufacturing processes. (Survey 3)
Survey 1 and 2 are rather quick with a pairwise comparison between the four different remanufacturing pro-
cesses we have identiﬁed.
Survey 3 is a bit longer and the evaluation of the different factors might be bit tedious. But we hope you take
your time to ﬁll out the survey, as the data is extremely important for our thesis.
Surveys introduction
This survey aims to understand the importance of product characteristics on energy consumption and cost in
relation to the remanufacturing of furniture. Remanufacturing is the process of bringing the product back to
index 100 (as new) condition through a series of 4 steps that are illustrated below. We invite you to share your
insights on how these factors inﬂuence the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing processes.
We have identiﬁed a number of factors and grouped them into the processes of remanufacturing. We would like
your evaluation of how important each factor is for the respective remanufacturing process in driving the cost
and energy usage of that speciﬁc process.
The 4 remanufacturing processes you will be asked to evualate the factors’ importance against are:
• Disassembly and Inspection: Taking the product apart to the level which is needed in order to inspect,
clean, and restore relevant parts.
• Cleaning: Cleaning the product in its assembled and/or disassembled state to make it able to enter and
be treated in the other processes.
• Restoring and Replacing: Parts are either being restored back to an as-new condition with necessary
processes or replaced. Every activity related to bringing parts back to a state that can be reassembled into
an as-new furniture product.
• Reassembly and Quality Check: Putting the components back together into an assembled furniture prod-
uct. Checking the product lives up to the standards for an as-new product.
How is cost and energy understood? For each factor and its relation to one of the 4 remanufacturing processes
you will be asked:
==PAGE:139==
1. You will ﬁrst be asked to evaluate the factor for its importance in inﬂuencing the cost of the speciﬁc process.
Consider cost as all the different activities in remanufacturing: Labor hours, machine hours, kWh hours
consumed, storage, tests, spare parts used.
2. You will then be asked to evaluate the same factor again for its importance in inﬂuencing the total energy
usage of the speciﬁc process. Consider here the direct energy consumed and used in the various reman-
ufacturing activities and processes. Also consider indirect energy coming from making spare parts and
other materials consumed and used in the remanufacturing processes.
This is excluding transport of products from user to company and storage used in company.
How will the survey be used? These inputs will be used to create a scoring of importance for the different prod-
uct characteristics in relation to the different steps in remanufacturing for furniture product.
Survey Results
The following is an overview of the survey results from the direct scoring of the product characteristics. The
survey was conducted in Google Forms.

Table 8: The gathered results from the survey looking into the product characteristics impact on
energy and cost
Cost Relative Importance Survey
Introduction
Introduction to Remanufacturing Cost Analysis Survey
Purpose of the survey: Welcome to our ’Remanufacturing cost analysis’ survey. The goal of this survey is to
gather insights into the relative importance of various remanufacturing processes in driving the total cost of
remanufacturing of furniture products. We only consider activities necessary for remanufacturing at the pro-
duction facility, which is why activities such as transportation are not included. Remanufacturing a product is
by us understood as: Returning a product to at least OEM performance speciﬁcation and quality with product
warranty as if it were sold as new, when entering a new lifecycle.
About the survey
In this survey, you will be asked to evaluate pairs of processes in how important they are in inﬂuencing the total
cost of remanufacturing furniture products. In our thesis, we have divided remanufacturing into the following
processes:
• Disassembly and Inspection: Taking the product apart to the level which is needed in order to inspect,
clean, and restore relevant parts.
• Cleaning: Cleaning the product in its assembled and/or disassembled state to make it able to enter and
be treated in the other processes.
• Restoring and Replacing: Parts are either being restored back to an as-new condition with necessary
processes or replaced. Every activity related to bringing parts back to a state that can be reassembled into
an as-new furniture product.
• Reassembly and Quality Check: Putting the components back together into an assembled furniture prod-
uct. Checking the product lives up to the standards for an as-new product.
Note that the processes are not always sequential, e.g., inspection might be possible only when the product has
been cleaned, as well as cleaning might be necessary both before and after restoring.
You will assess the relative importance of each pair of processes using a scale that ranges from 1 to 9, where:
==PAGE:146==
• 1 signiﬁes that both processes are of equal importance,
• 9 indicates that one process is extremely more important than the other,
• intermediate values signify varying degrees of importance from one process to another.
As a participant, you are asked to:
• Read each statement carefully: Each pair of processes will be presented sequentially. Consider the impact
of each process on the total cost.
• Select a score for each pair: Use the provided scale to indicate how much more important one process is
compared to the other.
• Provide consistent responses: Your choices should reﬂect a consistent judgment pattern, as they con-
tribute directly to our analytical models.
• Complete all comparisons: Each pairwise comparison must be completed for your responses to be valid.
You will be asked to evaluate the processes regarding their importance in affecting the total cost of remanufac-
turing. Consider cost as all the different activities in remanufacturing: labor hours, machine hours, kWh hours
consumed, storage, tests, spare parts used.
The survey should take 3-5 minutes maximum.
Survey Questions
1. How important is Cleaning and ’Restoring and Replacing’ processes in relation to each other for deter-
mining overall remanufacturing cost? (Seen in Figure H.7)
2. How important is ’Cleaning’ and ’Reassembly and Quality Check’ processes in relation to each other for
determining overall remanufacturing cost? (Seen in Figure H.8)
3. How important is ’Cleaning’ and ’Disassembly and Inspection’ processes in relation to each other for
determining overall remanufacturing cost? (Seen in Figure H.9)
4. How important is ’Restoring and Replacing’ and ’Reassembly and Quality Check’ processes in relation to
each other for determining overall remanufacturing cost? (Seen in Figure H.10)

to each other for determining overall remanufacturing cost? (Seen in Figure H.12)

Energy Relative Importance Survey
Introduction
Introduction to Remanufacturing Energy Usage Analysis Survey
Purpose of the survey: Welcome to our ’Remanufacturing energy analysis’ survey. The goal of this survey is to
gather insights into the relative importance of various remanufacturing processes in driving the overall energy
usage of remanufacturing of furniture products. We only consider activities necessary for remanufacturing at the
production facility, which is why activities such as transportation are not included. Remanufacturing a product
is by us understood as: Returning a product to at least OEM performance speciﬁcation and quality with product
warranty as if it were sold as new, when entering a new lifecycle.
About the survey
In this survey, you will be asked to evaluate pairs of processes in how important they are in inﬂuencing the
energy usage of remanufacturing furniture products. In our thesis, we have divided remanufacturing into the
following processes:
• Disassembly and Inspection: Taking the product apart to the level which is needed in order to inspect,
clean, and restore relevant parts.
• Cleaning: Cleaning the product in its assembled and/or disassembled state to make it able to enter and
be treated in the other processes.
• Restoring and Replacing: Parts are either being restored back to an as-new condition with necessary
processes or replaced. Every activity related to bringing parts back to a state that can be reassembled into
an as-new furniture product.
• Reassembly and Quality Check: Putting the components back together into an assembled furniture prod-
uct. Checking the product lives up to the standards for an as-new product.
Note that the processes are not always sequential, e.g., inspection might be possible only when the product has
been cleaned, as well as cleaning might be necessary both before and after restoring.
You will assess the relative importance of each pair of processes using a scale that ranges from 1 to 9, where:
• 1 signiﬁes that both processes are of equal importance,
• 9 indicates that one process is extremely more important than the other,
• intermediate values signify varying degrees of importance from one process to another.
As a participant, you are asked to:
• Read each statement carefully: Each pair of processes will be presented sequentially. Consider the impact
of each process on the energy usage.
• Select a score for each pair: Use the provided scale to indicate how much more important one process is
compared to the other.
• Provide consistent responses: Your choices should reﬂect a consistent judgment pattern, as they contribute
directly to our analytical models.
• Complete all comparisons: Each pairwise comparison must be completed for your responses to be valid.
You will be asked to evaluate the same processes again regarding their importance in affecting total energy usage
of remanufacturing. Consider here the direct energy consumed and used in the various remanufacturing activi-
ties and processes. Also consider indirect energy coming from making spare parts and other materials consumed
and used in the remanufacturing processes.
==PAGE:151==
Survey Questions
1. How important is Cleaning and ’Restoring and Replacing’ processes in relation to each other for deter-
mining overall remanufacturing energy usage? (Seen in Figure H.13)
2. How important is ’Cleaning’ and ’Reassembly and Quality Check’ processes in relation to each other for
determining overall remanufacturing energy usage? (Seen in Figure H.14)
3. How important is ’Cleaning’ and ’Disassembly and Inspection’ processes in relation to each other for
determining overall remanufacturing energy usage? (Seen in Figure H.15)
4. How important is ’Restoring and Replacing’ and ’Reassembly and Quality Check’ processes in relation to
each other for determining overall remanufacturing energy usage? (Seen in Figure H.16)
5. How important is ’Restoring and Replacing’ and ’Disassembly and Inspection’ processes in relation to
each other for determining overall remanufacturing energy usage? (Seen in Figure H.17)
6. How important is ’Disassembly and Inspection’ and ’Reassembly and Quality Check’ processes in relation
to each other for determining overall remanufacturing energy usage? (Seen in Figure H.18)
The survey should take between 3-5 minutes maximum.

I
Fuzzy AHP activity weight calculation
In this section, the calculation of weights based on a fuzzy pairwise comparison of criteria will be shown. It
follows the same procedure, method, and calculations that Kaganski et al. (2018) has used.
The ‘COO’ experts evaluation of the pairwise comparison of the importance of processes in relation to the overall
cost of remanufacturing will be used as an example of how the calculation for all pairwise weights has been
calculated for the remanufacturing activities.
The evaluation of the COO on cost can be seen in FigureI.19.
Figure I.19: COO experts pairwise comparison evaluation on the importance of the different reman-
ufacturing activities for cost
First, the values obtained from the experts are crisp values, that must be converted into fuzzy numbers to cal-
culate the weight of each process overall. In Table 9 there is an overview of the fuzzy linguistic terms and the
corresponding fuzzy and reciprocal fuzzy numbers(Kaganski et al., 2018).
Fuzzy logic works by translating a linguistic expression into a fuzzy number consisting of a lower bound, likely
value, and upper bound, which is why a fuzzy number consists of three numbers. The method works by includ-
ing the possibility for some uncertainty to be included in the evaluation through linguistic terms, where different
experts might have different perspectives as to how a linguistic expression translates into a crisp number Ishizaka
(2014).

Table 10: Pairwise comparison matrix
Step 1
The data collected in the survey is put into a pairwise comparison matrix. Here the pairwise comparison between
the same processes is always set to equal importance with a value of 1, which is why all the diagonal from the
top left to the bottom right are 1(Kaganski et al., 2018).
Since the expert has evaluated that ‘Disassembly and Inspection’ is moderately to strongly more important than
‘Cleaning’ as seen in FigureI.19, a 4 is put into the same row as D&I and in the C column in Table 10 (Kaganski
et al., 2018). Which is according to the translation of linguistic terms into a crisp number shown in Table 9.
Step 2
The reciprocal values of the inputted comparison are inputted into the opposite side of the matrix according
to Table 9 (Kaganski et al., 2018). E.g. D&I is evaluated to be 4 more important than Cleaning, meaning that
Cleaning is 1/4 as important as D&I as seen in Table 11.

Step 4

following formula(Kaganski et al., 2018):
