==PAGE:168==
M
MVP development feedback
Objective
To identify and prioritize requirements for a support tool that can investigate the environmental and economic
performance of furniture products in relation to reverse logistics.
Participants
The following stakeholders from Stykka participated in the workshop:
• CEO of Stykka
• COO of Stykka
• Head of Circularity of Stykka
Agenda
• 9:00 - 9:15: Introduction and Objectives
• 9:15 - 10:00: Prioritization of Requirements
Set-up
The setup was centered around a boundary object, which was a Miro board with listed requirements. A scale was
provided, divided into "nice to have" and "need to have" categories. Participants selected by dragging pre-ﬁlled
post-it notes into the appropriate category, as illustrated in Figure M.20 below.
Figure M.20: Set-up for MVP
Materials and Tools
• Miro (online collaborative whiteboard platform)
• Post-it notes (virtual, within Miro)
• Laptops
• Projector (for group view of Miro board)
==PAGE:169==
Activities
• Introduction: A general intro to the concept of the workshop
• Understanding of Requirements: Given an understanding of the requirements ﬁlled out
• Prioritization: Dragging requirements into Nice or Need to have boxes
• Relevant Users: Outlining the most relevant users of the tool.
Roles and Responsibilities
• Facilitator: Asbjørn
• Recorder: Magnus
• Participants: COO, Head of Circularity
Deliverables
A prioritized list of design requirements and a set of preliminary design concepts for the support tool.
Activites
To develop the MVP, the workshop was structured around the following activities:
• Input of Product Characteristics: This activity involved splitting the input into quantitative and qual-
itative product characteristics to provide an easier overview for the user. The emphasis was placed on
quantitative metrics to measure product design, as highlighted by the COO, CEO, and Head of Circularity
of Stykka.
• Generation of Index Results: This activity focused on generating results that provided an index for cost
and energy usage in relation to the product characteristics, paired with a spider diagram showing the
highest impacting product characteristics.
• Future Work and Recommendations: This activity provided recommendations based on product design,
along with links to other CE tools to improve relevant product characteristics.
Outcomes
Notes from the workshop and feedback sessions:
• Detailed Descriptions and Examples:
– CEO emphasized the need for more detailed descriptions and examples of each characteristic used
in the support tool.
– Importance: Explain why each factor is important and its relevance to remanufacturing.
• Iterate on the Spider Diagram:
– CEO and COO suggested iterating on the spider diagram.
– Goal: Make outputs more usable for designers, helping them understand which areas should be
changed.
• Material Durability:
– CEO and COO stressed the need to incorporate the material’s ability to enter new life cycles through
its durability aspects.
– Current version lacks this essential aspect for assessing long-term sustainability.
==PAGE:170==
N
Explainer for characteristics
The following is an overview of the explainers that were provided in the support of the quantitative and quali-
tative design characteristics.
Product Characteristic
Explainer
Parts Count
The total number of parts that make up the product.
Total Number of Fasteners
The total number of fasteners (screws, bolts, etc.) used in the product.
Amount of Tools Needed
The number of different tools required for disassembling and assem-
bling the product.
Size (Volume m2)
The overall size or volume of the product, measured in square meters.
Weight (kg)
The weight of the product in kilograms.
Amount of Different Mate-
rial Types
The number of different types of materials used in the product.
Possibility of Separation
The amount of parts connected to another part through an irreversible
joint.
Amount of Unique Parts
The number of parts that are unique and not interchangeable with other
parts in the product.
Amount of Unique Fasteners
The number of unique fasteners used in the product.
Main Material in Parts
The primary material used in the majority of the product’s parts.
Modularity
The degree to which the product is designed in modules, allowing for
easy removal and replacement of parts.
Fastener Accessibility
The ease with which fasteners can be accessed and manipulated during
disassembly and assembly.
Sequence of Disassembly
The complexity of the disassembly process, ranging from simple and
parallel to complex and sequential.
Hazardousness - Protective
Equipment Needed
The type and amount of protective equipment required to handle the
product safely.
Degree
of
Product
Cus-
tomization
The extent to which the product can be customized during manufactur-
ing or assembly.
Back-and-Forward Compat-
ibility
The compatibility of parts with previous and future versions of the prod-
uct.
Similarity in Appearance of
Parts and Joints with Differ-
ent Functions
The degree to which visually similar parts and joints have different func-
tions, which can complicate disassembly and reassembly.
Complexity of Design
The overall complexity of the product’s design, including the number of
components and their interconnections.
Inspectability
The ease with which the product and its parts can be inspected for dam-
age or wear.
Geometric
Complexity
of
Parts
The geometric complexity of the parts, ranging from simple shapes to
highly intricate designs.
Moveable Parts
The number of parts that allow for movement, such as hinges and slid-
ers.
Quality Testing
The extent of quality testing required to ensure the product meets stan-
dards.
Table 24: Explainers for all of the product characteristics within the Support Tool
==PAGE:171==
O
Interview Parvin Mallick: RL Tool Expert
Objective
To gain expert insights on the development of a support tool for reverse logistics (RL) by reviewing the tool and
learning from the experience of an expert in Circular Economy and Take-back Systems.
Participants
• Parvin Mallick, Ph.D. from DTU specializing in Circular Economy and Take-back Systems, with a focus
on the development of conﬁgurator tools.
Agenda
• Introduction and Objectives
• Showcase of the Current Tool Iteration
• Discussion and Feedback on Tool Characteristics and structure, this was the current iteration of the tool
O.21.
Figure O.21: The version of the tool viewed by Mallick
Preparation
The interviewer prepared by reviewing the current iteration of the tool and gathering background information
on Parvin Mallick’s work on take-back systems and conﬁgurator tools.
Materials and Tools
• Current iteration of the tool (digital format)
• Notes from previous industry interviews conducted by Mallick
• Recording device (if permission granted)
• Notepad or laptop for taking notes
==PAGE:172==
Activities
• Showcase of Tool: Present the current iteration of the tool to Parvin Mallick, highlighting key features and
intended user groups.
• Discussion: Engage in a semi-structured discussion to gather feedback on the tool’s characteristics and
structure.
• Insights from Experience: Discuss Mallick’s experience with building conﬁgurators, focusing on the de-
velopment of a playbook for terms and the importance of a structured user journey.
Roles and Responsibilities
• Interviewer: Magnus
• Notes: Asbjørn
• Interviewee (Parvin Mallick): Provides feedback and shares experiences related to the development of
conﬁgurator tools.
Deliverables
A summary of the interview highlighting key feedback and insights, to be integrated into the development
process of the support tool.
Outcomes
Notes from the interview with Parvin Mallick:
• Clear Explanation of Characteristics:
– Focus on clearly explaining the relevant characteristics identiﬁed to the user group.
– Importance: Users need to understand the relevance and importance of each characteristic.
• Creating a Playbook:
– Create a playbook for the terms involved.
– Function: Explain terms’ relation to take-back systems and their prioritization.
– Basis: Combine background knowledge with insights from prior industry interviews.
• Focus on User Journey:
– Emphasize the structure of the tool concerning the user journey.
– Insight: A stringent and easy-to-follow user journey should be outlined from the beginning.
==PAGE:173==
P
Prototype 1 Workshop
Objective
To gain insights into the usability and usefulness of Prototype 1, a workshop was initiated to gather feedback on
how the support aids the designer in evaluating product characteristics for remanufacturing. Additionally, the
workshop aimed to identify changes needed to improve the support’s usability and usefulness.
Participants
The following stakeholders from Stykka participated in the workshop:
• CEO of Stykka
• COO of Stykka
Agenda
• 10:00 - 10:45: Test with Support
• 10:45 - 11:00: Feedback on Support
Set-up
The workshop was set-up around the boundary object being the support tool. The idea was that the support
would support the open conversation, facilitated by the designers. The ability to use the support would give
better insight how the support would be used and help with giving concrete background for feedback. The
inputs for the support was an arbitrary chair furniture product chosen by the designers. A cutout from the
workshop is seen in Figure P.24
Figure P.22: Input Data Prototype 1
==PAGE:174==
H
Figure P.23: Results page in Prototype I
Figure P.24: Cutout from WS with CEO and COO
Materials and Tools
• Support tool prototype (Excel Format)
• Arbitrary chair for testing in the tool
• Laptops
• Miro (for collaborative feedback)
• Notepad or laptop for taking notes
Activities
• Tool Demonstration: Present the current iteration of the tool using an arbitrary chair to test its functional-
ity and gather initial impressions.
• Group Discussion: Facilitate a discussion to collect detailed feedback on the tool’s usability, ﬂow, and
recommendations.
• Feedback Gathering: Use Miro to collaboratively gather and organize feedback from participants.
Roles and Responsibilities
• Facilitator: Magnus
==PAGE:175==
• Recorder: Asbjørn
• Participants: CEO and COO of Stykka
Deliverables
A list of prioritized feedback and suggestions for improving the usability and usefulness of the support tool.
Outcomes
The feedback based on the workshop from the CEO and COO was found to be:
• Customization of weights and data ranges to tailor the tool to speciﬁc needs and furniture types.
• Split results for project leads and designers.
• Hotspot analysis to identify impactful characteristics.
• Comparability between furniture products in the portfolio.
Notes from WS
• COO: Importance of customizable weights and data ranges for ﬂexibility.
• COO: Need for an introduction page outlining tool’s purpose and usage.
• CEO and COO: Recommendations should be concise yet informative.
• CEO and COO: Split results for project leads and designers.
• Project leads: Quickly identify key performance indicators.
• Designers: Detailed results for focused efforts in the design phase.
• Hotspot analysis: Identify most impactful characteristics, cost, and energy usage index.
• Issue: Most impactful characteristics not highlighted; user must compare individual contributions.
• COO: Compare multiple products to provide context and identify well-designed products for remanufac-
turing.
• Beneﬁt: Enables designers to learn from successful designs.
==PAGE:176==

Prototype 2 ﬁnal evaluation
Objective
To test prototype II of the support tool with the CEO of Stykka, in order to gain insights on potential usefulness
and usability of the support. The goal was to understand which ﬁnal improvements the support needed in order
to be tested with external companies.
Participants
The following stakeholders from Stykka participated in the interview:
• Company: Stykka
• Role: CEO
Agenda
• 9:00 - 9:30: Tool Usage
• 9:30 - 9:50: Evaluation of Usability and Usefulness
Set-up
The set-up of the workshop was centered around the boundary object of prototype II. The concept was tested by
the CEO of Stykka and a arbitrary furniture product was chosen to test with. The tool had the following outlook
seen in Figure Q.25 and Q.26.
==PAGE:177==
Figure Q.25: Data input for Prototype II
Figure Q.26: Prototype II results
==PAGE:178==
Materials and Tools
• Microsoft Teams
• Laptops
• Pre-ﬁlled case study data
Activities
• Introduction: A general introduction to the concept of the interview and the objectives.
• Project Presentation: Reading out loud and explaining the introduction section of the tool, detailing what
the tool is about and what participants are required to do. Emphasis on the importance of honest feedback.
• Tool Usage: The user independently uses the tool, without active guidance from the instructors. Instruc-
tors answer questions and note down the nature of the questions to determine their signiﬁcance to the
usability of the tool.
• Evaluation of Usability and Usefulness: Open questions to evaluate usability and usefulness.
Roles and Responsibilities
• Facilitator: Asbjørn Søborg, Magnus Leick
Deliverables
A prioritized list of design requirements and a set of preliminary design concepts for the support tool based on
the feedback from the usability testing session.
Observation Metrics and Guidelines
• Record the number and types of errors made by the participants.
– Error type
– Severity (minor, moderate, severe)
– Amount
• Record the number and types of clarifying questions and where in the process they are asked.
– Type of clarifying question
– When
– Amount
• Record if some tasks in the tool usage are especially time-consuming.
– Time-consuming task
– Reason for time consumption
• Record user behavior and any signals of frustration, confusion, etc., while carrying out speciﬁc tasks in
the tool.
– User reaction
– Related task
Survey Questions about Usability and Usefulness
• Survey Goal and Interpretation:
– Success (score 4-5): The tool meets or exceeds desired requirements and expectations.
– Needs improvement (score 3): The tool works as intended, but has areas that need improvement.
– Needs redesign (score 1-2): The tool does not work as intended and needs redesign.
• Usability:
– How easy was it to understand the ﬂow of the tool?
– How easy was the tool to use?
==PAGE:179==
– How easy was it to understand the results of the tool?
• Usefulness:
– How relevant do you feel the product characteristics included in the model are?
– How useful do you ﬁnd the results and recommendations?
– How valuable do you ﬁnd the tool in evaluating the circularity of your product design?
• Reliability:
– How reliable do you think the tool is at identifying the key product characteristics that drive reman-
ufacture energy and cost?
• Open-Ended Questions (optional for sparking discussion):
– What did you like most about the tool?
– What did you ﬁnd most frustrating about the tool?
– Do you have any suggestions for improvement?
Outcomes
• Text and Comprehensibility:
– Cut down on text to make it easier to comprehend and get an overview.
– Too much text can lose the user’s interest.
– A lot of text in the input page can seem overwhelming.
• Customization and Validity:
– Simplify customization to reduce the risk of invalid answers.
– Simplify the term "homogeneity" for better understanding.
• Design and Evaluation:
– Clarify design complexity to avoid bias in self-evaluation.
– Inspectability may require more detailed inspection methods beyond visual inspection.
– Provide examples of movement for the movable parts characteristic.
• User Experience:
– Clarify whether a high score is good or bad and provide benchmarks.
– Highlight the three biggest recommendations.
– Ensure all text in recommendations is visible.
– Provide an ultra-short recommendation with the possibility of expanding for more information.
– Consider creating a report format instead of an Excel output.
– Add a slider to show the company’s current score and improvement goals.
• Visuals and Progress:
– Focus more on explanation graphics.
– Split the input page into two pages.
– Provide a sense of progress throughout the process.
• Practical Considerations:
– Consideration of ﬂatpack and stackability in terms of volume.
– How time-consuming would it be to ﬁll out the information? It would be valuable to get Malte to ﬁll
it out.

This is the mail used to send to them:
Dear XX,
We are master’s students in Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark, currently writing a thesis on
circularity in furniture design. Our project focuses on developing a support tool for furniture companies inter-
ested in adopting circular practices. This tool evaluates the suitability of a product design based on its qualitative
and quantitative characteristics, providing a cost and energy score along with recommendations for enhancing
product characteristics to better suit remanufacturing processes.
We are eager to test this tool with more companies and wondered if you might be interested in participating
by testing it with one of your products. This would involve providing information about the cost and energy
associated with producing a virgin product and the cost and energy usage of remanufacturing the same product.
Additional details about the chosen product and its characteristics would also be needed.
With necessary data ready, using the tool would take no more than an hour in an online session with us. In
exchange for your time and data insights, you will receive an overview of which product characteristics could
be advantageous for you to focus on, in order to enhance your remanufacturing efﬁciency.
Your participation would not only be a signiﬁcant academic contribution to our thesis but also help advance the
circular agenda within the furniture industry.
If you are interested in testing the tool with us, please let us know when you would be available for an hour in
the upcoming weeks. We will then provide you with a list of the information required for the session.
==PAGE:186==
Best regards,
Magnus and Asbjørn

Table 26: Energy calculation metrics used for the calculation of the Stykka MVP kitchen
Figure U.32: EPD Energy for Stykka MVP
The energy input for the calculations was based on the Environmental Product Declaration (EPD) for Stykka’s
MVP kitchen, focusing speciﬁcally on stages C1-C4. These stages are critical for understanding the energy con-
sumption associated with the end-of-life and remanufacturing processes.
Stages C1-C4 in the EPD represent the following:
- C1: Deconstruction/Demolition - This stage involves the energy used for disassembling the kitchen compo-
nents, which includes labor and machinery.
==PAGE:188==
- C2: Transport - This stage covers the energy used for transporting the deconstructed materials to the recycling
or remanufacturing facilities.
- C3: Waste Processing - This stage accounts for the energy used in processing the materials for recycling or
remanufacturing, which may include sorting, cleaning, and other preparatory steps.
- C4: Disposal - This stage involves the energy used for disposing of any waste materials that cannot be recycled
or remanufactured.

Table 27: Cost calculation metrics used for the calculation of the Stykka MVP kitchen, NDA is the

The cost calculation for the Stykka MVP kitchen involved several phases, each contributing to the overall cost.
Prices per part and per square meter for different phases, such as remanufacturing, inspection, disassembly,
cleaning, quality check, and the inclusion of 10% virgin materials, were provided by different companies and are
marked as NDA (Non-Disclosure Agreement) for conﬁdentiality.
The total cost is derived from aggregating these phase-speciﬁc costs. Additionally, time-based costs for inspec-
tion, cleaning, quality checks, and disassembly were calculated based on speciﬁed time durations and a standard
price per minute of work. This comprehensive approach ensures an accurate and detailed breakdown of the total
cost involved in the kitchen remanufacturing process.

The process of calculating the consistency of pairwise evaluations is described step by step by Kaganski et al.
(2018). A similar process will be used for calculating the consistency here. The ﬁrst step is to defuzzify the
numbers.
The method used for defuzzifying is the ‘Centre of area’, which formula is as follows according to Kaganski et al.
(2018):
l + m + u
3
An example of this calculation for the evaluation of D&I against C is as follows:
3 + 4 + 5
3
= 4

Now λmax can be calculated, which is the largest eigenvalue of the matrix, by the following formula(Kaganski
et al., 2018):
λmax = ∑n
i=1 Ei
n
Calculation of λmax:
4.69 + 4.15 + 4.48 + 4.64
4
= 4.49
With λmax, then the Consistency Index CI can be calculated with the following formula(Kaganski et al., 2018):
CI = λmax −n
n −1
CI calculation:
4.49 −4
4 −1
= 0.1633
==PAGE:191==
Having the CI, it is then possible to calculate the consistency ratio CR, to ﬁnally be able to determine whether
the expert evaluation is consistent. The formula is(Kaganski et al., 2018):
CR = CI
RI
Where RI is the random index. RI is variable depending on n, which in this case is 4 and therefore RI = 0.89.
RI has been determined by calculating the consistency in an evaluation with random evaluations.
The ﬁnal consistency calculation is:
0.1633
0.89
= 0.184
(11)
If CR < 0.10 then the expert evaluation is consistent. In this example CR > 0.10, in this case, the method sug-
gests revisiting the evaluation and redoing the evaluation until the evaluation is consistent (Kaganski et al., 2018).
Below in Table35 is the calculation of all the consistency calculations of the experts. It was possible to redo the
evaluation for three out of the four experts, which resulted in a more consistent evaluation of those 3 experts.
For this reason, it was decided to omit the evaluation of the Head of Design, which was the expert without
the possibility to redo the evaluation. The energy cost evaluation for the COO expert and the cost evaluation
of the Fulﬁlment & Logistics manager were also inconsistent in the second evaluation. It was decided to use
their evaluation regardless of the lack of a better alternative. In reality, they should have repeated the evaluation
another time, until their evaluation results were consistent, but it was not possible to do so.

Table 39: User Behavior Observations
Survey Questions about Usability and Usefulness
Survey goal and interpretation:
• Success (score 4-5): The tool meets or exceeds desired requirements and expectations
• Needs improvement (score 3): The tool works as intended, but has areas that need improvement
• Needs redesign (score 1-2): The tool does not work as intended and it needs redesign
Usability:
• How easy was it to understand the ﬂow of the tool?
• How easy was the tool to use?
• How easy was it to understand the results of the tool?
Usefulness:
• How relevant do you feel the product characteristics included in the model are?
• How useful do you ﬁnd the results and recommendations?
• How valuable do you ﬁnd the tool in evaluating the circularity of your product design?
Reliability:
• How reliable do you think the tool is at identifying the key product characteristics that drive remanufac-
ture energy and cost?
Open-Ended Questions (optional for sparking discussion):
• What did you as designer ﬁnd useful about the tool?
• Are there any design aspects/characteristics you found were lacking from the tool that would be relevant?
• Did you ﬁnd the recommendations actionable and useful for further work or how could this be improved?
• When would you use this design tool in your design process and for what purpose?
• What stakeholders would you engage/have to engage in order to use the tool?
• Would this tool aid you in making designs that could be focused towards remanufacturing of furniture
products?
• Would a tool such as this be helpful in making the decision to move the product portfolio into remanufac-
turing?
General Comments
• No issues with the beginning of the tool.
• Suggestions for different areas around the tool.
• Reference to building instructions for the chair.
• Active participation in building the chairs.
• Cross Chair selected for analysis.
• Identiﬁcation of main components of the product.
• Loose parts for fasteners and bushings identiﬁed.
• Recommendation to change descriptions around these areas.
==PAGE:195==
• General assembly time noted as a factor.
• Total amount of fasteners is 18 for this product.
• General confusion around the insertion of fasteners.
• Fasteners differ between assembly and disassembly; more tools are needed for assembly.
• Consideration of how the product is used when it comes bare in a box.
• Need to split the product for different areas.
• Prefer input for height, width, and length rather than total volume.
• Question on whether glue and other materials are included.
• Fasteners should not exclude glue and other materials.
• More types of materials need to be considered.
• Clariﬁcation on intended understanding of the materials.
• Additional set of wood components identiﬁed.
• Six parts set together.
• Clariﬁcation on how parts are considered.
• Amount of unique parts in the product identiﬁed as more than initially thought.
• Integrated way of Company A already in place for ﬁnding information.
Qualitative Aspects
• Modularity:
– Need to deﬁne modularity more clearly, possibly with examples like chairs.
– Better understanding when looking at different choices for modularity.
– Need to explore component levels and modularity more.
• Hazardousness:
– Clarify hazardousness in relation to remanufacturing and handling.
• Customization:
– Clarify customization, especially in terms of assembly by the customer.
– Address spelling mistakes and confusion around customization.
• Component Similarity:
– Similar components in the chair function the same but differ in product details.
• Inspectability:
– Organic products like Company A chairs need to be assessed for complexity.
Outcomes
• Tool Concept and Guidance:
– Concept for the tool of guidance makes sense for the area; it should relate to the product itself rather
than others.
– Data output should be fed around the product, making sense in relation to product categories and
areas.
– Break down into product categories and different areas for a better concept.
• Remanufacturing Potential:
– Explore possibilities inside the product area for remanufacturing.
– Learn to remanufacture the product around its concept.
• Material and Product Speciﬁcity:
– Make the tool more speciﬁc inside different materials and products used.
– Insert more areas around the product for detailed guidance.
==PAGE:196==
– Focus on speciﬁc concepts and principles for the product.
• Energy and Climate Impact:
– Consider the impact of energy and climate change on the product.
• Industry Characteristics:
– The furniture industry is conservative, with similarities across design and structure methods.
– Focus on similarities in design and structure within the industry.
– Composite materials are challenging to work with in this area.
• Material Deep Dive:
– Go deeper into the material department without making it too complex.
• Preparation and Introduction:
– Being prepared - clarify what data should be prepared beforehand in the introduction.
• User Perspective:
– Pasting parts based on the construction manual can cause pre-assembled parts to be pasted as one.
– Consider the difference in the number of tools needed for disassembly and reassembly.
– Clarify whether the perspective is from the user or producer.
– Make volume calculation from height, width, and depth rather than asking for the result.
• Error Handling:
– The error message worked perfectly.
• Material Considerations:
– Clarify ambiguity in material - is glue or surface treatment included?
– Consider the possibility of separation for glued wood boards making plywood.
• Modularity:
– Clarify modularity, particularly if it relates to assembly or manufacture.
– Different understandings of modularity and its meaning.
• Hazardous Materials:
– Clarify if hazardous materials are considered during remanufacturing or handling.
• Customization and Complexity:
– Clarify customization, especially in terms of assembly by the customer.
– Similarity was hard to determine between statements.
– Scores high in geometric complexity might not necessarily be considered complex.
• Product Categories and Structural Methods:
– Break it down into product categories: chairs, cabinets, couches, etc.
– Take a deeper dive into structural paradigms/methods in furniture manufacturing.
• Speciﬁc Conditions for Remanufacturability:
– Consider nuances and speciﬁc conditions for furniture, such as upholstery, surface treatment, oil
treatment, etc., and their impact on remanufacturability.

• User reaction: Seems to think it is hard to put both furniture and kitchen and other more permanently
mounted constructions in the house in the same category. A kitchen and toilet cabinet comes with the
building, typically, which means that different rules and regulations apply compared to a regular furniture
product, which is not bought as a part of the house
• Related task: During the results and general discussion of the tool and its applicability on different prod-
ucts.
• User reaction: They were surprised by the tool’s ability to highlight the two factors they recently have put
in focus as areas they need to be better on: number of parts and number of different materials
• Related task: During the ﬁnal discussion and sharing their thoughts on the results of the tool
• User reaction: They expressed that they have been investigating simplifying their product offering and
making their products less customizable; this seemed to make the connection for them between the relation
of customization and circular activities

contrast, a product designer usually is hyper-focused on only the product they are currently developing.

==PAGE:199==
Survey Questions about Usability and Usefulness
• Survey goal and interpretation:
– Success (score 4-5): The tool meets or exceeds desired requirements and expectations
– Needs improvement (score 3): The tool works as intended, but has areas that need improvement
– Needs redesign (score 1-2): The tool does not work as intended and it needs redesign
• Usability:
– How easy was it to understand the ﬂow of the tool?
– How easy was the tool to use?
– How easy was it to understand the results of the tool?
• Usefulness:
– How relevant do you feel the product characteristics included in the model are?
– How useful do you ﬁnd the results and recommendations?
– How valuable do you ﬁnd the tool in evaluating the circularity of your product design?
• Reliability:
– How reliable do you think the tool is at identifying the key product characteristics that drive reman-
ufacture energy and cost?
• Open-Ended Questions (optional for sparking discussion):
– What did you as designer ﬁnd useful about the tool?
– Are there any design aspects/characteristics you found were lacking from the tool that would be
relevant?
– Did you ﬁnd the recommendations actionable and useful for further work or how could this be
improved?
– When would you use this design tool in your design process and for what purpose?
– What stakeholders would you engage/have to engage in order to use the tool?
– Would this tool aid you in making designs that could be focused towards remanufacturing of furni-
ture products?
– Would a tool such as this be helpful in making the decision to move the product portfolio into re-
manufacturing?
Outcomes
Tool Concept and Guidance
• Clarify the speciﬁc purpose and stage of the design process the tool is intended to assist.
• Ensure the tool is focused on the product itself rather than on comparisons with others.
• Provide clear guidance on what the relative score means and how it can be used effectively.
Material and Product Speciﬁcity
• Make the tool more speciﬁc inside different materials and products used.
• Insert more areas around the product for detailed guidance.
• Focus on speciﬁc concepts and principles for the product.
User Experience and Evaluation
• Address confusion about the meaning and impact of customization.
• Ensure users understand the scoring system and its implications for their products.
• Provide clear benchmarks and examples to help users rate their products accurately.
• Clarify the role of similarity of parts and fasteners and how it should be evaluated.
==PAGE:200==
Master Thesis
DTU
Page xciii
Implementation and Practical Use
• Focus on how the tool can be integrated into the design phase effectively.
• Address how KPIs can be derived from the tool’s output and implemented in the design process.
• Ensure the tool provides actionable insights that are fact-based and relevant to the design phase.
Energy and Climate Impact
• Consider the impact of energy and climate change on the product.
Industry Characteristics
• Focus on similarities in design and structure within the conservative furniture industry.
• Address the challenges of working with composite materials in this area.
General Notes
• Ensure the tool is helpful in evaluating products accurately and objectively.
• Provide clear deﬁnitions and explanations for terms used in the tool to avoid confusion.
• Make sure the tool is perceived as useful by offering speciﬁc, relevant recommendations.
